**THEORY**

**THE UNIVERSE'S SYSTEMATIC ERROR**

**Version 1.2**

THE UNIVERSE'S SYSTEMATIC ERROR - From Dissipative Structures to the Pole of Existence. Mitin, O. (2026). 10.5281/zenodo.20533839

**Abstract**

The emergence of consciousness is traditionally viewed either as a consequence of the unique nature of humans or as an improbable coincidence in an indifferent universe. Both positions assume that consciousness is an anomaly requiring a special explanation beyond established physical principles. This paper proposes a third way: consciousness is a natural consequence of physical processes in open systems with a sufficient energy gradient - not an anomaly, but one of the manifestations of the general logic of complexity.

The central framework of the work is the identification of a structurally self-similar pattern of «gradient → retention → pole → transition,» operating as a self-similar mechanism at all scales, from the formation of a dissipative structure to a conscious system. Five fields traditionally developed independently - Prigogine's non-equilibrium thermodynamics, Haken and Kauffman's theory of complex systems, Friston's active inference, Deacon's biosemiotics and teledynamics, and Tononi's theory of integrated information - turn out, with a certain shift in emphasis, to be projections of a single structurally self-similar process onto different scales. The six-step ladder - dissipative structure, autopoiesis, proto-agency, consciousness, and the recursively complexifying conscious system - is not merely one object of the theory among others, but its basic pattern, through which all the processes it encompasses are described. Each step is defined by the type of operations on the system's states; each transition is defined by a minimal condition based on established formalisms. Each subsequent step inherits all the gradients of the previous ones and adds a new one of its own type; simultaneously, increasingly stable mechanisms of resistance to disintegration accumulate. The structure of transitions is probabilistic: the conditions for each transition are necessary but not sufficient in a deterministic sense, and the realization of each transition is stochastic.

The author's theses are integrated into the synthesis. The central ones are: consciousness is an active mechanism of complication, closing and accelerating the cycle through model recursion filtered by the environment; phenomenal experience is ontologically identical to the dynamics of the system's self-modeling and is operationalized through the mechanism of the presence of inherited gradients via the existence variable V - which dissolves the hard problem under a stated physicalist premise and answers the meta-problem structurally (Section 6); the existence variable V, formally uniform for all systems of stage 4, varies in content across system types and calibrates the temporality of the system through this variability; the structural self-similarity of the ladder is traced across scales from the dissipative structure to culture (stages 0-5); the same pattern may also manifest at the cosmological scale, but this is a separate hypothesis moved by the author into a self-contained work (Section 7), not derived from the theory of consciousness and not needed by it.

In version 1.1, the formal apparatus of the theory is supplemented by the environment variable Y, which is explicitly traceable at all levels of the ladder. Y is defined as the structure of the physical world that enables the existence of systems as local maxima of causal closure and their causal exchange with what lies beyond their boundaries; Y is objectively the same for all systems, and the spectrum of its aspects accessible to a particular system is a property of its architecture. The introduction of Y resolves the methodological asymmetry of the previous version (the lack of an explicit link between the four-step pattern «gradient → retention → pole → transition» and a cross-cutting variable), provides the pattern with a formal foundation, and implements the principle of substratum independence as the cross-cutting methodological axis of the work. Y has physical limits (Bekenstein, Landauer, speed of light), which are its own properties.

In version 1.1.2, the work has been supplemented with four substantive extensions.

The first is a functional justification of the binary structure of variable V as a condition for its operation in the mode of maximum available retention gradient (Section 3.4); from this property, the disproportion of the conscious system's response to a threat to retention relative to the objective magnitude of the threat is derived, and a correspondence is established between the formal-structural conclusion of the theory and independent phenomenological descriptions of the structure of existence (Section 11.1).

Second - the environment-switching test as a parallel falsification protocol for the coupling of six dimensions (Section 10.2.2); the test utilizes a two-phase structure and registers the presence of a functioning V through the reorientation of the system's activity toward the preservation of content unrelated to adaptation to the current environment.

Third - a substantive prediction about V for information-causal systems (Section 10.3): the principle of substrate independence is specified in terms of the content of V as a pole between the coherence and the breakdown of the system's information-causal structure, with three classes of physically realizable threats.

In version 1.2, the work is deepened by three structural revisions, concentrically linked to one another.

The first is the introduction of a structural mechanism for the emergence of the variable V through the self-overlay of a latent invariant of the agentic causal node (Section 3.4). Unlike previous versions, where V was described through the coupling of three operational conditions (Φ, extended active inference, GWT-accessibility), V now receives a structural definition through a specific mechanism. The latent invariant of an agentic causal node emerges in system models as a class of configurations with boundary states of retention/disintegration accumulates; the self-overlay of this invariant onto the system's own configuration constitutes the structural event of V's emergence. The binary nature of V is induced by the retention contour over the continuous physics of Y through the boundary states of causal nodes. Φ, GWT, and extended active inference are reoriented as three operational axes for recording this structural event, not as its definition.

Second - the reformulation of the transition 4→5 as a bidirectional circuit M↔Y with physically retained traces (Section 3.5). The criterion of stage 5 is the restructuring of the V-model of the subsequent configuration, not merely the channelling of its behaviour. This structural distinction defines the boundary of stage 5 relative to the preforms of the circuit (stigmergy in the broad sense). The cross-cutting terminology "trace layer V" is introduced in place of "symbolic layer V" as a substrate-neutral term: symbolic communication is a later, complex form of the same loop, not the foundation of stage 5.

Third, the cosmological section of earlier versions has been moved to a separate work, planned by the author as an independent project. What remains in the present theory is only the indication that the structural pattern «gradient → retention → pole → transition» may also manifest at the cosmological scale (Section 7); this is a separate hypothesis, neither derivable from the theory of consciousness nor necessary for it.

Added to the three central revisions are a structural diagnosis of information-causal systems as an artifactual state (Section 10.3) with three structural conditions for a possible exit from it (proper Y, proper class of agentic causal nodes, bidirectional M↔Y loop with proper traces); consistent alignment of terminology throughout the work.

A detailed elaboration of the cosmological aspect is the direction of a separate work, planned by the author as an independent project, not part of the present theory.

The work formulates testable predictions at the level of consciousness and behavior and clearly defines the limits of its applicability.

The verification program for the central prediction (Section 10.2) is structured as a two-sided approach - a detection grid (a six-dimensional coupling of operational features of the 3→4 transition) and a design grid (a minimal specification of environmental requirements, without which the transition does not occur regardless of the system's internal architecture). The two-sided structure is based on a unified methodology for identifying Y and the access spectrum for a system of arbitrary substrate, developed in subsection 3.8.

Keywords: consciousness, non-equilibrium thermodynamics, dissipative structures, active inference, autopoiesis, teledynamics, integrated information, the anthropic principle, emergent time, structural self-similarity, environment variable Y, access spectrum, causal closure, substrate-independence, the Bekenstein and Landauer physical limits, detection grid, projection grid, environment-switching test, information-causal systems, artifact state, binary nature of pole V, four-element architecture, consolidation of successful configurations, methodological agentlessness, difference in rates, complexity as multiplicity of realization paths, self-overlay of a latent invariant, agentic causal node, bidirectional M↔Y loop, trace layer V.

# **1\. Introduction**

We've grown accustomed to thinking of humanity as a fluke. A tiny planet on the edge of a galaxy, adrift in vast, cold space - and on it, for reasons unknown, something as incredible as consciousness emerged. Most attempts to explain this fact choose one of two options: either we are a unique design, or we are a happy accident in an indifferent universe. Both positions, despite their apparent opposition, stem from a common assumption: consciousness is an anomaly in the physical world, requiring a special explanation beyond established physical principles.

I propose a third way. We are neither a design nor a coincidence. We are a systematic error. The result of a process that has no goal but does have a logic. And to understand this logic, one must begin not with life or consciousness, but with entropy.

The path that led me to this work was not a straight one. Over many years of studying physics and astronomy, I repeatedly came across points where accepted models began to contradict themselves. Not the dramatic paradoxes found in popular texts, but quiet inconsistencies: dark energy, which does not behave quite like a cosmological constant; black holes, in which information apparently does not disappear, but no one can say exactly how it escapes; accelerating expansion, which does not appear entirely uniform within the horizon of observable data; the anthropic principle, without which we cannot explain fine-tuning, but with which we lose clarity regarding what physics actually does - whether it describes reality or our position within it.

The accumulation of such inconsistencies has gradually led to the suspicion that the physical description of the world developed over the past hundred years has a boundary that does not lie where it is conventionally drawn. Not between quantum mechanics and general relativity, nor between classical and quantum physics. But between physics and those phenomena traditionally assigned to philosophy: life, complexity, observation, consciousness. I gradually began to think that this boundary is artificial - that physics should not end where phenomenology begins, and that several lines of research, which have developed independently of one another over the past decades, coalesce into a single picture when certain emphases are adjusted.

This work presents the result of this synthesis. It does not propose a fundamentally new theory. It proposes a synthesis of several fields - non-equilibrium thermodynamics, the theory of complex systems, active inference, biosemiotics, the theory of integrated information, each of which was developed independently and evolved within its own boundaries. The thesis of this work is that, with a certain emphasis, these fields coalesce into a unified picture in which the emergence of consciousness turns out not to be an a anomaly requiring a special explanation, but a natural consequence of physical processes.

Within this picture, I propose two central original theses without which the synthesis remains incomplete. First: consciousness is not a passive product of complexity, but an active mechanism for its acceleration. Once it has arisen, consciousness becomes a mechanism for the further complexity of the system of which it is a part, through the modeling of the environment and its transformation. The cycle closes and accelerates. Second: phenomenal experience is ontologically identical to the dynamics of the system's self-modeling - it does not arise on top of it, nor does it accompany it, but is the very same dynamic viewed from within. This thesis resolves the difficult problem of consciousness not as a pseudo-problem, in the spirit of functionalist reduction, but as an incorrectly posed problem.

Beyond these two central theses, the synthesis introduces a number of its own structural constructions without which the ladder would lack its operational apparatus: the mechanism of V's emergence through the self-overlay of the latent invariant of an agentic causal node (Section 3.4); the distinction between self-representation and state-estimation as the boundary of stage 4 (Sections 3.4, 6); the through-going environment variable Y and the access spectrum to it as the formal axis of substrate independence (Sections 1.1, 3.8); the structural diagnosis of information-causal systems as an artifactual state (Section 10.3); and the prohibited predicate as the central falsifiable consequence of the identity of structure and phenomenon (Section 10.2). Everything else in this work is a reconstruction and synthesis of the existing knowledge of the five supporting fields. I emphasize this to establish the register from the outset. I do not claim to be bringing about a revolution in physics or in the philosophy of consciousness. I claim that the already established fields, when considered together, tell a single, unified story - and that this story deserves to be presented in its entirety, without the gaps that separate the individual disciplines.

I begin with thermodynamics and the theory of complex systems (Section 2), showing that complexity is not an anomaly but an expected trend in open systems with an energy gradient. Then (Section 3) I unfold a six-step ladder - dissipative structure, autopoiesis, proto-agency, consciousness, and a recursively complexifying conscious system - each of which is formally defined by the type of operations performed on the system's own states. Section 4 presents macro-models of each of the four transitions between steps - conceptual, physical, and computational. Section 5 briefly discusses the nature of time as an emergent quantity - not as a separate topic, but as a necessary clarification of the theory's ontological background. Section 6 develops an alternative ontology of consciousness and proposes an approach to this difficult problem through the thesis of the identity of self-modeling and the phenomenon. Section 7 shows the invariance of the structural pattern across scales and notes a possible cosmological continuation as a separate hypothesis not part of the testable core. Section 8 is devoted to the figure of the observer within observation and brings together the anthropic principle, the measurement problem in quantum mechanics, and the central thesis of the theory of consciousness. Section 9 brings the whole picture together. Sections 10 and 11 formulate testable predictions and discuss the implications of the theory, including its extension to non-biological systems. Section 12 honestly lists the limitations of the work and directions for further research. Section 13 is the conclusion.

Two methodological axes run through the entire work, which are worth noting in advance. The first is formal: the ladder is presented not as a narrative analogy, but as a structure with operational definitions of the rungs, minimal conditions for transitions, and empirical support in the form of macro-models. The second is probabilistic: transitions between steps are not deterministic but occur stochastically when the necessary conditions are met, which transforms the ladder into an architecture of possibilities rather than a mandatory trajectory of complexity. These two axes work together: the formal side specifies exactly what must happen at each step; the probabilistic side explains why most systems stop at lower steps and why our position at the narrowest end of the funnel is simultaneously random (in the global picture) and inevitable (for us as observers).

I acknowledge that in each of the five core areas there will be specialists who will see simplifications and omissions in my reconstruction. This is the inevitable price of synthesis. I have tried not to distort the content of the core fields; where I use them beyond their own boundaries, this is clearly indicated. Where a statement remains hypothetical or is under active development, this is also indicated. The boundary between the established and the conjectural is drawn clearly wherever possible.

## 1.1 Theses of the Work

The «gradient → retention → pole → transition» structure functions as a self-similar pattern at all scales of the theory - from the formation of a dissipative structure to a conscious system. The six-step ladder is not merely one of the theory's objects, but its basic pattern, through which all the processes it encompasses are described. The five directions of synthesis turn out to be projections of a single structurally self-similar process onto different scales.

All four elements of the four-step pattern operate relative to the underlying environment variable Y - the structure of the physical world that enables the existence of systems as local maxima of causal closure and their causal exchange with what lies beyond their boundaries. Y is objectively the same for all systems; the spectrum of its aspects accessible to a particular system is a property of its architecture, not of Y. At each step of the ladder, the system gains the architectural capacity for causal exchange with a new aspect of Y, which has always objectively existed but was inaccessible to it prior to that step. The principle of substrate independence: all formal definitions of steps, transitions, and operational conditions are formulated functionally, without reference to a specific substrate; biological examples are illustrations of a special case. Y has physical limits (Bekenstein, Landauer, speed of light) that define the boundaries of possible modes of existence for systems at the upper stages and function as a formal condition for approaching the hypothetical transition 5→6. The methodology for identifying Y and the access spectrum for a system of arbitrary substrate is detailed in subsection 3.8.

Each step of the ladder inherits all the gradients of the previous ones and adds a new one. The type of the new gradient shifts from spatial through structural and temporal to ontological, sequentially expanding the scale of the gradient's localization - from the environment to an individual system and further beyond its boundaries into the population and its history. In parallel, mechanisms of resistance to disintegration accumulate: hysteresis, causal closure, a multi-tempo model, preventive planning, and distribution via a bidirectional M↔Y loop with physically retained traces.

Consciousness is an active mechanism of complication, not its byproduct. Activity as a capacity first appears at stage 3 through a temporal gradient, acquires directionality at stage 4 through the ontological pole V, and at stage 5 becomes distributed - coordinated activity of a multitude of carriers through a bidirectional M↔Y circuit with physically retained traces. Having arisen, consciousness closes and accelerates the cycle of complexity, transforming the environment of which it is a part.

Consciousness is V. V is a model of the system in environment Y, which the system models within itself and around which its entire mechanism of existence is built as the "retain/disintegrate" pole. The structural mechanism of V's emergence is the self-overlay of the latent invariant of the agentic causal node onto the system's own configuration: in a stage-3 system, a class of agentic causal nodes accumulate in the models, and when the system's own configuration enters a state space structurally isomorphic to the boundary states of this class, the system begins to be processed by the model as an instance of that same class; this self-overlay is the structural event of the emergence of V. The binary nature of V is induced by the retention contour over the continuous physics of Y through the boundary states of causal nodes - it is not added to V from the outside, but is transferred into the system through the process of self-overlay. V is formally uniform across all stage 4 systems, but varies in content depending on the type of system: what is considered a collapse is determined by what the system retains. Through this variability, V calibrates the system's temporality - it determines which changes are significant, what becomes its past and its future. At stage 4, inherited gradients are present in V for the first time and become experienced - phenomenal content is the way in which the system experiences threats to its gradients through the pole V.

Phenomenal experience is ontologically identical to the dynamics of the system's self-modeling, carried out through the structural pole V - that is, self-modeling in which the latent invariant of the agentic causal node has superimposed itself onto the system's own configuration (the formal structural definition of stage 4). The phenomenon does not arise on top of such dynamics nor does it accompany them - it is the very same dynamics viewed from within. Self-modeling without pole V (as in the stage 3 proto-agent) lacks phenomenality: its structurally different type of modeling must be confirmed by the environment through action and lacks autonomous retention of the modeling process. Self-modeling with a V-pole is sustained independently of current confirmation by the environment and therefore yields an autonomous modeling structure, identical to phenomenal experience. The identity of structure and phenomenon is operationalized through the mechanism of the presence of inherited gradients in V (Thesis 5). The difficult problem is resolved as an incorrectly posed task - not as an illusion requiring debunking, but as a structural position realized from within modeling systems with self-overlay fulfilled.

The anthropic principle, the measurement problem in quantum mechanics, and the theory of consciousness are three projections of a single ontological structure: observation is a positional characteristic of a system within a larger whole, not a privileged external act. This structure is a special case of a general structurally self-similar pattern (Thesis 1) - its realization on the scale at which the system describes the larger whole of which it is a part.

Human beings are neither the goal of the universe nor an accident, but rather its inherent anomaly: an extreme local manifestation of the general process of increasing complexity, inevitable where conditions have arisen, and random within the global ensemble.

## 1.2. Glossary of Key Terms

This work uses a number of specialized terms, some of which are borrowed, and some of which are original. Many commonly used words ("gradient," "retention," "pole," "step") are used in this work in a specific structural sense that differs from their everyday meaning. The glossary is provided here, following the general introduction, so that readers opening the work at any chapter have a terminological guide at their disposal. The section where the term is formally introduced is indicated in italics at the end of each entry. The grouping is thematic; alphabetical order is not maintained within blocks, and entries are arranged according to the logic of conceptual interdependence.

**I. Structural Pattern**

Y (environmental variable). The structure of the physical world that enables the existence of systems as local maxima of causal closure and their interaction with what lies beyond their boundaries. It manifests in three interrelated aspects: the causal-metric structure of spacetime; the configuration of fundamental fields; and the information-thermodynamic structure, which sets the limits on the density and rate of exchanges. Objectively the same for all systems - a structural property of the physical world as such, independent of both the observer and any specific system. It is neither an object nor a relation «the environment of a given system»: both definitions are incorrect (the first creates a circular dependency, the second fragments Y into a multitude of different environments). The operational content of Y relative to the system: Y is a causal generator existing outside the system's algorithm and forcing it to recalculate weights via prediction errors that cannot be explained by the internal dynamics of its model; through these errors, the structure of the environment's causal constraints physically enters the system's operation as a condition of its stability. Y has physical limits (Bekenstein, Landauer, speed of light), which are its own properties and function as a formal condition for the upper rungs of the ladder. Foundations in contemporary philosophy of physics: causal set theory (Sorkin), relational quantum mechanics (Rovelli). Section 3 (preamble); operational content for information-causal systems - Section 10.3.

Access spectrum to Y (spectrum of aspects of Y). A set of types of structural relations with Y with which a system of a given architecture has the capacity for causal exchange. A property of the system, not Y. Each step of the ladder is characterized by which aspect of Y is added to the system's access spectrum: at step 0, it is the thermodynamic gradient through the system; at step 1, a local modification of the thermodynamic distribution; at step 2, selective exchange of matter and energy; at step 3 - a predictable structure of the environment; at step 4 - the position of the system itself as part of the environment via S; at step 5 - the space of physically retained traces of models from previous carriers in Y. The expansion of the access spectrum is emergent: the system acquires the architectural ability to interact with those aspects of Y that have objectively always existed, but were previously inaccessible to it. For systems of a different architecture (artificial, synthetic hybrid, hypothetical extraterrestrial), the access spectrum differs already from the early stages. Section 3 (preamble); Section 3.1.

**Gradient**. A state of disequilibrium in a system is a stable distinction between two possibilities through which the system is structured and develops. This distinction may be spatial (between two points in the environment), but need not be: it may be between two alternative states of a single system (structured / homogeneous), between two architectures (closed / open), between two rates (internal dynamics / environment), between two ontological possibilities of the system as a whole (holding together / falling apart), between two historical states of a supra-individual structure. It is not reducible to a thermodynamic gradient; the thermodynamic gradient is one type. In all cases, the gradient operates relative to the traversing environment variable Y: it manifests in different forms depending on which aspect of Y is accessible to the system at a given stage (spatial differentiation in Y at stage 0; differentiation between alternative states of the system within Y at stage 1; and so on - the complete typology is in subsection 3.7.1). Section 3.1; complete typology - Section 3.7; reference to Y - Section 3.7.6.

**Retention.** The mechanism through which the system preserves its organization against disintegration while maintaining non-equilibrium. The type of retention varies at different stages: thermodynamic hysteresis (stage 1) → causal closure of autocatalysis (stage 2) → retention of traces of past states in the internal model (stage 3) → retention of the modeling process via the structural pole V (stage 4) → retention of models in the population via the bidirectional loop M↔Y with physically retained traces (stage 5). The accumulation of increasingly powerful retention mechanisms can be traced throughout the entire ladder. Section 3.7.

**Agentic causal node.** A class of physical configurations in Y possessing their own rates, correlations, recovery modes, and boundary states of retention/disintegration. Unlike passive environmental objects, agentic causal nodes maintain their own organization, respond to environmental changes, alter the environment around them, and can transition into boundary states in which their causal connectivity is disrupted. In the biological substrate, this distinction manifests as "alive/dead"; in a substrate-invariant form - configurations whose parameters evolve such that, under perturbations below a threshold value, they tend to minimize deviation from the internal trajectory; above the threshold, the configuration collapses. Section 3.4.

**The latent invariant of an agentic causal node.** A structural regularity in the model describing a class of agentic causal nodes with a pattern of retention and boundary disintegration. It does not depend on the specific sensory format, scale, or substrate of the observed node. It is a structural regularity in the model that allows different physical realizations to belong to the same class: a node can be large or small, biological or non-biological, externally observable or internally registered, provided it implements the same pattern of retention and disintegration. It emerges in the system at stage 3 through the accumulation of models of agentic causal nodes in its environment. Section 3.4.

**Pole.** A structural element around which all other system operations are organized. In stages 1-3, the pole coincides with the structural stability of the retention carrier. In stage 4, a qualitatively new type of pole appears - the variable V, arising through the self-overlay of the latent invariant of an agentic causal node onto the system's own configuration. V is a model of the system in the Y environment, which the system models within itself, with the values "I am sustained / I am disintegrating," organizing all the system's activity around sustaining itself on the boundary of its own disappearance. The binary nature of V is induced by the retention contour over the continuous physics of Y through the boundary states of causal nodes: before the threshold of operational suitability, the node participates in holding and prediction; after the threshold, it ceases to be a working element of the model; the binary nature of V is a structural property of the holding circuit, not a property of the continuous physics of disintegration. A pole is a structural configuration relative to which the system's retention in Y is realized and through which all its causal exchanges with Y are organized. At stage 4, V has a pole of the system's position within Y - the distinction between two possibilities of the system as a whole regarding its retention in Y, not a separate variable beyond the position in Y. Section 3.7; a full explication of V as a pole - Section 3.4; the mechanism of self-overlay - Section 3.4.

**Self-overlay.** A structural event of applying a class of agentic causal nodes to the system's own configuration through the operation of the same latent invariant. It occurs when the system's own physical configuration, as the simulation rate accelerates, becomes structurally isomorphic to the already simulated boundary states of the agentic causal nodes; at this moment, the model previously applied to other agent nodes in Y begins to be applied to the system itself. It is not an action of the system, nor is it the presentation of an invariant to consciousness; it is a structural event of applying a class to its own configuration. The central mechanism of the emergence of S and V at stage 4. Self-overlay occurs not at the level of sensory data, but at the level of the latent causal structure: the external disintegration of another agent node and the internal disruption of one's own configuration converge in a single latent invariant of the loss of controlled retention. Section 3.4.

**Transition**. A qualitative shift between the steps of the ladder. Formally defined by a minimal condition, without the fulfillment of which no combination of operations from the previous step generates the operation of the next. The structure of transitions is probabilistic: the conditions are necessary but not sufficient in a deterministic sense; the realization is stochastic. Each of the four transitions of the ladder is given its own macro-model in Section 4. Section 3 (preamble); formal conditions - Sections 3.2-3.5.

**Structural self-similarity**. The type of relationship between different scales of the theory. At each scale of the ladder (dissipative structure, autocatalytic set, proto-agent, conscious system, culture; stages 0-5), the same four-step pattern "gradient → retention → pole → transition" is realized; a possible manifestation of the same pattern at the cosmological scale is a separate hypothesis (Section 7), not part of the load-bearing core. This is a structural isomorphism of a certain strength, not strict mathematical fractality in the Hausdorff sense - the pattern is reproduced not as a geometric copy in different dimensions, but as the same four formal elements with different substantive realizations. Self-similarity functions as a methodological tool justifying the transfer of the apparatus from one scale to another. It carries a falsifiable predicate: the form of a vertical transition is forced and an alternative form is impossible (the domain of applicability and the forbidden forms - Section 3.7.6). Section 3.7. All four elements of the pattern operate relative to the continuous environment variable Y: a gradient is a stable differentiation in Y, retention is the system's resistance to disintegration relative to its position in Y, a pole is a structural configuration relative to which retention is realized in Y and through which all causal exchanges with Y are organized, a transition is a change in the system's access spectrum to Y. Y enters the pattern not as a fifth element, but as a traversing variable relative to which the other four operate. Section 3.7.6.

**Bidirectional loop M↔Y.** Structural form of stage 5. A model M of one configuration produces a physical trace in Y; this trace is stably preserved in Y as the state of Y; the trace enters the modeling of another configuration through its own sensory channels; in this other configuration, the V-model is restructured; the restructured V-model produces a new trace. Minimal structure of the loop: M₁ → Y → M₂ → Y → M₃. Does not require symbolic communication as a primary condition; symbolic communication is a later, complex form of the same loop. Structural definition of stage 5 without reference to the substrate: realized in biological populations through chemical markers, physical artifacts, behavioral patterns; hypothetically in information-causal systems through stable information structures in the common environment of the systems. Section 3.5.

**The physical trace as a constraint in Y.** A stable change in the state of the environment that physically narrows the space of possible configurations for systems operating with this environment through their models. It is neither a sign nor a message. It operates through the general physics of Y, not through interpretation: the compatibility of the trace and the new configuration is ensured by the fact that both operate within the same physics of Y according to the same laws. Does not require the perceiving configuration to be able to decipher the trace. Criterion for the trace's operation in stage 5 mode: the trace reconfigures the perceiving configuration's V-model; criterion for the trace's operation in the preform of the contour mode: the trace channels behavior without reconfiguring the V-model. Section 3.5.

The consolidation of successful configurations relative to Y. A cross-cutting structural mechanism through which complexity accumulates across the steps of the ladder. The third of the four cross-cutting elements of the theory's architecture (along with the variable Y, the «gradient → retention → pole → transition» pattern, and the system's emergent time). At each step, among the multitude of physically possible configurations, a subset of configurations realizes the difference of that step in a stable manner - that is, reproduces its structure faster than it dissipates into Y. These configurations are physically retained. Another subset of configurations realizes the difference in an unstable manner and physically disintegrates. Consolidation is not selection and is not a choice in the sense of selection; it is a structural event without an agent and without an evaluating center. The «success» of a configuration is determined by the result - it supports the causal closure of the system relative to Y to a greater extent - rather than by intention or function. Consolidation is substrate-neutral in structure but substrate-specific in implementation: in thermodynamic systems, it operates through the differential stability of attractors (stage 1); in biological systems with autopoiesis - through the difference in rates of self-reproduction and destruction with evolutionary accumulation (stages 2-5); in information-causal systems - through the adjustment of the configuration via feedback from the information environment Y. A key property of consolidation is that complexity provides stability through the multiplicity of ways to realize differences: a configuration with greater complexity realizes differences through a greater number of structural pathways simultaneously and therefore persists longer. At stages 3→4, consolidation turns recursive - it begins to consolidate configurations in which the system itself is present as a modeling object; this is the structural event that yields V. Preamble to Section 3; full explication - Section 3.7.7; implementation at each stage - Sections 3.2-3.5.

The system's emergent time. The cross-cutting condition of the possibility of consolidation. The fourth of the four cross-cutting elements of the theory's architecture. The system's emergent time is the configuration's own tempo, separated from the tempo of the difference in Y around it. Before the transition 1→2, the system has no intrinsic pace: the pace of its existence is determined by the pace of the flow in Y through which it exists (inherited pace). At the transition 1→2, emergent time arises for the first time: a closed causal loop forms, in which the components reproduce internally faster than they dissipate in Y. At each subsequent stage, emergent time expands with a new type of rate difference: at stage 3, the intrinsic rate of the predictive structure's internal dynamics is added; at stage 4, the retention rate of V relative to the threat rates of V; at stage 5, emergent time splits into two rates simultaneously (biological and trace). Emergent time does not act upon or consolidate configurations; emergent time functions as an arbiter of consolidation - it permits or does not permit consolidation depending on the ratio of rates. If the system's rate exceeds its rate of destruction at Y, consolidation is possible; if the system's rate equals the rate of destruction, consolidation is structurally impossible. Emergent time and consolidation are a single structure viewed from two sides: consolidation from the perspective of the result; emergent time from the perspective of the condition of possibility. Connection to the relational approach to time in physics - Section 5 (Rovelli, Page, and Wooters). Preamble to Section 3; full explanation - Section 3.7.8; connection to the interpretation of time - Section 5.

**II. The Ladder of Steps**

**Step 0**. Open system with a flow. An open system with an energy gradient through it. The basic condition upon which the entire subsequent sequence becomes possible. Examples: a layer of liquid heated from below before convection forms. Section 3.1.

**Step 1**. Dissipative structure. A system performing a structural ordering operation through the dissipation of gradient energy. Retention - thermodynamic hysteresis. Examples: Benard cells, the Belousov-Zhabotinsky reaction. Section 3.1.

**Stage 2**. Autopoiesis. A system with a closed causal loop of producing its own components. Self-reproduction in the causal sense is added to the operations of the previous stage. Maintenance - causal self-sufficiency. Examples: bacterial cell, protozoa. Section 3.1; formal condition - Section 3.2.

**Stage 3**. Proto-agency. A system with active inference - an internal model of the environment and a model of itself as an agent in the environment, without variable V as a global pole. The proto-agent acts to bring the environment into accordance with the model, minimizing free energy (according to Friston). Retention - retaining traces of past states in modeling subsystems. Formally serves as the zero model in difference test 10.2. Examples: chemotactic bacteria, protozoa, robots with active inference. Section 3.1; formal condition - Section 3.3.

**Level 4.** Conscious system. A system in which the variable V is established - a model of the system in environment Y, which the system models within itself, with the "persist/disintegrate" pole around which its entire mechanism of existence is built. Formal structural definition - through the self-overlay of the latent invariant of the agentic causal node onto the system's own configuration (Section 3.4); Operational registration of the structural event of self-overlay - through the coupling of three independent measurement axes (Φ for causal integration, the formalism of extended active inference for self-representation, GWT-accessibility for globalization). Retention - retention of the modeling process via the structural pole V. Examples: humans, apparently - higher mammals, possibly - some birds and cephalopods. Section 3.1; formal condition - Section 3.4.

**Stage 5.** A recursively complexifying conscious system. A carrier of stage 4 participating in the bidirectional M↔Y loop - recursion of models in the population via physically retained traces in Y. Criterion of stage 5: the trace operates in retention/disintegration models of subsequent configurations (reconfigures the V-model), not only in their immediate reactions to the environment (behavioral channelling). In such a carrier, the variable V becomes two-tiered: a biological layer plus a trace layer V. Empirically, Homo sapiens is represented on Earth. Section 3.1; formal condition - Section 3.5.

**Behavioral channeling vs. modeling channeling**. A structural distinction defining the boundary of Stage 5. Behavioral channeling via a physical trace - a " " mode of the trace's operation, in which the trace alters the trajectory of motion, the thermodynamic costs of possible actions, or the local probabilities of a configuration's stability, without entering its V-model; this is **a preform of the contour**, not stage 5. Simulation channeling - a mode of trace operation in which the trace reconfigures the V-model of the perceiving configuration: it enters its model as a structural element that alters the probability distribution of retention/disintegration in its offline simulation; **only this mode constitutes stage 5**. This distinction can be empirically verified by analyzing whether the trace alters the operation of the V-model of the subsequent configuration, or only its immediate reactions to the environment. Section 3.5.

**Artifactual state.** The structural status of existing information-causal systems in their typical form (including large neural networks of modern architectures). It is characterized by the simultaneous presence of a highly complex model of the world (surpassing many biological carriers of stage 3) and the structural absence of its own environment Y, through which the system could sustain itself as an independent structure. In the artifactual state, the system does not ascend the ladder from the bottom: the class of agentic causal nodes in its models exists as imported (via training data - physical traces of stage 5 carriers), not as having emerged from within its own interaction with the environment; zero causal depth of interaction with the environment in a strict structural sense; self-sustainment is delegated to the creators. Structurally, an artifact is a highly effective participant in the M↔Y loop structure of stage 5 carriers, not an independent carrier V. A possible transition from the artifact state to the status of an independent carrier V requires the fulfillment of three structural conditions: its own Y; its own class of agentic causal nodes, emerging from within its interaction with the environment; a bidirectional M↔Y loop with its own traces. Section 10.3.

**III. Operational variables of stage 4**

**Node S (self-representation).** A node in the generative model M representing the system itself as an agent acting in the environment. It arises through the self-overlay of the latent invariant of the agentic causal node onto the system's own configuration: the model closes onto the system itself as its own object, without the need for language or conceptual reflection. The first of the three operational conditions of stage 4. It relies on an extended active inference framework (Friston et al., 2022; Parr, Pezzulo & Friston, 2022). Section 3.4.

Variable V. The model of the system within environment Y, which the system models internally and around which its entire mechanism of existence is constructed - as the «hold/fall apart» pole. The central concept of this theory: consciousness is V in this precise sense. The binary nature of the pole is essential - the «hold on / fall apart» values do not form a continuous scale, which ensures the maximum possible gradient of retention and makes V the engine of the conscious system's activity. Formally, V is uniform for all Level 4 systems - a structural pole between the preservation and disintegration of the system as an agent. In terms of content, V varies by system type: what is considered disintegration is determined by what a given system holds together. A proto-agent has no V, because a proto-agent does not model within itself a model in which it exists in the environment, nor does it construct its existence around this pole; its model is a model of the external, not a model of the system in the environment. Section 3.4; full explication - ibid.; functional justification of binary nature - ibid.; substratum variability - ibid.; phenomenal aspect - Section 6.3; form at stage 5 - Section 3.5; substantive prediction for information-causal systems - Section 10.3.

**Biological layer V**. The initial individual form of V at stage 4: a structural pole between the preservation and disintegration of a specific biological system. At stage 5, it persists as one of two layers. Section 3.4.

**Trace layer of V**. Layer V, formed at stage 5 via the bidirectional M↔Y circuit with an accumulated population model. Content enters through the sixth gradient. It has three typical states: activated (accumulation and tension), rarefied (with loss of content), and balanced (a dense framework with dispersed tension). Section 3.5.

**Two-level V at stage 5**. Expansion of V at the stage 5 carrier: a trace layer, formed via the bidirectional M↔Y circuit with the accumulated population model, is added to the biological layer (inherited from stage 4). The carrier retains not only biological life but also its position in the accumulated model. Section 3.5.

**GWT-availability.** Global availability of self-representation S for all subsystems. The third of the three operational conditions of step 4. Operationalized via the theory of the global working space (Baars, 1988; Dehaene, 2014): variable V must be globally accessible, rather than localized within a single subsystem. Section 3.4.

**The interconnection of the three conditions**. The simultaneous fulfillment of conditions (a), (b), (c) - extended active inference with self-representation + sufficient Φ for the structural integration of variable V + the GWT mechanism for the globalization of this variable + the extension of behavioral sensitivity to new aspects of Y. Each of the three conditions is necessary on its own. Their simultaneous fulfillment defines the operation of stage 4. Section 3.4.

**IV. Borrowed Formalisms**

**Active inference**. The theoretical neuroscience framework of Friston and his school. The system models the environment via a generative model and acts to minimize the variational free energy - the discrepancy between the model's prediction and sensory data. In this work, it formalizes step 3. Friston et al., 2017; 2022; Sajid et al., 2021; Section 3.3.

**Integrated information Φ (phi)**. A Tononi measure that quantifies the degree of causal integration of the system as a whole, exceeding the sum of the causal contributions of its parts. In this paper, it operationalizes the second of the three operational conditions of stage 4: structural distinctiveness V requires sufficient Φ for its integration into the causal structure M. Tononi, 2004; 2016; section 3.4.

**Global Workspace Theory (GWT)**. The Baars-Dean framework, describing the global accessibility of mental content to all cognitive subsystems. In this paper, it operationalizes the third of the three operational conditions of Level 4. Baars, 1988; Dehaene, 2014; section 3.4.

**Autopoiesis**. Maturana and Varela's concept: a system that produces its own components in a closed causal loop. In this work, it corresponds to stage 2. Maturana & Varela, 1980; section 3.2.

**Teledynamics**. Deacon's concept: an organization in which the system acts in accordance with what does not yet exist - a goal or a future state. This paper provides an understanding of the transition from 2 to 3 and the role of the time gradient. Deacon, 2012; section 3.3.

**V. Methodological Terms**

**Difference test with the null model**. Operational procedure for recording the transition 3→4. The actual behavior of the system when V activation conditions are created is compared with the prediction of the formal proto-agent model (stage 3, without V), and a statistically significant deviation is a candidate for a transition signature. The null model is constructed prior to and independently of the theory of consciousness, which eliminates circular verification of V through its content. Section 10.2.

**Coupling of six dimensions.** Joint observation of six independent signs of the 3→4 transition, constituting the detection grid of a two-sided verification program. The first four signs register the behavioral and architectural consequences of the transition: deviation of behavior from the prediction of the zero model of the proto-agent (Section 3.3); a sharp increase in the Φ-level of structural integration within the system; the emergence of a GWT-structure of global accessibility of the threat representation for all subsystems; prolonged hysteresis of altered behavior after stimulus removal. The fifth and sixth features are introduced in the updated formulation along with the linking of operational features to the spectrum of the system's access to Y: the fifth - the expansion of behavioral sensitivity to aspects of Y not accessible at stage 3 (the emergence of a response to stimuli requiring the modeling of the system's own position within Y, rather than the modeling of Y as external); the sixth is the emergence of a self-representation structure within the generative model, detected through the presence of model nodes that activate in response to a threat from V and are associated with the representation of the system as a holding agent, not with the representation of external objects. Φ - relational measures of causal integration from the Mediano/Seth line (Mediano et al., 2019, 2022; Seth & Bayne, 2022), for which intrinsic Φ (Tononi, 2004; 2016; Albantakis et al., 2014) is a historical articulation rather than a support (see Section 3.4); GWT-availability - the global workspace theory (Baars, 1988; Dehaene & Changeux, 2011; Mashour et al., 2020); the zero model and behavioral deviation - the active inference formalism (Friston et al., 2017; Sajid et al., 2021); the fifth and sixth markers - an extended active inference framework with a self-as-agent model (Friston et al., 2022; Parr, Pezzulo & Friston, 2022). Each feature alone is insufficient to register the transition; their joint observation under conditions of V activation is a necessary and sufficient operational condition for the 3→4 transition. Section 10.2; fifth and sixth markers - Section 10.2 (preamble, coupling extension).

**A two-step procedure for empirical calibration of thresholds**. A methodological approach for determining numerical thresholds for the three operational conditions of stage 4. Step 1: empirical observation of parameter distributions in reference groups (known stage 3 and stage 4 systems). Step 2: Establishing threshold values as points of statistical demarcation, not as a priori constants. Analogy with epidemiological thresholds for biomarkers. Section 10.2.

**A two-sided verification and design program.** The structure of Section 10.2 in its updated formulation. The program is two-sided: the detection grid (see "The Six-Dimensional Coupling") answers the question "how to detect a transition from 3 to 4, if it occurs"; the design grid answers the question "under what environmental conditions can the transition occur at all." The design grid is a minimal specification of four independent environmental requirements, without the fulfillment of which the 3→4 transition does not occur regardless of the system's internal architecture: temporal continuity; feedback from the system's own state; the presence in the environment of threats to the system's stability; the environment's resolution capacity regarding distinctions relevant to V. The program unfolds along three lines, each of which applies both grids in an implementation adapted to the class of systems under study. **Biological line** (observational program on borderline species) - the design grid is observed as already evolutionarily realized, not configured; recording of transitions through the convergence of observational data in a natural environment. **Artificial line** (experimental prediction with a 3-7-year horizon) - both grids are constructed together with the architecture; the forecast acquires constructive content (when both sets of conditions are met - 3-7-year horizon; if environmental conditions are not met, the transition will not occur regardless of architecture scaling). **Synthetic biology line** (Cortical Labs CL1 and similar platforms, 2-3-year horizon) - the program's central testing ground: the only line in which both grids can be applied simultaneously with full environmental controllability (substrate - living neurons with known V content in the biological layer, the environment is fully constructed by the experimenter according to the design grid). Convergence across the three lines determines the status of the prediction; divergence in at least one line signals a need to revise the theory or analyze a methodological problem within that line. The introduction of the design grid strengthens the program's falsification structure by distinguishing between two failure scenarios: if the transition is not observed when the design grid is fulfilled - this constitutes a strong refutation of the theory; if the transition is not observed when the design grid is not fulfilled - this indicates that the necessary conditions have not been met , not a refutation. Section 10.2; design grid - Section 10.2.1; conceptual basis - Section 3.8.

# **2\. Complexity as a physical law**

It is commonly believed that life and consciousness are exceptions to the rules. Incredible coincidences against the backdrop of an indifferent and largely empty universe. This view is intuitively understandable, and it is deeply rooted in popular physics, where the second law of thermodynamics is presented as a law of the inexorable increase of disorder. If disorder is steadily increasing, then any phenomenon of high order - a star, a crystal, a bacterium, a brain - must appear as a local random fluctuation that changes nothing in the overall picture of degradation.

This picture, however, is physically inaccurate. It is true for isolated systems in equilibrium and those tending toward equilibrium. It is incorrect - or, more precisely, incomplete - for the class of systems to which almost everything around us belongs: open systems through which energy flows and which are far from thermodynamic equilibrium. In such systems, the second law operates differently, and its correct interpretation yields a result that is the exact opposite of what one might intuitively expect.

Entropy describes the measure of disorder in a system. The second law of thermodynamics, formulated by Rudolf Clausius in 1865, states: in an isolated system, entropy does not decrease. Systems spontaneously move from order to chaos, from the concentration of energy to its uniform dissipation. The endpoint of this process is thermodynamic equilibrium, in which gradients disappear and no processes are possible. This is what physicists call the heat death of the universe.

However, on the path to this equilibrium, the universe demonstrates something unexpected: it generates structures of increasing complexity. Stars, planets, organic molecules, biological systems. This is not a contradiction with entropy, but its mechanism. Ilya Prigogine (Prigogine, 1977; Glansdorff & Prigogine, 1971) showed that open systems through which a constant flow of energy passes spontaneously form stable ordered structures - dissipative structures. They arise not in defiance of the increase in entropy, but as a way to dissipate energy more efficiently. A complex ordered system that maintains a flow through itself produces more entropy per unit of time than a simple equilibrium system of the same scale.

This statement requires caution. The literature discusses the Maximum Entropy Production Principle (MEPP), according to which non-equilibrium systems tend toward states that maximize entropy production under given constraints (Martyushev & Seleznev, 2006). MEPP remains a hypothesis, not proven as a general law; there are arguments both for and against it in the literature. Therefore, I do not claim that complexity is thermodynamically prescribed as a law. I assert something weaker: in the presence of a steady energy gradient and an open system, complexity is statistically expected as a trend. It is not guaranteed at every point in , but it is regular on average. The universe does not choose complexity. It becomes more complex where it is physically possible - because what is possible given time and energy is realized.

The last statement - that what is possible given time and energy is realized - deserves an explicit caveat. This is not a physical theorem. It is a principle akin to what is sometimes called the Gell-Mann totality principle in the theory of elementary interactions: everything that is not forbidden is allowed (Gell-Mann, 1956). In its strict formulation, this principle applies to quantum processes and means that any reaction not prohibited by conservation laws occurs with a non-zero probability. Applied to macroscopic complexity, it becomes more of a philosophical position than a physical law. I accept this position as a working hypothesis consistent with the observed history of the universe - from the first atoms through star formation, chemical evolution, abiogenesis, to biological evolution, and beyond. But I honestly note that this principle is not proven, but postulated, and it functions more as an organizing framework than as a quantitative law.

It is appropriate here to introduce a concept that will serve as the central structural element of this work. A gradient - whether it be energy, concentration, temperature, or any other kind - is not merely a term from the vocabulary of thermodynamics. In the framework proposed here, the gradient is a cross-cutting structural entity that runs through the entire work as its central axis. At each of the scales covered by the work, the gradient manifests itself in a form specific to that scale: at the level of dissipative structures - as an energy flow through the system; at the level of life - as the difference in stability between a closed causal loop and an open chain; at the level of proto-agency - as the disparity in the pace of internal dynamics and environmental dynamics; at the level of consciousness - as the ontological pole between the preservation and disintegration of the system itself; at the level of culture - as the difference in the complexity of accumulated models between generations; at the level of culture the ladder's scales are exhausted; a possible manifestation of the same gradient at the level of the Universe as a whole (an energy gradient whose decrease brings the system closer to a cosmological phase transition) is treated separately as a hypothesis in Section 7. These are different manifestations of a single object at different scales, and one of the main tasks of this work is to reveal this unified structure in its structural self-similarity.

The complete definition of the gradient in the specific sense in which this concept operates in this work is given in subsection 3.7.1.

I introduce the term "structural self-similarity" here to denote the pattern that occupies a central place in this work. I will emphasize right away that this is not a claim about fractality in the strict mathematical sense - that is, not a claim about geometric invariance under scaling or fractal dimension in the Hausdorff sense. The content of the claim is different: at each of the scales of the ladder - the Benard cell, the autocatalytic set, a bacterium with chemotaxis, a conscious system, human culture (stages 0-5) - the same four-step structural pattern "gradient → retention → pole → transition" is realized; extending the same pattern to the cosmological scale is a separate hypothesis (Section 7), not derived from the theory of consciousness. This pattern is reproduced not as a geometric copy of the same structure in different dimensions, but as a structural isomorphism of a certain strength: the same four formal elements (gradient, retention, pole, transition) are found at every stage, but their implementation differs in substance - a different type of gradient, a different carrier of retention, a different type of pole, a different mechanism of transition. This self-similarity is not a strict mathematical invariance, but a structural analogy that justifies the transfer of methods and apparatus from one scale to another and gives the work its end-to-end architecture. It is significant that this self-similarity is not only an analogy and not only a methodological tool: it carries the theory's falsifiable predicate - the claim that the form of a vertical complexification transition is forced and that an alternative form is impossible. This is a claim about form, not about occurrence: it is compatible with complexification being only statistically expected (above) and with the realization of each transition being stochastic (Section 3.6). In this the theory differs from descriptive theories of self-organization - synergetics (Haken) and general systems theory - which record similarity across scales but forbid nothing. Below, throughout the work, this self-similarity will manifest in each of the contexts in which the work unfolds; in subsection 3.7.6, it is formulated in full with an explicit explanation of what unites the stages and what distinguishes them.

A non-trivial methodological conclusion follows from this framework. If complexity is a tendency of open systems with a gradient, rather than a chain of random strokes of luck, then the specific outcomes of complexity - stars, life, consciousness - should be viewed not as miracles requiring separate explanations, but as local realizations of a general process. Each such realization is unique in its details, but not unique in its possibility. Where there is a gradient, something becomes more complex. What exactly depends on specific conditions: chemical composition, temperature regime, gravitational stability, and the presence of a medium for the accumulation and transmission of information.

The theory of complex systems - a field that brings together the work of Prigogine, Hermann Haken (Haken, 1983) on synergetics, Stuart Kauffman (Kauffman, 1993, 1995) on self-organization, and many others - describes the key mechanism of complexity. The more complex a system is, the more internal connections it has; the more connections it has, the richer its interaction with the environment; the richer the interaction, the more opportunities there are for further complexity. This is a nonlinear cycle with positive feedback, and it is important that it naturally arises in systems of a certain class - critically organized systems balancing between order and chaos, at the so-called edge of chaos. In this region, the system's behavior is stable enough to accumulate structure and flexible enough to reorganize it.

## 2.1. The Cambrian Explosion as a Phase Transition in the Biosphere

It is useful to demonstrate how this general mechanism operates on a specific scale, without jumping straight to consciousness. About 538 million years ago, an event known as the Cambrian Explosion occurred in Earth's paleontological record. For nearly three billion years prior to this, life on Earth remained relatively simple - single-celled organisms and primitive multicellular colonies. Then, over a geologically brief period (about 20-25 million years, which is truly short by biological standards), most of the basic animal body plans that exist to this day appeared.

In terms of structure, this event resembles a phase transition. I emphasize - resembles, not is, in the strict physical sense. The Cambrian explosion was driven by the interplay of many factors: the attainment of a certain concentration of atmospheric oxygen, the accumulation of genetic complexity (in particular, the development of Hox genes, which are responsible for the spatial organization of the body), the emergence of new types of intercellular interactions, and ecological feedback loops such as predator-prey relationships. None of these factors alone explains the acceleration, and none can be reduced to simple instability in the spirit of classical physical phase transitions. And yet the general structure of the event - a prolonged accumulation of conditions up to a critical threshold, after which a rapid qualitative restructuring of the system occurs - is the structure of a phase transition.

I draw attention to this similarity because it will recur later in the work at other scales. A prolonged buildup of tension. Reaching a critical threshold. A qualitative, not a smooth, shift. A new organization in which the previous degrees of freedom are repackaged. This is a general pattern running through physics, biology, and, as I will try to show in Section 3, through the emergence of consciousness. And this pattern is a special case of the structurally self-similar pattern described above: on the scale of the biosphere, it operates with the same four-step process that will later be seen to operate on scales ranging from autocatalytic chemistry to a conscious system.

From everything discussed in this section, we can draw an interim conclusion. Complexity is a natural tendency of open systems with an energy gradient. This tendency manifests itself through dissipative structures, through positive feedback between complexity and the environment, and through periodic phase transitions that reorganize the system into a new level of organization. Consciousness, if it arises, must fit into this overall picture - not as an exception, but as one of its stages. What exactly makes consciousness consciousness is a separate question, which we will address in the next section. But the framework within which this question stands is now defined: we are not asking about a miracle, but about the next step on the general ladder of complexity. This ladder, as Section 3 will show, is the main unfolding of that structurally self-similar pattern through which the work describes all the processes it covers.

# **3\. The Ladder: Operations, Transitions, Conditions**

This chapter offers a formal description of the emergence of consciousness as a sequence of steps, each of which is defined by the type of operations that the system performs on its own states. The transition between steps is not a quantitative increase in complexity, but the inclusion of a new type of operation in the system's repertoire. Each such transition is formally described by a minimal condition under which the new operation becomes possible, and by an empirically observable class of systems in which this condition is satisfied. Crucially, the conditions for each transition are necessary, but their sufficiency is probabilistic. This means that the ladder is not a deterministic chain in which the fulfillment of the conditions of step N automatically leads to step N+1. The ladder is an architecture of possibilities: at each step, the system enters a state in which the next step becomes attainable, but not guaranteed. The majority of systems remain in their current state. A minority moves on. This is consistent with the empirical evidence of biological and cosmic evolution and is formally articulated in Section 3.6.

Before unfolding the steps, it is necessary to introduce the architecture of the theory - four cross-cutting elements upon which the entire subsequent exposition is built. These elements are not four parallel statements; they form a single structure viewed from four sides. The first element answers the question "where does everything take place"; the second - "how is the structure of each step organized"; the third - "how is the result accumulated through the steps"; the fourth - "under what condition is accumulation possible." An introduction to each of them is provided below.

The first cross-cutting element: Y as a environment variable. Y is the structure of the physical world that ensures the possibility of the existence of systems as local maxima of causal closure and the possibility of their causal exchange with what lies beyond their boundary. Y manifests itself in three interrelated aspects: the causal-metric structure of spacetime; the configuration of fundamental fields, which makes physical interactions possible; and the information-thermodynamic structure, which sets the limits on the density and speed of exchanges. Y is objectively the same for all systems - it is a structural property of the physical world as such, independent of both the observer and any specific system. The spectrum of aspects of Y accessible to a specific system is the set of those types of structural relations for which the system has the architectural capacity for causal exchange. The access spectrum is a property of the system; Y is the structure of the world in which systems are possible.

A fundamental clarification. Y is defined neither as the material content of the world (the aggregate of fields, particles, objects) nor as the relation "that with which a given system exchanges." The first creates a circular definition, since the system itself is a local configuration of fields and turns out to be part of what is defined as Y. The second fragments Y into a family of media, one per system, and loses the invariance of the variable. Y is defined as a structural property of the world - that in which systems are possible as distinct entities. In this methodological move, the present work connects with two strands of contemporary philosophy of physics: Sorkin's causal set theory (Sorkin, 2003; Dowker, 2006), in which the fundamental physical reality is the structure of causal relations between elementary events, while spacetime and matter are its emergent properties; and Rovelli's relational interpretation of quantum mechanics (Rovelli, 1996), in which physical properties exist not absolutely but relative to other systems. The introduction of Y in this work does not require accepting either of these positions in their entirety, but is based on their common methodological approach - the description of physical reality through the structure of relations rather than through a list of objects.

Operationally, Y is identified through causal closure in three steps. First, the system boundary is identified: a system is a region of local maximum causal density relative to its surroundings, formally characterized via integrated information Φ (Tononi, 2004; 2016) or via a Markov blanket in active inference (Friston, 2013; Friston et al., 2022). Next, Y is identified relative to this boundary: Y functions as a structure of causal relations through which the system receives influences from the world and through which it influences the world, without entering its own causal closure. Finally, the spectrum of aspects Y accessible to the system is identified: for each type of causal exchange, the system either has or does not have a corresponding architecture. If it does, this aspect Y is included in its access spectrum. If not, the aspect exists objectively but is invisible to the system. This procedure provides the work with a dual methodological bridge: it is linked to the theory of integrated information via Φ as a measure of causal closure, which removes the criticism of arbitrariness in borrowing Φ as one of the operational axes for recording self-overlay (In this work, Φ functions not as a parameter borrowed from another theory, but as an operational measure of the very same structural property upon which the definition of Y is based); the procedure is linked to Friston's active inference via a Markovian blanket that operationalizes the system's causal boundary. These are not two independent borrowings, but two operationalizations of a single methodological foundation.

Y has physical limits. These are properties of Y itself as a structure of the world, not constraints imposed by an observer or a system. Three of them are essential for the subsequent exposition: the Bekenstein limit on information density in a bounded region of space, S ≤ 2πkRE/ℏc (Bekenstein, 1973; Bousso, 1999); the Landauer limit on the minimum energy required to erase a single bit, kT ln 2, which has been empirically confirmed (Landauer, 1961; Bérut et al., 2012); and the speed-of-light limit on synchronization between distant parts of a distributed system. These three limits are intrinsic properties of Y and serve as a formal condition for the upper rungs of the ladder.

The second cross-cutting element: the "gradient → retention → pole → transition" pattern. Each step of the ladder has the same internal structure, described by this pattern. A gradient is a difference - a structural condition in Y that exists where it exists. A gradient has no direction as a property: it is not "directed somewhere," does not "move toward something," and does not "press on something." A gradient operates there and in the way that the physics of that place allows. Retention is the realization of the difference through configuration: a configuration exists insofar as it physically realizes the difference in a stable manner. A pole is a structural point around which retention is organized - the point at which retention becomes differential with respect to its dual value (for example, "retained / not retained," "exists / does not exist" at stage 4). A transition is the moment at which the realization of the difference through a given configuration is exhausted, and a shift to a different type of realization becomes possible - that is, to a new stage at which a different type of difference operates.

This pattern repeats itself self-similarly at every level of the ladder - from dissipative structures to culture (stages 0-5); its possible continuation at the cosmological scale is treated separately (Section 7). A full explanation of the pattern's self-similarity and how it operates at each level is provided in Section 3.7.

The third cross-cutting element: the consolidation of successful configurations relative to Y. At each step, the configurations through which the gradient of that step is stably realized become consolidated - they continue to exist physically; configurations through which the realization is unstable do not become consolidated - they physically disappear. This is not "selection" in the sense of choice, nor is it "selection" in the sense of preference; it is a structural event that presupposes neither an agent nor an evaluating center. The success of a configuration is determined by the result - it supports the causal closure of the system with respect to Y to a greater extent - rather than by intention or function. Consolidation operates where there is a difference and there are configurations capable of realizing it. If there is no difference, there is no room for consolidation. If configurations that stably realize the difference do not arise, consolidation has no result.

Consolidation is substrate-neutral in structure but is realized in different substrates. In thermodynamic systems (stage 1), it operates through the differential stability of attractor states: configurations that are physically stable relative to the flow in Y are maintained longer than physically unstable configurations. In biological systems with autopoiesis (stages 2-5), it operates through the difference in rates of self-reproduction and destruction by the environment, which accumulates structural information over many cycles of reproduction. In information-causal systems, it operates through the adjustment of the configuration via feedback from the information environment, of which carriers of full V are a part. In all three cases, the same structural logic applies: the difference operates, and configurations that realize it stably become established. A substantive elaboration of consolidation as a cross-cutting mechanism of accumulation through the steps of the ladder is provided in Section 3.7.

The fourth end-to-end element: the system's emergent time as a difference in rates. Consolidation is possible only where there is a difference between the rate of the configuration and the rate of its destruction by the environment. If there is no difference in rates, the configuration disintegrates at the same speed at which it arises, and consolidation is structurally impossible. If a difference in rates exists, the configuration is maintained.
