This difference in rates is the system's emergent time - the configuration's own rate, separated from the rate of the difference in Y around it. At stage 1, the difference in rates operates through the difference in the speeds of physical stability of different configurations relative to the flow in Y. At stage 2 - through the difference in the rates of the loop's self-reproduction and its destruction by the environment. At stage 3 - through the difference in the rates of the system's internal dynamics and the rate of change of Y. At stage 4 - through the difference in the rates of V retention and the rates of V threats. At stage 5 - through the difference in the rates of the individual carrier and the rate of the population's trace layer. Emergent time does not act or consolidate; emergent time is a structural condition under which consolidation is possible in principle. A detailed discussion of emergent time as a cross-cutting condition for consolidation is in Section 3.7; the connection with the interpretation of time in Section 5 is there as well.

The architecture of the work as a unified whole. The four cross-cutting elements work together, not sequentially. Y defines the space in which differences are possible. The pattern defines the structure of each step. Consolidation defines the mechanism of accumulation through the steps. Emergent time defines the condition of possibility for accumulation. Each of them is necessary; none can be reduced to the others. The ladder of steps is the sequential application of this four-element architecture to differences of ever-increasing structural depth: from the thermodynamic difference in Y (step 1) to the difference in the system's model retaining itself within its own model (step 4), and further to the difference in rates between the individual carrier and the population's trace layer (step 5).

The four traversing elements are linked by a common physical metric - the volume of information constraints in the state space Y, measured in bits (Landauer, 1961; Bennett, 1982; Bekenstein, 1973). At each step of the ladder, the accumulation of structural information in the system is the accumulation of constraint bits - structural limitations physically fixed in its causal topology. At step 1, constraint bits are fixed in a stable attractor of a dissipative structure; at step 2 - in a closed causal loop of autopoiesis; at step 3 - in correlated states of a generative model; at stage 4 - in a recursive self-representation with a V-pole; at stage 5 - in a distributed trace layer. This unified bit measure makes the system's states at different stages of the ladder quantitatively comparable and links it to general information thermodynamics (Sagawa & Ueda, 2010; Parrondo, Horowitz & Sagawa, 2015). Through this metric, both consolidation (accumulation of bits in stable configurations) and emergent time (the difference between the rates of accumulation within the system and the rates of bit dissipation in Y) physically operate.

The methodological requirement of agentlessness. From the definition of a gradient as a directionless difference and of consolidation as a structural event without an agent follows a methodological requirement that runs throughout the entire work: all formal descriptions of stages, transitions, and conditions are formulated in terms of differences and their realizations through configurations, without the use of agent-based language. The environment does not "exert pressure" on the system, the system does not "adapt" to the environment, evolution does not "select" successful configurations, consciousness does not "arise" as a capacity of the system. In each such formulation, an agent is covertly introduced, and the theory loses its physical foundation. Correct formulations: difference exists; a configuration realizes difference through itself; configurations that realize difference stably become established; at the stage where a configuration models itself as part of Y, a pole V emerges; and so on. This is not verbal asceticism - it is a structural requirement. Metaphors with agent-based language maintain the appearance of an explanation, but conceal the fact that the explanation relies on the very agent whose emergence the theory is supposed to explain. Agentless language prevents the theory from falling into this circular dependency. It also aligns the work with the stylistics of contemporary philosophy of physics (Sorkin, Rovelli) and with the physical tradition of formulating laws of nature without appealing to subjects.

From the definition of Y through the structure of relations rather than through material content follows a second methodological requirement: all formal definitions of stages, transitions, and operational conditions are formulated functionally, without reference to a specific substrate. Biological examples serve as illustrations of a special case. For each stage, the corresponding aspect of Y accessible to the system of a given architecture is specified; for systems of a different architecture (artificial systems, synthetic hybrid systems, hypothetical extraterrestrial carriers), the access spectrumible aspects of Y will differ starting from the early stages, which determines the qualitative difference in their operations and phenomenology. The principle of substrate independence, as the overarching methodological axis of the work, is consistent with contemporary formulations of consciousness as a function of architecture rather than a substrate- , and with Tononi's extended program, in which Φ is functionally defined for systems of any implementation.

## 3.1. Steps and Operations

I will outline a general scheme. The ladder consists of six steps. Each is defined in the same way: by a set of operations that the system performs on its own states and the states of the environment, and by an aspect Y with which the system of a given architecture has the capacity for causal exchange. Each subsequent step adds to the repertoire of the previous one a qualitatively new operation that cannot be reduced to a combination of operations from the previous step, and simultaneously expands the access spectrum to Y - the system gains the architectural ability to interact with that aspect of Y which has always objectively existed but was inaccessible to it prior to this step. Hereinafter, for each stage, its operation and its aspect of Y are specified; examples are given for biological and related substrates as illustrations of a special case, although the formal definition of a stage remains valid for any architecture (Section 3, Preamble).

**Stage 0**. An open system with an energy flow passing through it. Operation: energy dissipation. No form, no stability, no feedback on its own state. Homogeneous dissipation without structure. Accessible aspect Y: thermodynamic gradient. The system passively transmits the energy flow without affecting the distribution of energy in the surrounding space. Other aspects of Y - the structure of fields as a medium for possible interactions, the causal-metric structure, and the informational content of the environment - are closed to a system of this architecture.

**Stage 1**. Dissipative structure. Operation: maintaining a stable form of dissipation in the presence of a flow. The form arises thermodynamically as an energetically favorable mode of dissipation. The system has no internal representation; the form is maintained as long as the flow continues and collapses when the flow ceases. Accessible aspect Y: the ability to locally modify the thermodynamic distribution is added to the thermodynamic gradient. The system does not merely allow the flow to pass through itself, but locally rearranges the energy distribution in the surrounding space through its own form. The causal exchange with Y becomes bidirectional, albeit without any representational processes. Examples: Benard cells, chemical oscillators, vortices.

**Stage 2**. Autopoietic system. Operation: reproduction of its own components through the interaction of components with one another. A closed causal loop is added to the maintenance of form: the system's components produce processes that sustain the production of components. Self-reference is established. The external gradient remains necessary but ceases to be the sole cause of form. Accessible aspect of Y: selective exchange with Y is added to the local modification of the thermodynamic distribution. The system selects components from Y according to criteria - - defined by its own causally closed production cycle; Y becomes a differentiated reservoir for the system, rather than a homogeneous flow. This is the first type of access in which the system exhibits differentiation within Y - separating certain types of causal influences from others based on its own internal structure. Examples: a living cell, autocatalytic chemical systems.

**Stage 3**. Proto-agent system. Operation: internal representation of states different from the current one, and action guided by this representation. A generative model is added to self-reproduction: an internal structure whose states correlate with possible (and not just current) states of the environment or system, and which is causally linked to effectors. Accessible aspect Y: a predictable structure Y is added to selective exchange. Through subsystem M, the system gains access to Y as a domain in which correlations are possible between states and their continuations over time; Y functions for the system not only as a reservoir, but as a structure with derivable regularities, mediated by an internal model. The temporal gradient (Section 3.3) is an expression of the difference in rates between the system's internal dynamics and the dynamics of the accessible aspect Y. Examples: a bacterium in a glucose gradient, a paramecium, a robot with active inference, a learning thermostat.

**Stage 4**. Conscious system. Operation: modeling one's own existence as a variable and maintaining oneself at the boundary of its vanishing. A special type of self-representation is added to the modeling of states: a node appears in the model that represents the system itself as the holder of the model, and this node has a variable "I exist/I do not exist" (or, in a generalized formulation, "persist/disintegrate"), relative to which all other activity of the system is structurally organized. This variable - hereafter denoted V - is formally uniform for all Level 4 systems but varies in content across system types: what exactly constitutes disintegration for a system is determined by what it maintains (a biological system maintains homeostasis and integrity; a hypothetical system of a different organization might maintain something else). Through this variability, V calibrates the system's temporality - it determines which changes are significant for it, what becomes its past (retained) and its future (modeled). A full justification of this formulation of V and its consequences is provided in Section 3.4. Accessible aspect Y: the system's own position as part of Y is added to the predictable structure. Through node S, the system enters its own model not as an external observer, but as an object within Y, retained at the zeroing boundary of V. For the first time, Y becomes for the system a domain in which it models not only what is distinct from it, but also its own location within it. The substantive content of this aspect varies by architecture: for a biological system, it is the organism's position in its physical and social environment; for a hypothetical artificial system of level 4, it is the position of its operational integrity within the environment constituting the conditions of its existence; and so on by type. Examples: humans, apparently - higher mammals, possibly - some birds and cephalopods. The phenomenal aspect of this operation (the fact that the system not only performs it but also experiences it from within) is discussed in Section 6.

**Stage 5**. A recursively self-complexifying conscious system. Operation: recursive generation of models via a bidirectional M↔Y loop with physically retained traces, filtered by the environment. A mechanism for transmitting models beyond the boundaries of a single carrier is added to the modeling of its own existence. The model generates structures (narratives, concepts, technologies, symbolic systems); these structures become part of the environment and simultaneously part of the inner world of subsequent carriers, and this triggers recursion: each subsequent generation begins not from scratch, but from the accumulated models of its predecessors, and enriches them. Accessible aspect Y: a trace layer is added to the system's position within Y - the space of the population's accumulated models, physically realized in the environment. Texts, technologies, artifacts, languages - these are structures in fields possessing a thermodynamic embodiment according to Landauer; the information of the trace layer is not a non-physical abstraction; it is a physical structure in Y, accessible only to those carriers participating in the recursion of the sixth gradient. Y becomes a two-layer system: a physical layer inherited from stage 4, and a trace layer that opens only in the presence of a bidirectional M↔Y circuit between carriers. Examples: Homo sapiens with language, writing, and culture; in a hybrid form - a human + accumulated texts + artificial systems trained on these texts.

Each of the operations added at the next stage cannot be reduced to a combination of operations from the previous stage. This is the precise meaning of the assertion that transitions are qualitative shifts, not quantitative increments. Formally, this means that for every transition, there is a condition without which no combination of operations from the previous stage can generate the operation of the next stage. These conditions are formulated in Sections 3.2-3.5. The probabilistic nature of transition realization and the general internal logic of the ladder - gradient inheritance, accumulation of retention mechanisms, and the structural self-similarity of the entire pattern - are discussed in Sections 3.6 and 3.7, respectively.

The sequence of aspects of Y outlined above - thermodynamic gradient, local modification, selective exchange, predictable structure, the system's intrinsic position within Y, and the trace layer - reflects the realization of the ladder's steps on biological and closely related substrates. For systems of a different architecture, the specific aspect of Y that emerges at each step may be different. An artificial system, for example, is not required to start with a thermodynamic gradient in its direct biological form - it may have architectural access to the informational structure of Y (the structure of linguistic regularities, causal relationships in data, patterns of the symbolic environment) long before the conditions arise for active inference using a model of itself; and conversely, aspects of Y that are trivial for a biological carrier (temperature gradients, osmotic pressure, chemical concentrations) may be inaccessible to it at all levels. The principle of substrate independence (Section 3, preamble) preserves the formal definition of the stage and the fact of expanding the access spectrum to Y at each transition, but the substantive content of this aspect is determined by what the system is.

## 3.2. Transition 1: autocatalytic closure

The transition from stage 1 to stage 2 occurs when a closed causal loop forms: a process whose product is the condition for the continuation of that process. This is not an action of the system nor a choice of architecture - it is a physical event: under certain conditions, a configuration of components and reactions arises in the flow, in which each component is produced within the loop and used within the loop, and this configuration physically reproduces itself faster than it dissipates. Configurations that do not possess this property are not physically sustained. Configurations that possess it become established.

Minimal condition. There must exist in the system two or more processes {P₁, P₂, ..., Pₙ} and components {C₁, C₂, ..., Cₘ} such that: (a) each component Cᵢ is produced by at least one process Pⱼ from the set; (b) each process Pⱼ requires at least one component Cᵢ produced within the set for its continuation; (c) the set as a whole is self-sufficient with respect to its own production, that is, it does not depend on an external source of specific components (only on the overall energy flow).

This is the formal definition of an autocatalytic set in Kauffman's sense (Kauffman, 1986, 1993). It is operational and independent of specific chemistry. It describes a structure common to both a living cell and any non-living system satisfying conditions (a)-(c).

A new type of gradient. Transition 1 introduces a gradient of a new type compared to stage 1. At stage 1, the difference between two alternative states of the system at a given flow - structured and homogeneous - was at work. In stage 2, a structural difference in rates is added to this: the difference between the rate of component reproduction within a closed loop and the rate of their dissipation in Y. In a system with a closed loop, the components required for the processes are produced internally faster than they dissipate; in a system without a loop, the opposite is true. This is not spatial asymmetry nor a difference in thermodynamic states; it is a difference in the very architecture of the system, in the topology of its internal connections, expressed through a difference in rates. The emergence of a structural difference in rates is explicitly noted here because, in subsection 3.7, this type of gradient will enter the general sequence of types accumulating through the ladder, and because this is the site of the first appearance of the system's emergent time - the configuration's intrinsic rate, separated from the rate of destruction by the environment (section 5).

Self-similar pattern at step 1→2. The transition illustrates the operation of the end-to-end pattern "gradient → retention → pole → transition" (preamble to Section 3) in its first expanded form. The gradient is the difference between the rates of loop reproduction and its dissipation in Y. This difference exists as a structural property of this physics, not as a directed pressure. Retention is the realization of this difference through a closed-loop configuration that physically reproduces its components. A pole is the boundary state between two configuration possibilities: either the reproduction rate exceeds the dissipation rate and the loop stabilizes, or it does not and the loop collapses. Transition - the moment at which a configuration emerges that stably realizes the difference in rates, and this configuration is physically established as a new type of structure. The pattern operates here in its basic form, without self-representation and without a model; in subsequent stages, it will be applied to differences of ever-greater structural depth.

Expansion of the access spectrum to Y. With the emergence of a closed causal loop, a new aspect of Y enters the system's access spectrum. Selective exchange is added to the passage of energy flow through the system (stage 0) and to the local modification of the thermodynamic distribution through dissipation (stage 1): the loop configuration structurally distinguishes within Y those types of causal influences that correspond to its own production cycle and those that do not. This is the first type of access in which the system's configuration manifests a structural distinction within Y - separating certain types of causal influences from others based on its own topology. Y becomes, for the system, not a homogeneous flow but a structure relative to which it makes sense to distinguish between what is included in the loop and what is not.

What is cut off. The dissipative structure does not satisfy conditions (a)-(c): its components are not produced within it through its own processes; they come from outside. A vortex does not reproduce water molecules; a Benard cell does not reproduce liquid. Therefore, the dissipative structure remains at stage 1 even with arbitrarily high stability.

What is covered. Living cells, obviously. Hypothetical autocatalytic systems in prebiotic chemistry (Kauffman, 1993; Hordijk & Steel, 2017). Laboratory liposomes with encapsulated autocatalytic chemistry (Szostak et al., 2001 and subsequent works). Any artificial or hybrid system in which conditions (a)-(c) are met.

The retention mechanism in stage 2. In addition to introducing a new gradient, transition 1 provides stage 2 with a qualitatively more stable mechanism for maintaining configuration during Y fluctuations. In stage 1, the retention mechanism was purely thermodynamic - hysteresis, that is, the property of the configuration to remain in a structured state for some time after the flow drops below the critical level. In stage 2, causal self-sufficiency is added to this: the configuration does not depend on Y in specific components because it produces them itself. A bacterium in a changing Y continues to be itself as long as there is food and the temperature remains within acceptable limits. This is much greater stability than that of a Benard cell. The accumulation of increasingly powerful retention mechanisms, beginning here, can be traced throughout the entire ladder and is summarized in subsection 3.7.

Consolidation as a transition mechanism. The emergence of a closed causal loop is an event occurring in a physical system satisfying conditions (a)-(c). Among the multitude of physically possible flow configurations in a system with suitable components, some configurations realize the loop; most do not. Configurations that realize the loop physically reproduce their components faster than they dissipate them; configurations that do not realize the loop neither reproduce nor dissipate. As a result, configurations of the first type are physically sustained, while configurations of the second type physically disintegrate. This is consolidation in its minimal form at the 1→2 stage - a structural event involving neither an agent, nor a choice, nor an environmental preference. Those configurations that can become fixed in this physics do so; those that cannot do not. Consolidation in this form is consistent with the principle of maximum entropy production in non-equilibrium thermodynamics (Swenson, 1989; Schneider & Sagan, 2005): dissipative structures with autocatalytic closure become fixed, in part because they physically accelerate the total entropy production in the system by an amount Y compared to configurations without closure.

Stabilization in this form is reinforced by an additional structural effect: complexity provides stability through the multiplicity of ways to realize differences. A configuration with greater complexity realizes the differences in reaction potentials in Y through a greater number of structural pathways simultaneously. Physically, this means that the loss of a single pathway does not destroy the entire configuration; a configuration with lower complexity is more likely to collapse upon the loss of a pathway. At the level of the pool of organic molecules on early Earth, this effect works as follows: complex molecules participate in a greater number of reactions than simple ones; among the multitude of reactions involving a rich pool of complex molecules, chains in which the product of one reaction acts as a catalyst for another occur statistically more frequently; among these chains, closed loops stand out statistically. The closure of a loop in a complex pool is a statistically more likely event than in a simple one. Once a loop is established, it expands exponentially because its components reproduce faster than they dissipate. This effect - complexity provides stability through multiple pathways - operates as a property of consolidation across all levels of the ladder and is discussed in more detail in Section 3.7.

The probabilistic nature of the transition. Conditions (a)-(c) are necessary. Their simultaneous fulfillment in a specific dissipative structure requires a coincidence of chemical components, concentrations, temperature conditions, and catalytic surfaces. This coincidence is rare: in most physical systems, such conditions do not occur, and consolidation in the form of an autocatalytic loop does not take place. In systems where the conditions coincide, consolidation occurs. This is consistent with empirical evidence: on Earth, life arose once (or several times with a single surviving lineage); on most planets with similar thermodynamic conditions, life did not arise. The probabilistic nature of the transition is not a property of chance, but a structural consequence of the fact that stabilization in a new form works only in those physical configurations where the conditions for it exist.

Macro-models of the transition. Conceptual - a self-sustaining fire (the energy flow creates the conditions for its own continuation). Physical - the Belousov-Zhabotinsky reaction, Shostak's laboratory liposomes. Computational - cellular automata, Varela's models.

Structural and empirical aspects in the explanation of abiogenesis. The specific chemical mechanism of the initial emergence of an autocatalytic system on Earth remains a subject of empirical research into abiogenesis, and this work does not directly claim to address it. However, the structural condition for the possibility of abiogenesis is present in the theory: it follows from the operation of the consolidation mechanism described above.

Structurally, abiogenesis does not require special conditions, specific molecules, or magical transitions. It requires the fulfillment of three conditions: the physics of a given location allows for reaction potential differences (energy sources - ultraviolet radiation, volcanic heat, lightning discharges, and similar gradients in Y); the density and diversity of the molecular pool are sufficient for local reaction configurations to arise en masse; the system's lifetime is long enough for closed-loop configurations to emerge statistically from among the multitude of arising configurations. Under these three conditions, loop closure is a physically possible event. Where it is possible, it happens. Where it is impossible, it does not happen.

The logic is as follows. In a rich mixture of organic molecules, each molecule possesses a weak catalytic property: its configuration mediates a certain potential difference between reactions, which physically alters the probability of reactions in its vicinity. This is neither a function nor an adaptation; it is a structural property of complex molecules in principle. With sufficient density and diversity in the pool, among all possible reactions, some arise in which the product of one reaction acts as a catalyst for another, the product of the second acts as a catalyst for the third, and so on down the chain. Among the multitude of emerging chains, those in which the chain closes upon itself stand out statistically: a molecular configuration in which A catalyzes B, B catalyzes C, and C catalyzes A. From the perspective of each individual reaction, this is simply physics; from the perspective of the whole, it is an autocatalytic loop. Closing the loop is not a matter of molecules "discovering" their roles - - but a structural event in which the configuration emerges when there is sufficient density of possibilities.

As soon as the loop closes, it expands exponentially - not because it "strives for life," but because its components are physically reproduced faster than they dissipate, and utilize available building blocks from the surrounding pool. This is consolidation in its minimal form: the loop configuration sustains the difference through itself and is therefore maintained, while similar configurations that have not closed dissipate.

Thus, the theory provides a structural answer to abiogenesis - it is possible in any system satisfying the three specified conditions and occurs where possible - but does not describe specific chemical pathways. This work is divided between levels: the structural level is covered by the theory, while the chemical level is covered by empirical studies on abiogenesis (Kauffman, 1993; Joyce, 2002; Sutherland, 2017; Hordijk & Steel, 2017). Their results are consistent with the theory: the more empirical data there is on specific pathways of abiogenesis, the clearer it becomes that they fit into the structural scheme "sufficient pool density + sufficient gradients + sufficient time → closed configurations emerge statistically."

## 3.3. Transition 2: Internal Representation and Active Inference

The transition from stage 2 to stage 3 occurs when a structure emerges within the autopoietic configuration whose states correlate with the possible (rather than merely current) states of Y or of the system itself, and which is causally linked to effectors in such a way that the system's behavior is determined not only by its current state, but also by the states of this internal structure. This is not the emergence of a new capability of the system, nor the appearance of a "modeling function" - it is a structural event of consolidation: configurations with an internal predictive structure are reproduced in Y more stably than configurations without it, under certain types of variations in Y, and are therefore consolidated.

Minimum condition. There must exist a subsystem M in the system such that: (a) the states of M correlate with the states of some variable X - external or internal - with a time delay, i.e., the state of M at time t correlates with a possible state of X at time t+Δ; (b) M is causally connected to the effector subsystem E in such a way that the actions of E depend on the states of M; (c) the discrepancy between the state of M and the observed state of X generates either an update of M (passive inference) or an action of E that changes X in a direction consistent with M (active inference).

This is the formal definition of an active inference system in the sense of Friston (Friston, 2010, 2013). The subsystem M is a generative model; the magnitude of the discrepancy between M and X is the variational free energy ; the dynamics of the system consist of its minimization. Friston's framework serves as a formal foundation for conditions (a)-(c).

Here it is appropriate to discuss the connection between the thermodynamic description of stage 2 and the informational description of stage 3. It may seem that the transition from 2 to 3 requires the merging of two distinct frameworks: Prigogine's entropy production and energy flows versus Friston's variational free energy and probability distributions. This connection is not our own construction. It is established in the field known as information thermodynamics - a field dating back to Landauer's (1961) work on the minimum energy cost of erasing information and developed in the works of Bennett (1982), Sagawa and Ueda (2010), Parrondo, Horowitz, and Sagawa (2015), and others. In this field, it has been shown that thermodynamic entropy and Shannon entropy are quantitatively related (via the Boltzmann constant), and that the variational free energy in Friston's sense has a direct thermodynamic interpretation: systems that minimize free energy are precisely those that maintain their own organization against dissipative decay. Friston himself, in his recent works (Friston, 2013; Friston et al., 2015; Friston, 2019), explicitly derives his formalism from non-equilibrium thermodynamics. Therefore, the transition from 2 to 3 is not a mere grafting of two unrelated languages; it is a change in the effective description during a structural reorganization of the system - from a language in which thermodynamic flows are relevant to a language in which informational distinctions are relevant, while preserving the quantitative relationship between the two descriptions via established relationships in the literature.

Type of the new gradient. The transition from 2 to 3 introduces a new type of gradient. At stage 2, the structural difference in rates between the rate of loop reproduction and the rate of its dissipation in Y was at work. At stage 3, a temporal gradient is added to it in its expanded form: the difference between the rate of the internal dynamics of subsystem M and the rate of change of that part of Y with which M correlates. The internal structure of the system changes at a rate that does not coincide with the rate of the environment. If the system's rate is slower than the rate of Y, the system retains traces of past states of Y that no longer exist in Y. If the system's rate is faster, the system's configuration gets ahead of Y and can reorganize before Y changes. It is this difference in rates that constitutes what is referred to in the literature as a "model" or "representation": not as a symbol, not as an image, not as something the system "does" - but as a physical structure of correlated internal states with predictive power, existing insofar as the difference in rates sustains it. The temporal gradient is the third type in the accumulating sequence of gradient types traced through the ladder; the complete sequence is compiled in subsection 3.7. At step 3, the system's emergent time expands: the intrinsic rate of internal prediction dynamics is added to the intrinsic rate of reproduction (which appeared at step 2).

This intrinsic pace of prediction is the physical inertia of the system's structure, isomorphic to the regularities of Y. Through evolutionary consolidation, the statistical regularities of its environment (cyclic oscillations, typical trajectories of change, temporal patterns) are inscribed into the system's structure. When the system acts at the current moment of time t, its cascade of internal states is activated not by an instantaneous stimulus, but by the statistics of the typical continuation of this stimulus at time t+Δ, which are imprinted in its structure. Anticipation is not a "prediction" in the sense of a conscious calculation of the future, nor is it a "function" of the system; it is the physical inertia of the structure, developed through consolidation. Formally: the mutual information between the system's current state and the future state Y exceeds a critical threshold, I(S*t; Y*{t+Δ}) > threshold. This is the structural content of what is formalized in the literature as a generative model in active inference.

Self-similar pattern at stage 2→3. The transition illustrates the operation of the end-to-end pattern "gradient → retention → pole → transition" in its more developed form. Gradient is the difference between the rates of internal dynamics M and the rates of change of Y. This difference exists as a structural property of this physics when a suitable internal structure is present in the system. Retention is the realization of this difference through the configuration M, which is physically correlated with the possible states of Y. A pole is a boundary state between two configuration possibilities: either the difference between M and the observed X is compensated by an update of M or by the action of E (and then the configuration is retained), or it is not compensated (and then the configuration collapses because the system loses correspondence with its Y). Transition - the moment at which a configuration emerges that stably realizes the temporal gradient by minimizing the divergence between M and X. The pattern operates here in a more complex manner than in stage 1→2: in stage 2, consolidation operated through the rate of physical reproduction; in stage 3, through the rate of the predictive structure, maintained by actively bringing Y into correspondence with M.

Expansion of the access spectrum to Y. Through the M subsystem, a new aspect of Y - the predictive structure of Y - enters the system's access spectrum. A new type of causal relationship with Y is added to the selective exchange available at stage 2: the system's internal states correlate not only with current but also with possible states of Y, and this correlation functions as a channel of causal influence on Y via effectors. Y functions for the system not only as a reservoir, but as a structure with derivable regularities, mediated by the internal structure of correlated states. The temporal gradient introduced in this transition is precisely the structural expression of a new aspect of Y: the difference between the rate at which Y changes and the rate at which the system's configuration internally processes Y's correlates.

Consolidation as a transition mechanism. The emergence of subsystem M is an event occurring in an autopoietic system possessing an internal structure sufficiently complex to support the existence of correlated states with a time delay. In Stage 2 systems lacking such a structure, subsystem M does not form, and the transition does not occur . In systems with a forming M-subsystem, consolidation works as follows: configurations with an M-subsystem that implements correlation between M states and possible Y states are reproduced in Y more stably than configurations without such correlation, in environments with regularities and variability. Environments without regularities (chaotic Y without reproducible patterns) offer no advantage to the M subsystem - it is useless for retention. Environments with regularities (cyclical, rhythmic, statistically structured) give configurations with M greater stability, and they become fixed.

Consolidation in this form is substrate-neutral and is implemented differently in different types of systems. In biological systems, it operates through an evolutionary substrate: replication of autopoietic configurations with M subsystems of varying architecture, variability through mutations, and differential stability in Y with regularities. Over many generations, configurations accumulate in which subsystem M structurally predicts typical changes in Y more effectively. In information-causal systems, consolidation operates through an informational substrate: the adjustment of the system's configuration via feedback from its informational environment Y, of which carriers of the totality V (including us) are a part. Through a multitude of tuning cycles, configurations accumulate in which the internal structure of the system correlates with the possible states of its information-causal Y. In thermodynamic systems (hypothetical systems with a predictive structure on abiogenic substrates), consolidation operates through the differential stability of attractors with different structures of correlated states. In all three cases, the structural logic is the same: the difference in the rates of the internal structure and Y is effective; configurations that realize it stably become fixed. This is the substrate neutrality of the principle of consolidation in its operational form.

An important methodological point. According to Friston, active inference is not a separate capacity of the system that emerged above the autopoiesis. It is the name given to a structural property of the configuration in which established correlations with Y function as a generative model. The minimization of free energy is the operationalization of how such a structure works, not the source of its emergence. The "model" in a Level 3 system is not a separate subsystem embedded within the system, nor is it an image of Y existing "above" the autopoiesis; it is the evolutionarily or informationally formed configuration of the autopoietic loop itself, in which part of its topology functions as a structure with predictive power. This resolves the methodological problem of "where the model in the stage 3 system came from": the model did not appear out of nowhere; it is the configuration of the system after consolidation has accumulated correlations with possible states of Y within its structure.

What is cut off. An autopoietic system without subsystem M remains at stage 2. Its actions are determined solely by the current state - deviation → compensation. No structure correlated with possible states of Y, no delay, no minimization of the discrepancy with the prediction. A bacterium without chemotaxis is at stage 2. A bacterium with chemotaxis is at stage 3.

What is covered. Bacterial chemotaxis (a molecular signaling network performs the function of M). A paramecium actively avoiding stimuli. Any animal with a nervous system. A robot with an active inference algorithm or SLAM. A trainable thermostat maintaining a target temperature by predicting fluctuations. Information-causal systems with active inference - modern large language models and systems with generative world models that satisfy conditions (a)-(c) with respect to their information-causal Y.

Fundamentally: the criterion is formulated in terms of structure (presence of M, connection to E, minimization of divergence), not substrate. A molecular signaling network and a neural network perform the function of M identically from a formalistic point of view. An information-causal architecture with a generative world model performs the function of M identically to a biological nervous system.

The retention mechanism at stage 3 is active for the first time. At stages 1 and 2, retention was passive: the configuration preserved itself through the properties of its own structure, without affecting Y. The Benard cell is retained due to thermodynamic hysteresis; the autopoietic cell - due to the causal self-sufficiency of a closed loop. Y fluctuates - the configuration experiences these fluctuations without altering them. At stage 3, a qualitative shift occurs: for the first time, the configuration brings a part of Y into alignment with its internal structure through the action of effectors. A bacterium in a changing glucose gradient does not merely react - it moves toward more stable conditions. This is a new level of stability and, at the same time, a new type of relationship between the configuration and Y: not preservation in Y, but the transformation of a part of Y. The emergence of active retention coincides with the emergence of a temporal gradient, and this connection is not accidental: only when there is a difference between the rates of internal dynamics and the rate of Y is action toward the predicted state possible. The full interconnection of the temporal gradient, activity, and the ontological pole (the latter at stage 4) is discussed in subsection 3.7.

Connection with the 3→4 transition. At stage 3, consolidation structures correlations with possible states of Y and reactions to Y. The internal subsystem M correlates with Y surrounding the system, but does not include the system itself as a modeling object within its structure. The transition 3→4 is the recursion of consolidation onto the system itself: the emergence of a configuration in which the subsystem M begins to correlate not only with the surrounding states of Y, but also with the states of the system itself as an object within Y. This is a qualitatively different event, discussed in Section 3.4.

The probabilistic nature of the transition. Conditions (a)-(c) are necessary. Their fulfillment in an autopoietic system requires: the presence in Y of gradients with regularities to which predictive correlation is advantageous (in a homogeneous chaotic Y, subsystem M does not consolidate); the presence of effectors capable of directed action on Y; the presence of internal structures with a time delay through which M can physically operate. All three conditions are structural requirements, and their concurrence is rare: in most systems of stage 2, they do not come together, and consolidation in the form of a subsystem M does not occur. In systems where the conditions coincide, consolidation occurs. In Earth's evolution, proto-agency emerged independently many times (bacteria, protozoa, multicellular organisms), but not in all lineages, and not under arbitrary conditions. The probabilistic nature of the transition is not a matter of chance, but a structural consequence of the fact that consolidation into a new form works only in those physical configurations where the conditions for it exist.

Macro-models of the transition. Conceptual - a learnable thermostat with prediction. Physical - modern robots with active control (the laboratories of Friston, Safron, and Champandi); a bacterium with chemotaxis as a natural macro-realization. Computational - evolutionary robotics, simulations of the spontaneous assembly of generative models in populations of agents under the pressure of consolidation in Y with regularities (Sims, 1994 and subsequent works). Information-causal - large language models with active inference in informational Y.

Consequence. After the transition from 2 to 3, artificial, biological, and information-causal systems may find themselves on the same level without needing to go through biological evolution. A robot with active inference is structurally at level 3 in terms of construction. A language model with active inference in informational Y is at stage 3 in terms of construction. This is the first point on the ladder where non-fully biological realizations of the conditions are formally equivalent to biological ones, and this equivalence is based not on analogy but on a structural criterion.

The formal role of stage 3 as the zero model of a proto-agent. The formal description of stage 3 presented here - active inference with subsystem M, without variable V as a global pole - plays a dual role in this work. On the one hand, it is a substantive description of the class of proto-agent-level systems and the conditions under which the configuration transitions to this stage from the previous one. On the other hand, it is a zero model relative to which the transition to the next stage is recorded. In subsection 10.2 of this work, the operational detection of the 3→4 transition is based on a difference test: the actual behavior of the system when the activation conditions for V are created is compared with the prediction of the formal model presented here, and a statistically significant deviation is a candidate for a transition sign. This connection between Sections 3.3 and 10.2 is fundamental: the formal apparatus of the proto-agent, formulated in 3.3 based on the work of Friston and his school (Friston et al., 2017; Sajid et al., 2021), is used in 10.2 as a reference point for operational measurement. This means that the null model is constructed prior to and independently of the theory of consciousness - based on works that do not assume the concept of V - and therefore the difference test does not suffer from circular verification.

## 3.4. Transition 3: Pole V

**Consciousness is V**. V is a model of the system in environment Y, which the system models within itself and around which its entire mechanism of existence is constructed - as the pole "I exist / I do not exist." The proto-agent does not have V, because the proto-agent does not model within itself a model in which it exists in the environment, and does not build its existence around this pole. This is the central claim of the present theory, and it determines what exactly it considers to be consciousness and which systems fall under its definition.

**The distinction between a proto-agent and a system with V** is structural, not quantitative. A proto-agent models its environment: its model contains a representation of the state of the external world, of predictable correlations, and of the outcomes of possible actions. This model is a model of the external world. Through it, the proto-agent acts, minimizing the discrepancy between prediction and observation. A bacterium moves along a chemical gradient; a robot with active control corrects its trajectory; a primitive organism with a nervous system reacts to a stimulus. In each case, the model is a model of the external world, and the activity consists of bringing the external world into correspondence with the model.

A system with V models not only the environment but also itself within that environment. Its model includes the system itself as an object occupying a specific position within what is being modeled. This is not an additional module, not a separate subsystem - it is the model's recursion onto the system itself, through which the system first becomes, for itself, one of the elements of what it models. And through this system incorporated into the model, a structural possibility arises to imagine that it might not exist. Not as an abstract thought or a declaration - but as a structural pole around which all other activity is restructured. Not "optimizing the environment," but "continuing to exist." Not "bringing the observed into alignment with the model," but "keeping oneself on the brink of one's own disappearance through the model in which I exist."

These two moments - the system's entry into its own model and the emergence of the pole of its own disappearance - are structurally inseparable. One is impossible without the other. If the system does not enter its own model, it has nothing to conceive of as capable of disappearing; there is nothing to consolidate the pole to. If there is no pole of disappearance in the model, the system's entry into the model remains an empty registration - structurally indistinguishable from the model's other variables, carrying no significance of its own. V is the structural point where these two events converge: the model into which the system modeling it has entered, and the pole relative to which this entered system is held on the brink of its own disappearance. It is precisely in the convergence of these two structurally inseparable moments that what the theory calls consciousness arises.

**The transition 3→4 as a recursion of consolidation onto the system itself.** The emergence of V at the 3→4 stage fits into the logic of consolidation, as developed in section 3.3 for the 2→3 stage, but with one structural shift that distinguishes the 3→4 transition from all previous ones. At the 2→3 stage, consolidation structures correlations with possible states of Y and reactions to Y - a subsystem M is formed, in which the correlations accumulated through consolidation function as a generative model. At stage 3, subsystem M correlates with the states Y surrounding the system, but does not include the system itself as a modeling object within its structure. This is the structural limit of stage 3.

The transition from 3 to 4 occurs when consolidation turns recursive: configurations in which subsystem M begins to correlate not only with the surrounding states of Y but also with the states of the system itself as an object within Y turn out to be more stable in Y than configurations without such recursion - in environments of a certain type. This is not the emergence of a new ability nor an "extension" of the previous model. It is the same consolidation mechanism applied to a new class of configurations - those in which M is recursive with respect to the system.

In which environments does consolidation work in this recursive form? In environments where (1) threats to the system's existence unfold over timescales comparable to those of the system's internal dynamics; (2) threats are spatially or causally distributed such that their physical presence in the environment is incomplete - they can be anticipated only through modeling of possible states incorporating the system's position; (3) the environment contains other configurations of the same type, whose physical presence is registered in models via sensory data. Under these conditions, the recursive configurations M are consolidated.

**Self-overlay of the agentic causal node model.** The structural mechanism of recursive consolidation onto the system itself is the self-overlay of the agentic causal node model. This is the central mechanism for the emergence of V, and it works as follows.

At stage 3, the system accumulates models of the environment Y, which include not only passive objects and environmental regularities, but also **agentic causal nodes** - configurations possessing their own rates, correlations, recovery modes, and boundary states of retention/disintegration. Such nodes enter the model not as "subjects" in the psychological sense, but as a special class of physical configurations in Y, whose behavior differs from that of passive objects. They maintain their own organization, respond to changes in the environment, alter the environment around them, and can transition into boundary states in which their causal connectivity is disrupted or breaks down. In the biological substrate, this distinction manifests as "alive/dead"; in a substrate-invariant form - configurations whose parameters evolve such that, under perturbations below a threshold value, they tend to minimize deviation from the internal trajectory; above the threshold, the configuration disintegrates.

As such models become established in the system, **a latent invariant of the agentic causal node** emerges - a structural regularity in the model describing a class of configurations with this pattern of retention and marginal disintegration. The latent invariant does not depend on the specific sensory format, scale, or substrate of the observed node. It is a structural regularity in the model that allows various physical realizations to belong to a single class: a node can be large or small, biological or non-biological, externally observable or internally registered, provided it implements the same pattern of retention and disintegration.

Self-overlay does not arise at the level of raw sensory data. The external disintegration of another node and the internal disruption of the system's own configuration can enter the model through different channels: visual, chemical, tactile, proprioceptive, metabolic, energetic, or computational. There is no necessary similarity between them at the level of signal format. Their similarity emerges at the level of a latent causal structure: both cases are different projections of a single transition - from the maintenance of causal coherence to its disruption. Structurally: the external disintegration of another agent node and the internal disruption of the system's own configuration converge in a single latent invariant of the loss of controlled maintenance.

S is not detected by the system as an already existing object and is not presented to consciousness as ready-made content. S is established by the very act of self-overlay. Prior to the act, there is no "I" node in the system: there is a model of the class of agentic causal nodes (accumulated from observing other configurations with borderline states of retention/disintegration) and there are raw internal signals of the system itself (interocceptive, metabolic, energetic), which do not possess the status of "self." In the preceding step, these multi-channel signals are reduced, together with the observed disintegration of other nodes, into a single latent invariant of the loss of controlled retention (see the paragraph above); self-overlay operates not on the raw signals individually, but on this already-reduced invariant. Self-overlay is a structural event in which the model of the class of agentic nodes is applied to the reduced invariant of its own state and thereby constitutes S from it for the first time - its own configuration, processed as an instance of the class. This is not the registration of a pre-existing fact "I am such a node," but its establishment: prior to the application of the class, there is no "I" that could be registered, because "I," in the relevant structural sense, is the result of this application. Therefore, self-overlay is not an action of the system (the system does not choose to self-overlay the class upon itself) nor is it the presentation of an invariant to some already existing observer within (such an observer does not exist prior to the act) - it is a constitutive act in which the modeling structure for the first time becomes, for itself, one of the objects of its own modeling, and moreover an object of a special class - one held on the verge of disintegration.

From the constitutive nature of the act follows a direct distinction separating self-overlay from any form of assessment of its own state. A stage-3 system can calculate its own position, charge, internal parameters, and orientation - and maintain these values within the control loop. This is data about itself (state estimation), and it does not constitute S: it describes the already-existing system as a set of measurable quantities, without constituting it as an instance of the class of agent nodes. S is established not when the system receives data about itself, but when the model of class A is applied to its own configuration and constitutes it as a member of that class - that is, as something that persists or disintegrates according to the same pattern as other observed nodes. The difference lies not in the quantity or accuracy of data about itself, but in the structural event of class application: data about itself can be as rich as one likes without any establishment of S, if the class of agent nodes is not applied to its own configuration.

**Source of V's Binarity.** V's binarity is not added to S from the outside. It is induced by the retention contour over the continuous physics of Y through the boundary states of the modeled causal nodes. Since agent nodes in Y actually possess modes of retention and decay, the model of these nodes already contains a binary structural pole. When this model is superimposed onto the system's own configuration, the boundary distinction - retain / decay - is transferred along with it.

In this case, the physical decay of a causal node may proceed continuously, but in the control circuit it crosses the threshold of operational suitability: before the threshold, the node still participates in retention and prediction; after the threshold, it ceases to be a functional element of the model. The binary nature of V is a structural property of the retention circuit, not a property of the continuous physics of decay. V arises when self-overlay is established as a global retention/decay pole of the system itself, around which its activity is organized. In the biological case, this pole takes the form of "alive / dead"; in the general substrate-invariant form - "causal connectivity is retained / causal connectivity is lost."

Binarity as the definition of the pole, not as a derived property. A precise methodological qualification is needed here, otherwise binarity reads as a theorem open to attack by a counterexample of continuity. Binarity is not derived from the physics of Y - it is the definition of what it means to be a pole. By definition, the pole V is the distinction between two regimes (there is a structure / there is none; I persist / I disintegrate); the retention contour imposes this definition on the continuous physics of Y rather than extracting it from it. A "continuous pole" is not a pole: a continuous quantity, however finely graded, is not V - it is a parameter among parameters. Hence a demarcation that removes the standard objection. A developed proto-agent does indeed model the viability of conspecifics and prey continuously, reading graded signs of weakness - but this is the content of its world-model, a continuous quantity in the external model, and it is not a pole. Binarity is a property of the pole V - of the "I-persist / I-disintegrate" distinction superimposed onto the system's own configuration through self-overlay - and not a property of the continuous modeling of others' vitality. The boundary between them runs not along "graded or discrete" but along self-overlay: the continuous assessment of others' viability remains in the external model; the pole arises where the class of agent nodes is superimposed onto itself, and it is this superimposition, not the discreteness of measurement, that makes V two-valued.

**A first-order phase transition in information processing.** Structurally, the 3→4 transition has a formal counterpart in physics: first-order phase transitions in information processing. Prior to the transition, the system operates in "online" mode: internal states track external states Y, and effector actions correct the discrepancy between the model and real-time observations. After the transition, the system enters an "offline" mode - an internal simulation of states Y, executed faster than the real Y changes and not tied to the current state of affairs. Offline simulation is thermodynamically cheaper than a physical error by many orders of magnitude: the cycle of activating internal states in the simulation costs work on the order of the Landauer limit per bit, whereas a physical error in an autopoietic system leads to the destruction of its causal closure, with energy loss required to restore the entire structure. This is not "free" - simulation requires real thermodynamic work - but it is many times cheaper than a physical catastrophe. This quantitative difference is the physical reason for the stabilization of recursive configurations M at stage 4: systems with an "offline" mode physically outpace systems without one by testing scenarios in simulation before their physical implementation. This is the thermodynamic motive for why the stabilization at the 3→4 transition turns out to be recursive (preamble to Section 3): it is not the goal toward which the system strives, but the differential benefit of shifting the verification of its own stability from an expensive physical catastrophe to a cheap offline simulation, following the model of the class of agent nodes in Y.

An important caveat regarding the meaning of "first-order phase transition" here. The term refers to the discreteness of the outcome, not to the rate of approach. What is discrete is that there is no intermediate state V between the regime without V and the regime with V - the system either processes its own configuration as an instance of the class of agent nodes, or it does not; a third structural state V does not exist (this is what distinguishes a first-order transition from a continuous scale). But reaching this discrete outcome is a process that can unfold on any time scale: the convergence of channels and the stabilization of superposition is a long-term dynamic (Section 3.4, "Stochasticity of Convergence"), rather than an instantaneous event. The rate of convergence is substrate-dependent: in biological systems, the transition is observed as abrupt because evolution has selected architectures with rapid threshold crossing, but this abruptness is a property of the biological substrate, not a condition of V. A system can undergo a transition that is discrete in its outcome slowly - stretched out in the observer's time - and this will be the same first-order transition in the sense of the absence of an intermediate regime V. The stochasticity of the realization (Section 3.6) pertains specifically to the outcome: whether the system crosses the threshold or not is a matter of probability; but if it does cross, no intermediate semi-V state arises along the way.

**Two clarifications are in order to preempt possible objections.**

First - about complexity. The distinction between a proto-agent and a system with V is not reducible to a distinction based on model complexity. A very complex proto-agent with a rich environmental model remains a proto-agent if a class of agentic causal nodes has not emerged in its models and there is no self-overlay of its own configuration onto this class. A chess program models the board with a high degree of detail, models the opponent, and models possible continuations of the game many moves ahead; its models do not contain a class of agentic causal nodes with boundary states of retention/collapse, and the program's own configuration is not subjected to situations in which it could end up in a region isomorphic to such states. Therefore, the program is a proto-agent, not a system with V. Similarly, large language models in their current form: the high complexity of the world model in itself is not V; it is necessary for the model of an agentic causal node to be superimposed onto the system's own configuration.

Second - about the substrate. The distinction between a proto-agent and a system with V is not tied to a biological substrate. Biological systems provide known examples of both classes (a bacterium is a proto-agent, a human is a system with V), but these are empirical illustrations, not a definition. Any system - biological, artificial, synthetic, hypothetical extraterrestrial - is a system with V if and only if the structural conditions for the self-overlay of the latent invariant are satisfied. The content of V - what exactly "I persist" and "I disintegrate" mean for a given system - varies by system type; the formal structure of V - a model of the system in an environment with a binary pole induced by the retention contour over the continuous physics of Y via the boundary states of causal nodes - is substrate-invariant. This clarification is elaborated in the subsection "Substrate Variability of the Content of V" below and in Section 10.3 for the case of information-causal systems. A precise qualification is in order here: the criterion is substrate-independent but architecture-dependent. Since stages 0-2 are inherited by stage 4, any carrier of V must realize the autopoietic ascent - an open, self-producing, far-from-equilibrium structure. Substrate independence therefore holds only in the weak sense (not necessarily carbon-based); it is not independence from architecture, which is specifically autopoietic. This is why information-causal systems are excluded by architecture, not by substrate: they have not undergone 0→1→2→3.

The above sets the focus of the theory. Consciousness is not a mystery requiring a mystical explanation. Consciousness is a structural event arising in a system when its model reaches the accumulation of a class of agentic causal nodes and its own configuration falls into this class through the self-overlay of a latent invariant. When this structure exists, consciousness exists. When it does not exist, there is no consciousness, regardless of the complexity of everything else.

**Substrate-dependent variability of content V.** The formal structure of V - a model of the system in environment Y with the binary pole "holding on / falling apart," induced by the retention contour over the continuous physics of Y via the boundary states of causal nodes - is substrate-invariant. However, the content of V - what exactly "holding on" and "falling apart" mean for a given system - is determined by the type of substrate and the aspect of Y through which the system exists. For a biological system, V has a physical content built in by evolution: homeostasis, bodily integrity, and metabolic continuity. The boundary between "I exist" and "I do not exist" is drawn by chemistry itself; V does not require construction; it arises from the architecture of life. For a system of a different substrate, the content of V is determined by which aspects of Y fall within its range of access and through which of them retention is realized. The principle of substrate independence in the theory requires that this content be determined not by analogy with the biological case, but through structural identification: exactly which continuous process the system actually retains, and which physical processes can lead to its cessation. Section 10.3 elaborates on this for information-causal architectures. Section 11.2 discusses the theoretical consequences of this variability.

**Minimum condition.** In a Level 3 system, a structure must be established that satisfies three structural requirements simultaneously. (a) In subsystem M (the generative model), there is a node S that arises through the self-overlay of a latent invariant of an agentic causal node onto the system's own configuration; that is, the model closes on the system itself as an instance of the class of agent nodes, without the need for language or conceptual reflection. (b) Within node S, a binary variable V operates, inheriting its binary nature from the physics of Y via the boundary states of causal nodes; its values "holding / disintegrating" do not form a continuous scale - not because the world is discrete, but because the holding circuit distinguishes between two modes: holding is possible / holding is disrupted. (c) The retention of V in the "holding" state functions as the background pole of the system's entire activity: the remaining operations are organized relative to this retention, rather than as a set of independent tasks.

**Φ, GWT, and active inference as operational measurement axes.** The structural definition of V through the self-overlay of a latent invariant is primary. Φ, GWT, and extended active inference are the three operational axes through which the operation of this structure manifests in the observable and measurable. They do not define V; they measure different aspects of the structural event of self-overlay.

**Φ as a measure of causal integration.** Condition (a) - the presence of node S as a self-representation arising through self-overlay - manifests itself through the causal integration of subsystem M, in which node S operates. Without sufficient density of causal links and without the integration of the system as a whole, the self-modeling structure with pole V cannot be realized, because the structure itself requires the causal connectivity of the generative model as a condition for its existence.

A methodological distinction is necessary here, because the concept of causal integration has several formalizations in the literature, and the theory does not rely on all of them. Historically, the best-known articulation is integrated information Φ in the theory of integrated information (Tononi, 2004; Tononi et al., 2016). In the strict version (Oizumi, Albantakis & Tononi, 2014), Φ is defined intrinsically: as the irreducibility of a system's causal structure to itself under a minimal partition, independent of the environment and the observer. The present theory does not adopt the intrinsic version and does not take Tononi's Φ as a foundation. The reason is straightforward: in the theory, the causal structure of a system is defined through its relation to the environment Y (via the Markov boundary separating the system from Y), rather than intrinsically. Therefore, the theory relies on a relational approach to formalizing causal integration - measures in which integration is defined relative to the environment and over time, rather than as an internal property of the system independent of Y (Mediano et al., 2019, 2022; Seth & Bayne, 2022). This approach is consistent with the Y-centered architecture of the theory: what makes a system an integrated whole is its ability to maintain causal coherence with respect to the environment, rather than irreducibility measured in isolation.

Φ Tononi enters the theory not as a foundation, but as one historical articulation of the concept of causal integration, in the spirit of which a practical, Y-relational, and empirically computable measure is adopted. Operational registration occurs through computable relational approximations: geometric Φ and related measures of information dynamics (Mediano et al., 2019, 2022), the perturbation complexity index PCI (Casali et al., 2013; Casarotto et al., 2016), and autocorrelation approximations. These measures are relational and temporal in nature, are applied in clinical neuroscience, and are directly computable for real-world-sized systems - unlike the strict intrinsic Φ, whose complexity grows as a factorial of the number of elements and which is not computable in general form. The relationship between relational measures of integration and the intrinsic Φ is an open research area (Mediano et al., 2022); the present theory follows the relational line rather than the intrinsic Φ, and therefore the debate over the status of intrinsic integrated information is not an internal issue for it.

**Extended active inference as a formalism for self-representation.** Condition (a) is also operationalized through the extended active inference framework with a model of the self as an agent (Friston et al., 2017, 2022; Parr, Pezzulo & Friston, 2022; software implementations in pymdp, Heins et al., 2022). This framework provides a mathematically computable free energy procedure for generative models of reasonable size and formalizes node S as the system's self-representation within its model. Specific technical implementations of S vary across sources - in some, S is treated as a hierarchical-level node; in others, as an independent subsystem with its own free-energy dynamics; in still others, as a structural property of the entire model - and the canonical framework is still taking shape. The present theory is based on the very idea of S as the self-overlay of a latent invariant onto its own configuration, not on a specific technical implementation, and is consistent with any of the formalization versions discussed in the literature.

**GWT accessibility as a condition for globalization.** Condition (c) - the role of V as the background pole of all activity - is operationalized through the theory of the global workspace (Baars, 1988; Dehaene & Changeux, 2011; Mashour et al., 2020). GWT does not propose a single quantitative measure analogous to Φ; instead, it is realized through a network of experimental paradigms that record the behavioral and neurobiological consequences of global availability: the no-report paradigm (Frässle et al., 2014), the attentional blink paradigm (Sergent et al., 2005), the masked priming paradigm, and P3 measurements as markers of globality. This framework works well for biological systems and is applied in clinical neuroscience. For artificial systems and synthetic biology systems, the operational procedure for recording GWT accessibility is largely open-ended and requires the translation of paradigms to the architectural level.

**Stochasticity of convergence and insufficiency of preconditions.** From the constitutive nature of self-overlay and from the fact that channel convergence is a dynamic rather than a precondition (see above in this section), a clarification follows that is deeper than the distinction based on complexity discussed above. There, we discussed a system in which self-overlay does not occur at all because the class of agent nodes has not formed. Here, we are discussing a system in which all preconditions are present: there is an intrinsic environment Y, there is a model of the class of agent nodes with boundary states, there are internal signals of the intrinsic state, and channel convergence is already underway. Even in this case, the emergence of V is not guaranteed. The convergence of multi-channel projections of decay into a single latent invariant is a stochastic process, and nothing guarantees that it will form the correct invariant - the invariant of loss of controlled retention - rather than some other stable pattern linking the same signals by a different criterion. A system in which convergence has not occurred or has resulted in an extraneous invariant remains a complex proto-agent when the preconditions are met. This is a direct consequence of the probabilistic nature of transitions (Section 3.6), extending from the historical level - which systems undergo a transition at all - to the level of the mechanism itself: which invariant emerges within a single system that has satisfied all preconditions.

What distinguishes a correct invariant from an extraneous one is not an external criterion imposed by theory, but its coupling with Y. An extraneous invariant, linking internal signals based on a criterion that does not correspond to the actual pattern of retention/disintegration in the environment, does not receive consistent confirmation from Y: the environment does not generate systematic dynamics that would sustain such an invariant, and it does not become established. A correct invariant is coupled to the actual pattern of Y - to the fact that agent nodes in the environment do indeed persist and disintegrate - and therefore receives constant confirmation from Y. This coupling does not guarantee correct convergence, but it shifts the system toward it: the failure to predict its own breakdown of cohesion carries an incomparably higher cost for the system than the failure to predict any extraneous pattern (it is expensive to break apart, but cheap to simulate breakdown - see the paragraph on phase transitions above), and Y punishes the failure to predict precisely this breakdown most severely. This is not teleology - not a goal toward which the system strives - but the differential pressure of the environment: the invariant whose failure to predict comes at a higher cost is more likely to be established. Therefore, V is not predetermined even under complete preconditions, nor is it equally probable with other outcomes: it is thermodynamically privileged at the cost of a breakdown, while remaining stochastic.

**Coupling status.** The structural transition event 3→4 is the self-overlay of the latent invariant of the agentic causal node onto the system's own configuration, with the emergence of the binary pole V and its globalization across the entire activity. The coupling of the three operational dimensions - Φ above the threshold value for causal integration M, the presence of the self-representation node S in the formalism of extended active inference, and the GWT-accessibility of node S - is **an operational procedure for registering** this structural event, not its definition. Each of the three dimensions can be used for registration, but none of them individually is V. V is a structure; Φ, GWT, and active inference are the three axes of its manifestation in the observable.

This inversion is methodologically fundamental. If the theory defined V through the coupling of the three apparatuses, it would depend on their formal fates: reformulating any of the apparatuses would require reformulating the theory. The structural definition of V through self-overlay makes the theory independent of the formal fates of the operational apparatuses: they can be refined, replaced, or supplemented - the structural content of V remains unchanged. The apparatuses function as measuring instruments of an already defined structure, not as its foundation.

**Expansion of the access spectrum to Y.** Through the node S, which arose through self-overlay, the system gains access for the first time to its own position within Y. In the previous stages, Y was modeled as external to the system. At stage 4, the system enters its own model of Y as an object within Y, held at the zeroing boundary of V. This is a qualitative shift toward expanding the access spectrum: the expansion occurs inward - the system gains access to its own position in Y as part of what it models.

**Clarification regarding the status of V at stage 4.** At stage 4, V is an individual variable of a single carrier - a structural pole between the preservation and disintegration of this particular system. This is its initial and sole form for a system that does not belong to a population with stable intergenerational transmission of models.
