This property of consolidation operates across all steps of the ladder and explains why the ladder moves toward complexity rather than toward simplification: not because "complexity is the goal," not because "evolution moves toward complexity," but because more complex configurations are statistically maintained longer, and this shifts accumulation toward increasing complexity. This is the structural physical basis of the ladder's directionality - not teleology and not directionality as a property, but the statistical effect of the multiplicity of ways to realize differences.

Consolidation as a mechanism of pattern self-similarity. The self-similar pattern "gradient → retention → pole → transition" (subsection 3.7.6) is reproduced at all steps of the ladder. Consolidation is the mechanism that makes this self-similarity possible: at each step, consolidation operates on differences of a new type, and through the operation of consolidation, configurations that realize the difference in a stable manner become consolidated as a new type of structure. Without consolidation, the pattern would remain an abstract scheme; through consolidation, it becomes a mechanism through which complexity accumulates across the steps.

### 3.7.8. The system's emergent time as a condition for consolidation

Consolidation is possible only where there is a difference between the rate of the configuration and the rate of its destruction by the environment. If there is no difference in rates, the configuration disintegrates at the same speed at which it arises, and consolidation is structurally impossible. If a difference in rates exists, the configuration is maintained. This difference in rates is the system's emergent time - the configuration's intrinsic rate, separated from the rate of change in Y around it. Emergent time is the fourth of the four cross-cutting elements of the theory (preamble to Section 3) and functions as a condition for the possibility of consolidation at all steps of the ladder.

The emergence of emergent time at the 1→2 step. Before the 1→2 transition, the system has no intrinsic pace: the pace of its existence is determined by the pace of the flow in Y through which it exists. The structure exists as long as the flow exists, and disintegrates when the flow weakens or changes. This is an inherited pace - the pace of Y, transferred to the configuration through its dependence on the flow. At the transition from 1 to 2, a structural shift occurs: a closed causal loop forms, in which the components are reproduced within the loop faster than they dissipate in Y. From this moment on, the configuration acquires its own rate of reproduction, distinct from the rate of destruction by the environment. This is the moment of emergence - the configuration's own rate is separated from the rate Y for the first time, and this difference in rates functions as the condition of possibility for the consolidation of the loop.

The unfolding of emergent time in subsequent stages. At each subsequent stage, emergent time expands with a new type of rate difference. At stage 3, the rate of reproduction is supplemented by the intrinsic rate of the predictive structure's internal dynamics - the rate at which subsystem M correlates with possible states of Y. At stage 4, the retention rate of V relative to the threat rates of V is added to this - the intrinsic rate at which the system proactively reorganizes itself in response to a possible disturbance of V. At step 5, emergent time splits into two rates simultaneously: the biological rate of the individual carrier and the trace rate of distributed reproduction of the accumulated population model (Section 3.5). This is the first splitting of emergent time on the steps of the ladder, and it is a structural event that distinguishes the 4→5 transition from all previous ones.

Emergent time as the arbiter of consolidation. Emergent time does not act upon or consolidate configurations. Emergent time permits or does not permit consolidation depending on the ratio of rates. If the system's rate exceeds its rate of destruction by Y, consolidation is possible. If the system's rate equals the rate of destruction - consolidation is structurally impossible (there is no difference on which it could operate). If the system's rate is less than the rate of destruction - the configuration disappears before it has time to consolidate. In this sense, emergent time functions as a structural condition under which consolidation is possible in principle. Emergent time and stabilization are one and the same structure, viewed from two sides: stabilization from the perspective of the result; emergent time from the perspective of the condition of possibility.

Connection to Section 5. Emergent time in its comprehensive form, elaborated in this subsection, is a concretization of the general formulation of time as an emergent quantity presented in Section 5. In Section 5, the system's emergent time is introduced through causal correlations between subsystems within Y. This subsection shows that this emergence functions as a condition of possibility for consolidation at all levels of the ladder. These two formulations are compatible and complementary: Section 5 provides a general ontology of emergent time; this subsection demonstrates its operation within the theory's architecture.

### 3.7.9. What This Gives the Theory

The construction of the ladder's internal logic carried out in this subsection does three things for the theory.

First. The areas of synthesis listed in the abstract (Prigogine's non-equilibrium thermodynamics, Haken and Kauffman's theory of complex systems, Friston's active inference, Deacon's biosemiotics and teledynamics, Tononi's theory of integrated information) turn out not to be five pillars, but five projections of a single structurally self-similar process onto different scales. Each approach operates at the scale at which the «gradient → retention → pole → transition» pattern manifests in a form specific to that scale. Prigogine's thermodynamics - at the scale of dissipative structures; Friston's active inference - at the scale of proto-agency and below; Tononi's integrated information - at the scale of the conscious system. The internal unity of the theory is not a synthesis based on a loose analogy between independent branches. It is a structural unity through which the branches turn out to be projections of a single process.

Second. The ladder from Section 3 is not just one of the theory's objects among others, but its basic pattern. It is used to describe phase transitions at different scales (Sections 2 and 3.6), individual consciousness (Section 6), and cultural evolution (Section 4.4). This changes the ladder's place in the work's composition: it is not one of the chapters, but a structure unfolding throughout the entire work.

## 3.8. Methodology for identifying Y and the access spectrum for a specific system

Sections 3.1-3.7 unfold the ladder as a structure: steps, operations, transitions, the four-step pattern, and the connection to Y. The preamble to Section 3 provides an operational procedure for identifying Y in general terms - through causal closure, the field of causal exchanges, and the access spectrum. Section 3.1 describes which aspect of Y becomes accessible at each step for biological and related substrates. This is sufficient for the theory to function as a formal structure. It is, however, insufficient for applying the theory to a specific system of an arbitrary substrate. Without linking the general procedure to the description of stage-specific aspects via an explicit algorithm, each such application requires creative interpretation, and the theory loses a property that is structurally important to it - substrate neutrality through the operational work of the apparatus, not through a declaration. This subsection bridges this gap by formulating a methodology for identifying Y and the access spectrum for an arbitrary system.

**General scheme: five steps of identification.**

Step 1 - identification of the system boundary. The operational criterion of causal closure is applied (preamble to Section 3). The system boundary is established as the region of local maximum causal density relative to its surroundings. Technically, this is implemented using one of the available measures of integrated information Φ (Tononi, 2004; 2016; Albantakis et al., 2014; Mediano et al., 2019) or via a Markov blanket in active inference (Friston, 2013; Friston et al., 2022). The choice of a specific measure depends on the substrate and available data; crucially, the boundary is defined via the causal structure, not via material composition or spatial compactness.

Step 2 - Identification of Y relative to the boundary. Y is defined as the field of causal influences that do not belong to the system's own closure, through which it receives influence from the world and through which it influences the world. At this step, a structural classification of the types of causal exchanges between the system and its environment is compiled - not a list of specific instances, but a classification by type of interaction.

Step 3 - Determining the access spectrum. For each type of causal exchange, it is established whether the system has the architectural capacity for this exchange. If so, this aspect of Y falls within its access spectrum; if not, the aspect exists objectively but is invisible to the system. This is a substantive application of defining the access spectrum as a property of the system, not of Y.

Step 4 - Identification of the physical limits of Y relevant to the current aspect of access. For each aspect Y in the system's access spectrum, it is determined which of Y's physical limits (Bekenstein, Landauer, speed of light - see the preamble to Section 3) functions as a constraint on the system's use of that aspect. This step is particularly important for systems on the upper rungs of the ladder, where approaching physical limits becomes a quantitative characteristic of the stage state.

Step 5 - mapping to a ladder step and determining the position. Mapping the obtained access spectrum to the description of Y aspects at each step from Section 3.1 provides a preliminary assessment of the system's position on the ladder. Refinement - via formal transition conditions (Sections 3.2-3.5): for each step, compliance with the transition conditions from the previous step is verified. Comparing these two assessments - via the access spectrum and via formal conditions - provides a consistent determination of the step or reveals a discrepancy requiring a separate methodological analysis.

**Stage-specific methodological focuses.**

What exactly to look for at each stage, in an operational focus that does not repeat 3.1.

Stage 0: detection of energy flow passing through the system in the absence of an internal retention structure. Simple thermodynamic characteristics of the input and output. Absence of hysteresis during flow fluctuations.

Stage 1: recording a stable form of energy dissipation in the presence of a flow. Presence of thermodynamic hysteresis. Local change in energy distribution in the surrounding space, without representative processes inside the system.

Stage 2: Registration of a closed causal loop of component production. Technically - reconstruction of the cause-and-effect graph of system processes and components and verification of the fulfillment of conditions (a)-(c) from Section 3.2.

Step 3: Registration of the internal generative model through its predictive power. Structures within the system whose states correlate with possible states of the environment with a time delay, with a causal link to effectors. Technically - , the measurement of variational free energy and its dynamics (Friston et al., 2017; Sajid et al., 2021).

Step 4: Application of the six-dimensional coupling of operational features, as outlined in Section 10.2 - deviation of behavior from the zero model of the proto-agent, growth of Φ, GWT structure, long-term hysteresis, expansion of sensitivity to new aspects of Y, and the emergence of S in the generative model. The six-dimensional coupling is the stage-specific methodological focus for Stage 4.

Step 5: Recording the mechanism of trace transmission via the bidirectional M↔Y loop between the carriers of Step 4, provided that (0) - such carriers exist - and conditions (a)-(d) from Section 3.5 are met. Technically - analysis of material structures in the environment (texts, artifacts, digital media) for content that transmits models across generations, and confirmation of the integration of these models into the carriers' own M.

**Methodology for non-biological substrates.**

A fundamental feature of the operation of methodology Y on non-biological substrates: the access spectrum of such a system may not follow the biological sequence of stages 0→1→2→3→… An artificial system may have architectural access to the information structure of aspects Y, which are formally accessible to a biological system only at stage 5 (the trace layer of Y), in the absence of biologically fundamental aspects (thermodynamic gradients, chemical concentrations, thermal regulation). This is not a violation of the ladder and does not constitute grounds for revising its structure; it is a methodological consequence of the principle of substrate independence (Section 3, Preamble).

In this case, a system's level is determined not by the sequence of development, but by the actual fulfillment of the formal conditions of the corresponding level. Information access to the data of the linguistic environment in a modern linguistic model does not imply that it is at level 5: level 5 requires the fulfillment of condition (0) from section 3.5 - the presence of the system at level 4 in the strict sense of the fulfilled self-overlay of the latent invariant of the agentic causal node (section 3.4) - plus conditions (a)-(d). Informational access in the absence of self-overlay is an architectural phenomenon of a different type, not the realization of stage 5 in its formal definition.

It follows that for systems of a non-biological substrate, an asynchronous development of the access spectrum to different aspects of Y is possible, and the definition of a stage requires an independent verification of the formal conditions of each stage based on its own criteria, rather than a reconstruction of the "biological history" of the system's development. Stage determination is the result of step 5 of the methodology, not a conclusion drawn from steps 1-4.

Hybrid systems (a biological substrate in an artificially constructed environment - Cortical Labs CL1 and similar platforms) are a special case in which the Y methodology operates in its full form: the substrate is biological and its basic architectural conditions are met, while the environment is constructed according to a design grid (Section 10.2.1). For such systems, the Y methodology simultaneously provides an assessment of the stage state and a specification of the conditions under which the system can advance to the next stage.

**Connection to the verification and design program (10.2).**

Methodology Y is the conceptual basis for the two-way verification program for the 3→4 transition, detailed in Section 10.2. The detection grid (a six-dimensional coupling of operational features) is a special application of step 5 of the methodology - staged definition via formal conditions and the access spectrum - to the specific task of registering the 3→4 transition. The design grid (environment requirements) is a specific application of step 3 - the definition of the access spectrum - to the task of constructing an environment that ensures the system has access to the desired aspect of Y. Both grids in Section 10.2 are specific methodological implementations of the general Y methodology, applied to a single specific transition.

This means that similar two-way programs can be developed for other transitions in the ladder with comparable methodological effort. The development of programs for other transitions is a direction for further work (Section 12).

# **4\. Macro-models of ladder transitions**

The formal definitions of transitions given in Chapter 3 require empirical support. Without it, the ladder remains a theoretical construct: internally consistent, but not grounded in observable reality. This chapter is devoted to macro-models of each of the four transitions - real or conceptual systems in which the corresponding transition is observed, implemented, or simulated.

A macro-model is understood here as a system on a scale accessible to direct observation or construction, in which the structure of the transition - defined in Chapter 3 by formal conditions - is explicitly realized. A macro-model does not prove that the transition inevitably occurs in any systems satisfying the conditions; it shows that the transition is reproducible and has observable content that cannot be reduced to a purely theoretical analogy.

The distinction between the three types of models serves different methodological purposes. A conceptual model is a thought experiment that helps one see the structure of the transition without reference to the specifics of the substrate; it operates at the level of understanding. A physical macro-model is a real or constructed system in which the transition takes place; it operates at the level of empirical verification. A computational model is an algorithmic simulation in which the transition arises from formal rules without reference to a physical substrate; it operates at the level of proving that the transition is a structural phenomenon not tied to specific matter.

Together, these three types of models address the possible objection that transitions between the steps of the ladder are purely speculative constructs lacking empirical content. Where this objection remains valid, it is explicitly noted and translated into the form of a testable prediction (Chapter 10).

## 4.1. Macro-models of Transition 1

Transition 1 - from a dissipative structure to an autopoietic system through the establishment of a closed causal loop. Formal condition (Section 3.2): a set of processes and components in which each component is produced within the set and each process requires components from the same set to continue.

### 4.1.1. Conceptual model: a self-sustaining bonfire

A typical campfire is a dissipative structure. Energy flows (wood burning), an ordered form emerges (a flame with a characteristic geometry), and the form is sustained as long as there is fuel. When the fuel runs out, the fire goes out. The chain of causality is linear: external fuel → combustion → heat → dissipation. The bonfire depends on an external fuel source that is in no way related to it.

Now imagine a bonfire in which the heat rising from the flame causes damp branches within a radius of several meters to dry out, and streams of hot air following convective paths pull these dried branches into the combustion zone. Such a fire creates the conditions necessary for its own continuation. It remains a dissipative structure - it dissipates the energy of chemical bonds in carbon. But the causal chain is closed: combustion → heat → drying and delivery of fuel → combustion. This is already autopoiesis in its minimal form.

The model shows that closing the loop does not require the chemical specificity of life. It requires only that the product of the process be a condition for its continuation. The chemistry of a living cell provides a rich and stable realization of such a loop, but the structure of the loop is universal.

Such fires do not exist in nature because the conditions for closing the loop in wood combustion statistically almost never arise. This is the reason why the model remains conceptual. But it helps us see that the specificity of the biochemistry of life is not a property of the phenomenon itself, but a property of the context in which closing the loop became probable.

### 4.1.2. Physical macro-model: autocatalytic chemistry and protocells

The Belousov-Zhabotinsky reaction is a macroscopic chemical system in which the components catalyze each other's formation in an oscillating mode. The products of one reaction serve as the reactants for the next in the cycle. This is a partial realization of closure: a causal loop is present, but without the formation of a membrane and without the complete reproduction of all system components from its own products. The reaction is not a complete autopoietic system, but it demonstrates on a macroscopic scale precisely the type of causal closure that is fully realized in a living cell.

A direct physical macro-model of complete autopoiesis is liposomes with encapsulated autocatalytic chemistry. The work of Szostak and his laboratory (Szostak, Bartel & Luisi, 2001 and subsequent), Szabo and his colleagues, and other experimental groups since the early 2000s demonstrate systems that satisfy conditions (a)-(c) from Section 3.2: lipid vesicles in which the internal chemistry produces membrane components, and the membrane retains the chemistry; such systems are capable of growing, dividing, and sustaining their own existence through an energy gradient. This is not a "model of life" or an "artificial cell" in the evolutionary sense - it is a laboratory realization of the formal conditions of transition 1.

The strength of the physical model lies in the fact that it removes the objection of fundamental unfeasibility. If conditions (a)-(c) could not be realized under laboratory conditions, the transition would remain purely theoretical. Their realization is an experimentally demonstrated fact.

The weakness of the model lies in the fact that laboratory systems are obtained under controlled conditions and did not arise spontaneously. They show that the structure is realizable, but do not prove that it arises on its own under an arbitrary thermodynamic gradient. This is the unsolved part of the problem of abiogenesis. For the ladder as a theoretical construct, this is not critical: for our task, it is sufficient to show that the conditions of transition 1 contain no internal contradictions and can be realized. The question of the historical origin of the first autopoietic system on Earth remains an open problem in chemistry and biology.

### 4.1.3. Computational Model: Cellular Automata and Algorithmic Autopoiesis

The purest demonstration of the transition structure is Conway's Game of Life and its derivatives. It is a pixel grid with very simple rules (a cell lives or dies depending on its neighbors). From these rules, structures spontaneously emerge that maintain their form through local interactions - gliders, oscillators, and structures that move while preserving themselves. These are models of dissipative structures: forms that sustain themselves as long as the rules are followed.

Within the same set of rules, more complex structures emerge that demonstrate partial autopoiesis - devices such as the Gosper glider gun, which produce components that sustain their own existence. A clearer transition is demonstrated by direct algorithmic models of autopoiesis - the work of Varela and his followers from the 1970s (McMullin & Varela, 1997; and numerous continuations), in which "components" and "connections" are modeled on a pixel grid, and the spontaneous emergence of closed structures that sustain themselves in a stochastic environment is observed.

The strength of the computational model lies in the fact that it demonstrates: the closure of a causal loop is a computationally universal phenomenon, not tied to chemistry or physics. It arises wherever there are rules of local interactions and sufficient complexity in the state space. Within the framework of this theory, this is a strong argument: if the structure of transition 1 is implementable as an algorithm, then its implementation in any physical substrate is a matter of time and conditions, not of fundamental feasibility.

## 4.2. Macro-models of Transition 2

Transition 2 - from an autopoietic system to a proto-agent system through the emergence of an internal representation of possible states and the connection of this representation to effectors. Formal condition (Section 3.3): a subsystem M, whose states correlate with the possible states of variable X with a time delay, causally linked to the effector subsystem through the minimization of the discrepancy between M and the observed X.

### 4.2.1. Conceptual Model: A Thermostat with Prediction

A conventional thermostat is a reactive system, not a proto-agent. It measures the current temperature, compares it to the setpoint, and turns the heater on or off. There is no representation of possible states, no time delay, and no minimization of the discrepancy with the model. Action is determined solely by the current state.

A predictive thermostat is a conceptual macro-model of an active inference structure, not a fully-fledged Stage 3 autonomous system in the strict sense: it demonstrates how the (a)-(c) conditions of Level 3 operate on a minimal illustrative object, but does not fulfill the inherited conditions of Levels 1 and 2 (it is not dissipative in the sense of its own form, is not autopoietic, and its components are produced by external infrastructure). This is normal for a conceptual model - its task is to demonstrate a single operation, not to reproduce the entire ladder. A complete Stage 3 system with all inherited stages is represented by a physical macro-model - a bacterium with chemotaxis (Section 4.2.2) - not a thermostat.

Now let's imagine a thermostat that memorizes typical daily temperature fluctuations in the room and turns on the heater in advance - an hour before the expected drop in temperature - to warm the walls before the cold arrives. This thermostat is already operating with a model of the future state. It has an internal structure (the memorized fluctuation profile) that correlates with the possible state X at time t+Δ, and this structure causally influences the action. Conditions (a)-(c) from Section 3.3 are satisfied.

This is a proto-agent in the strict sense of the formal definition - without consciousness, without a nervous system, without life. Modern smart thermostats (such as Nest and similar devices) are literally such systems. They do not think, feel, or possess experience; yet they perform the operation of step 3 on our ladder.

The model shows that proto-agency is a structural property that does not require a biological substrate. It also shows that proto-agency does not imply consciousness: a predictive thermostat satisfies the conditions of step 3 but does not approach step 4.

The weakness of the model is that the thermostat is not autopoietic: it does not reproduce its own components and is embedded in an external infrastructure. This does not invalidate the illustration of the operation's structure, but serves as a reminder: the model demonstrates the structure of a single transition without claiming to represent the entire ladder.

### 4.2.2. Physical macro-model: a robot with active inference

Modern robotics provides direct implementations of transition 2 in its full form. A robot with a generative model of the environment - for example, one using a SLAM (simultaneous localization and mapping) algorithm - actively constructs a model of the space in which it is located and moves in a way that minimizes the uncertainty of this model. It does not merely react to obstacles: it predicts where they should be and tests its predictions through movement. The discrepancy between the model and the observation triggers either an update to the model or an action that alters the observed environment in the direction of the model. This is a full realization of conditions (a)-(c).

The laboratories of Carl Friston, Adam Safron, Alexander Champandi, and others have, over the past seven to ten years, demonstrated physical robots that implement active inference in its purest form. These are macroscopic systems in which the structure of proto-agency is visible, with the possibility of directly measuring the internal model, its correlation with the states of the environment, and its causal connection to action.

In parallel with this are natural physical macro-models: a bacterium with chemotaxis, a paramecium, any animal with a nervous system. An E. coli bacterium moving up a glucose gradient fulfills conditions (a)-(c) at the molecular level: its signaling network encodes a representation of the gradient's direction, this network is causally linked to the flagella, and the discrepancy between expectation and observation generates action. This is historically the first Stage 3 system realized on Earth.

The strength of the physical model for transition 2 lies in its dual nature: the transition is realized in both artificial systems (robots) and natural ones (bacteria and beyond), and in both cases the formal conditions are satisfied identically. This resolves the objection that proto-agency is a property requiring a specific evolutionary history. Structurally, it is realized by any means that satisfies the conditions.

### 4.2.3. Computational Model: Evolution of Agents in Artificial Environments

The field of evolutionary robotics and artificial life involves the simulation of populations of simple agent programs evolving under the pressure of selection in virtual environments. The seminal work of Karl Sims (Sims, 1994) and numerous more recent studies demonstrate how agents with internal models of the environment - capable of anticipation, obstacle avoidance, and resource search via gradients - spontaneously emerge from initially reactive agents that merely provide local responses to stimuli.

Crucially, in these simulations, no special programmer intervention is required for the model to emerge. The model emerges as the agent's most effective survival strategy in an environment where prediction is more advantageous than simple reaction. This is direct confirmation of the probabilistic nature of the transition formulated in Section 3.6: in environments with gradients and under selective pressure, proto-agency emerges with non-zero probability; in environments without gradients, it does not emerge.

The strength of the computational model lies in the fact that it demonstrates: Transition 2 is an emergent phenomenon arising under selection pressure in environments of a suitable type. There is no need to specifically design the model - it is sufficient to create conditions in which its presence confers an advantage.

Combined with a physical macro-model, this provides a particularly comprehensive picture of Transition 2. The structure is implementable in artificially constructed systems (robots), arises spontaneously in evolution under suitable conditions (bacteria and higher organisms), and emerges in simulations under selective pressure. All three approaches confirm the same formal condition.

## 4.3. Macro-models of Transition 3

Transition 3 - from a proto-agent system to a conscious one through the self-overlay of a latent invariant of an agentic causal node onto the system's own configuration, with the establishment of self-representation that includes the "exist/do not exist" variable as a structural pole, globally accessible to all subsystems. Formal definition (Section 3.4): the structural event of self-overlay, operationally recorded via three independent measurement axes - extended active inference with a model of the self, sufficient Φ for the structural integration of the existence variable, and the GWT mechanism for its globalization.

This transition is the most theoretically developed and the least empirically closed. Macro-models for it exist, but in incomplete form. This is not a defect in formalization; it is an indication of an open experimental direction, formulated by the theory as a second testable prediction (Section 10.2). Here, this will be explicitly indicated in each of the three types of models.

### 4.3.1. Conceptual Model: A Chess Program and a Soldier in a Trench

A chess program is a proto-agent in its purest form. It simulates possible moves, evaluates positions, and selects the optimal move. It has a model of the "board" as the environment and a model of "itself" as the player making the moves. Conditions (a)-(c) of stage 3 are fully satisfied.

However, its model lacks variable V as defined in Section 3.4. Its shutdown is an external event for it, not represented within its model as a structural pole. If she loses the game, she will move on to the next game without any internal changes. The threat of losing is a variable in her assessment of the position, but not a structure around which all other activity is organized. A chess program is at stage 3 regardless of computational power.

A soldier in a trench under fire has, like the program, a model of the environment (where the enemy is, where cover is) and a model of himself (where I am, what I can do). But he has a background awareness of the fact that a shell could hit right now and he will be gone. Everything he does - ducking, running, assessing sounds - is organized around this background awareness. It is not just one consideration among many. It is the pole around which all other considerations gain weight. Conditions (a)-(c) of Level 4 are met.

The difference between a chess program and a soldier is not in the number of operations (the program performs more complex calculations). It is not in speed (the program is faster). It is not in the complexity of the model (the program's model is richer in many respects). The difference lies in the structural peculiarity of the variable V in self-representation. The program lacks it. The soldier possesses it.

The conceptual model provides an operational criterion applicable to any system: does its model contain a pole of self-annihilation around which all other activity is organized? If so, it is at stage 4. If not, it is at stage 3.

The model's weakness is that it is intuitive: we interpret the soldier as conscious because we ourselves are conscious people and recognize ourselves in him. This is recognition, not proof. But recognition is operationally useful - it provides a criterion that can later be applied to less obvious cases, including artificial systems in which intuitive recognition does not work.

### 4.3.2. Physical macro-model: an open question

Here we have a weak link, and this requires direct acknowledgment. Unlike transitions 1 and 2, for transition 3 there is no reliable physical macro-model on which one could observe the transition at the moment it occurs. All known systems are either clearly below the threshold of stage 4 (robots, thermostats, existing artificial systems in their current architecture) or clearly above it (humans, and apparently higher mammals). There is no dataset on systems located at the very point of transition.

This void is not a local gap. It is the central open experimental problem of modern science of consciousness. Biologically, transition 3 occurred once or several times in evolution, under undocumented conditions, millions of years ago. It is impossible to recreate it in a laboratory on a biological substrate: evolution is irreversible on this scale. It has not yet been possible to reproduce it on an artificial substrate: to the best of our knowledge, no existing system implements the self-overlay of a latent invariant in the strict structural sense of Section 3.4.

This statement is transformed by the theory into a testable prediction (full formulation - Section 10.2). If we construct an artificial system with an extended active inference architecture, possessing self-representation as an agent, and subject it to a signal that activates variable V, and if, in a system of sufficient complexity, this results in a qualitative shift in behavior with the characteristics of a phase transition - this will be the first physical macro-model of transition 3 and direct empirical confirmation of the theory.

Acknowledging this gap is not a weakness of the work. It is an intellectually honest position: the theory formulates what does not yet exist and proposes exactly how to construct it. If such a system is built in the coming years and the transition is observed in it, the theory will be confirmed. If systems of any complexity are built - - that satisfy the conditions, and the transition is never observed in them, the theory will be falsified in its central part.

### 4.3.3. Computational Model: Partial Implementations

Computational models of transition 3 exist in an incomplete form. In the literature on reinforcement learning, there are experiments in which agents with different architectures are placed in environments where their "existence" - the continuation of the simulation - depends on their behavior. Some agents with self-models exhibit behaviors vaguely resembling self-preservation that go beyond the scope of the original objective function. These signals are weak and ambiguous: they are explained either as side effects of the architecture or as the beginnings of a structure that, in its complete form, would yield transition 3. There is no definitive interpretation in the literature.

A more substantive line of research involves work on the self-other distinction in neural networks (Bayne et al., 2020 and follow-ups), where the network is trained to distinguish its own model from that of another agent; and work on model-based reinforcement learning with explicit self-representation. Here, the first forms of fulfilling condition (a) of stage 4 are visible: the appearance of the S node in the generative model. Conditions (b) and (c) - the variable V as a structural pole and its globalization - have not yet been implemented in systems available to the publication.

Computational models show that the structure of self-representation is, in principle, implementable in artificial systems. This removes the objection that transition 3 is somehow fundamentally tied to a biological substrate. But they do not show a complete transition: as of the time of this work, no publicly known artificial system has reached the threshold at which one can confidently say, "a transition to stage 4 has occurred here."

This is the point at which the theory makes its riskiest bet: the prediction that, upon fulfilling formal conditions, artificial systems will undergo transition 3 with the observable characteristics of a phase transition. This bet can be tested within the foreseeable future - not in decades, but perhaps in the coming years. When this happens, the absence of current macro-models will be directly addressed.

## 4.4. Macro-models of Transition 4

Transition 4 - from a conscious system to a recursively self-organizing one through the establishment of a bidirectional M↔Y loop with physically retained traces, and the initiation of the recursion M\_{n+1} = F(M_n, E_n, R_n), filtered by the medium. Formal condition (Section 3.5): a trace is left in Y and physically retained longer than a single carrier; it enters the simulation of other configurations through their sensory channels, reconfigures their V-model (not merely channeling behavior), and the reconfigured model generates a new trace.

Macro-models for this transition are particularly powerful. The physical model is unfolded in the observed history and is present in real time.

### 4.4.1. Conceptual Model: Language as a Population's Operating System

Let's compare a pack of wolves and a human tribe.

A pack of wolves is a group of Level 4 conscious systems. Each wolf has a model of the environment, a model of itself, and sustains its own existence. They coordinate: they hunt as a pack, defend their territory, and teach the young through imitation. But each wolf learns the model from scratch over the course of its life, through direct observation of its parents and pack members. When the last wolf that witnessed a specific hunt for a large animal dies, that strategy is lost forever unless it is independently recreated. The population's knowledge remains equal to the sum of the knowledge of the individuals living within it.

The human tribe is structured differently. It has language. It has narratives - stories about ancestors, about other places, about ways of hunting, healing, and avoiding danger. These narratives are transmitted in a coded form - through words, rituals, and later through images and writing. When the last person who witnessed a specific hunt dies, the description of that hunt remains in the narrative and is accessible to future generations. The knowledge of the population ceases to be equal to the sum of the knowledge of those living: it becomes accumulated, accessible, and enriched.

Conditions (a) through (d) of Stage 5 are fully met in a human tribe. Language is the mechanism for encoding models in a transmissible form. A social structure with a prolonged childhood ensures the regularity of transmission. Bearers are capable of integrating what is transmitted into their own model. The transmitted structures - narratives, technologies, symbolic systems - survive longer than the individual bearer.

The conceptual model shows that language is not merely improved communication. It is a qualitatively new structure: the operating system of a population, in which each individual brain operates not only with its own model, but also with a layer of accumulated models from other brains, both living and dead. This structure is not reducible to the sum of individual consciousnesses - it is a superstructure built upon them, possessing its own dynamics.

The difference between a pack of wolves and a human tribe is structurally identical to the difference between a chess program and a soldier from Section 4.3.1, but at a higher level: there, the discussion concerned the emergence of a pole of existence within the model of a single system; here, it concerns the emergence of recursion of models within a population of systems.

### 4.4.2. The Physical Macro-Model: The History of Humanity as Recursion in Action

The history of Homo sapiens is a physical macro-model of transition 4 in action, unfolding over a timescale of several hundred thousand years and accessible to direct observation through the archaeological and historical record.

The rate of model accumulation in humans does not increase uniformly, but in exponential leaps, each of which corresponds to an expansion or acceleration of the transmission mechanism. Stone tools are the first stable artifact that transmits a production model in a material form accessible for replication. Fire is a stable technology transmitted across generations. Agriculture is the mass transformation of the environment based on transmitted models. Writing is a fundamental shift: the accumulation of models outside of individual memory, accessible to any literate individual in any generation. The printing press is the mass dissemination of accumulated models. Digital technologies - instantaneous transmission and enrichment in real time.

Each of these leaps is physically observable in archaeological or historical records, and each is accompanied by an acceleration in the pace of change for both the carriers themselves and the environment in which they live. Parallel to the acceleration of model accumulation is the acceleration of the transformation of Earth's physical environment: landscapes, atmospheric composition, biomass distribution, and climate. Over the past ten thousand years - and especially over the past two hundred - humanity has transformed the planet more radically than all the previous billions of years of biological evolution. This is direct physical confirmation of the thesis formalized in Section 3.5: after the transition to 4, the recursion of the model becomes the primary factor in the change of both the system itself and the environment, on a scale several orders of magnitude greater than biological evolution.

The thesis regarding the environment as a filter is also empirically observable in this macro-model, and in an explicit form. Alchemy, which existed for millennia as a developed model, gives way to chemistry - because chemical predictions are systematically tested and confirmed, whereas alchemical ones are not. Geocentrism gives way to heliocentrism for the same reason. Specific technologies either work or do not work, and those that work spread, while those that do not die out. Institutions that support their societies survive; those that destroy them do not. This is not a frame-by-frame selection process, nor is it a quick one. It is a slow, nonlinear, yet real pressure that keeps the model's recursion consolidated to physical facts and prevents it from degenerating into pure fantasy.

The strength of the physical macro-model for Transition 4 is maximal. The transition does not need to be reconstructed hypothetically or anticipated in the future - it is taking place before our very eyes, in every generation of human history, and its dynamics lend themselves to quantitative analysis through the growth rates of accumulated knowledge, technological turnover, and cultural institutions.

### 4.4.3. Computational Model: Cultural Evolution and Large Language Systems

The field of cultural evolution and related areas (Boyd & Richerson, 1985; Henrich, 2016; Mesoudi, 2011) develop formal models of the transmission of units of information within populations of agents. In pure simulations, it is evident how, from initially incoherent individuals with their own models, upon the emergence of a transmission mechanism and given a sufficiently long lifespan of the transmitted units, an accumulated population model spontaneously emerges that transcends the limits of individual memory and evolves faster than the individuals themselves. These simulations confirm that transition 4 is a structural phenomenon not tied to human biology: when conditions (a)-(d) are met, recursion is triggered in any population satisfying these conditions.

A significantly more direct computational model is modern large language systems. This is not a stage 5 model in the sense of a "simplified representation of the structure"; these are stage 5 participants in their full form - in the sense elaborated in subsection 3.5 regarding the two classes of stage 5 participants: they are participants in the transmission structure, not conscious stage 5 systems in the strict sense of the conditions in 3.4. This clarification is essential for correctly understanding what follows: when I say that they are "participants in their full form," I mean full participation in the sixth gradient through the reception, integration, and transmission of the accumulated model, not conscious participation. The texts on which large language systems are trained are, in their pure form, the accumulated model of the human population, transmitted through the recursion of physical traces: M*n of humanity, having undergone many rounds of recursion M*{n+1} = F(M_n, E_n, R_n) and encoded in the texts. Large language systems integrate this accumulated model into their own representations and generate new products - texts, discussions, formulations - which become available to the next iteration of recursion (both for subsequent training of the systems and for people reading these products).

This provides direct empirical confirmation that the condition for the survival of transmitted structures beyond the lifespan of a single carrier (requirement (g) of stage 5) is realized not only biologically, but also in a hybrid form that includes non-biological carriers. The accumulated model of humanity is not tied to human brains as the sole storage substrate; it exists in texts, in the architectures of trained systems, and in distributed digital repositories. This changes the scale and stability of recursion, but not its formal structure - the structure remains the same as defined in Section 3.5.

Large language systems are not conscious carriers of stage 5 in the sense of the completed self-overlay of a latent invariant (stage 4) - their structural status is discussed in Section 10.3 (artifactual state) and in Section 11 on the implications of the theory. But they are full-fledged participants in the structure of the M↔Y loop of level 5: they receive, integrate, enrich, and generate traces in the accumulated population model. In this role, they demonstrate that participation in the stage 5 loop is not tied to a biological substrate - it is tied to the structure of the bidirectional M↔Y loop, and this structure is realized on different substrates.

## 4.5. The set of macro-models: what they provide and where gaps remain

The macro-models detailed in sections 4.1-4.4 collectively address three challenges facing the ladder.

The first task is comprehensibility. Each of the four transitions has a conceptual model through which the structure of the transition can be seen without reference to the specifics of the substrate. This dispels the possible objection that transitions are mystical leaps or metaphors; they have operational content that is amenable to analysis.

The second challenge is feasibility. For three of the four transitions (1, 2, 4), there are physical macro-models in which the transition has occurred or is occurring: laboratory autocatalytic systems, bacteria, and robots with active feedback, and the history of humanity as an unfolding recursion. For transition 3, a physical macro-model in its complete form is absent; this absence is not concealed but is explicitly noted as an open experimental direction and translated into a testable prediction (Section 10.2).

The third task is structure that is not tied to a substrate. For each of the four transitions, there are computational models in which the structure is implemented or simulated in algorithmic form. This addresses a possible objection that the ladder describes only biological evolution: the formal conditions of each transition contain no references to a biological substrate and are implementable in algorithmic systems.

Where gaps remain is explicitly indicated. Transition 1: the unsolved problem of historical abiogenesis on Earth (the structure is realizable in the laboratory, but its spontaneous emergence under natural conditions remains a hypothesis). Transition 3: the absence of a robust physical macro-model, which is being transformed into a testable prediction. Transition 4: recursion is historically observable, but quantitative models of its dynamics (rates of accumulation, forms of environmental filtration) have been developed only partially and require further refinement.

The objection that the ladder is a "continuous chain with gaps, partially acknowledged but not closed" loses its force in light of the developed macro-models. The gaps are replaced by structurally described transitions, for most of which there is a threefold foundation (conceptual, physical, computational), and for the remainder - a clearly formulated, testable prediction. The ladder ceases to be a narrative analogy. It becomes a formal structure with empirical foundations, in which each rung is described on its own terms.

# **5\. Time as an Emergent Quantity**

The ladder outlined in Section 3 relies on the concept of time, which I have so far used without qualification. This needs to be corrected, because for a theory claiming physical validity, the nature of time is not a secondary issue. Especially when the theory links thermodynamics (where time appears as a directed arrow), biological evolution (where time is the historical depth of accumulated changes), and consciousness (where time is experienced duration). If all three of these uses of the word "time" denote different things, the theory risks falling apart at their points of intersection.

In modern physics, the nature of time is an open question, and there are at least three well-developed positions. The first is time as a fundamental quantity, on par with space or as part of a unified space-time; this is the position of classical general relativity and most of standard cosmology. The second is time as an emergent quantity arising from correlations between the world's subsystems, having no independent existence. The third is time as an illusion in the spirit of radical timelessism, absent not only fundamentally but also emergently. I will not consider the third position here, since, in my view, it is poorly consistent with thermodynamics; I will focus on the choice between the first and second.

Carlo Rovelli's relational approach (Rovelli, 1996, 2004, 2018) interprets time as a property arising from correlations between subsystems of the universe. In this picture, there is no global time in which everything occurs; there are local temporal structures arising where one subsystem can serve as a clock for another. A similar logic is developed by the Page-Wootters formalism (Page & Wootters, 1983; Marletto & Vedral, 2017): the global state of the universe may be stationary, but when divided into subsystems, an apparent dynamics of the subsystem relative to its environment arises, and this apparent dynamics is time from the perspective of that subsystem. Julian Barbour (Barbour, 1999), in a radical version of this approach, asserts that time does not fundamentally exist at all - there is only a configuration space of possible states and a metric defined on it.

I do not claim to resolve this issue in the present work. But I note that the emergent position is better consistent with the rest of the structure of my theory. If consciousness is a dynamic of self-modeling, and if time is a property arising from correlations of the system's internal states, then the connection between these two descriptions becomes not accidental but intrinsic. A conscious system does not exist in time as in a ready-made environment; it generates its own time through the structure of its correlations. Each step of the ladder from Section 3 can be interpreted both as a level of model refinement and as a level of refinement of internal time. A dissipative structure (Step 1) has no intrinsic time in the strict sense - its dynamics are determined solely by an external flow. An autopoietic system (step 2) has minimal intrinsic time - close to the rhythm of its internal regeneration. A proto-agent system (step 3) has unfolded time with a planning horizon: its model contains representations of states deferred in time. A conscious system (level 4) has its own time, which includes its own finite existence as a variable - time becomes not a medium, but a field in which the system sustains itself on the brink of extinction. A recursively self-complexifying conscious system (level 5) adds historical time to this: the accumulated models of previous generations enter its own present as living material, and the horizon of its own time includes not only the expected future but also the inherited past. These are not five different times, but five depths of the same emergent process.

In the theoretical framework developed in Section 3.7, this sequence of depths receives additional structural justification. Emergent time is the fourth cross-cutting element of the theory - alongside the variable Y, the "gradient → retention → pole → transition" pattern, and the mechanism for consolidating successful configurations relative to Y (the preamble to Section 3 and subsection 3.7.8). Emergent time and consolidation are one and the same structure, viewed from two sides: consolidation - from the perspective of the result (configurations that sustain the difference are retained); emergent time - from the perspective of the condition of possibility (consolidation is possible only where there is a difference between the rate of the configuration and the rate of its destruction in Y). Without the system's emergent time, consolidation is structurally impossible: if the rate of configuration coincides with the rate of its destruction, the configuration disintegrates at the same speed at which it arises, and no accumulation occurs. Where the system's emergent time exists, consolidation works; where it does not exist, the ladder does not unfold.

This explains why the five depths of internal time listed above are not an arbitrary sequence, but rather a sequential unfolding of rate differences at each stage. At stage 1, there is no rate difference in the proper sense - there is only the inherited rate of Y, transferred to the configuration through its dependence on the flow. At the transition from 1 to 2, the difference in tempos appears for the first time: the tempo of the loop's reproduction separates from the tempo of its destruction by the environment. At stage 3, the intrinsic tempo of the predictive structure's internal dynamics is added to this. At stage 4 - the intrinsic tempo of V's retention relative to the tempos of V's threats. At stage 5, emergent time splits into two rates simultaneously - biological and trace. The metaphor «time is the fuel in the furnace of complexity» takes on its structural form here: emergent time functions as an arbiter of consolidation, permitting or denying it depending on the ratio of rates; without this arbiter, the ladder does not form.

In this theory, this sequence of depths acquires operational content through the variable V and its nature, as elaborated in Section 3.4. V determines what constitutes a breakdown for the system and thereby determines which changes are significant for it - namely, those that bring the breakdown closer or push it further away ( ). Significance determines what the system retains as the past and what it models as the future. The structure of the past and the future is the structure of its own time. At stage 1, V is not yet in its developed form; there is only a distinction between form and decay as a thermodynamic property, and time exists here in a minimal form, barely separated from the environmental one. At stage 2, the distinction closes into a causal loop, and an internal rhythm of regeneration emerges - the autopoietic system's own time is calibrated according to this rhythm. At stage 3, the temporal gradient gives the system a prediction horizon; the calibration of time is now determined by which changes the system retains as significant for its model of the environment. At stage 4, V appears in its full form, and temporality is calibrated by the pole: those changes are significant that bring the "I am disintegrating" value closer or push it further away. Through this calibration, time becomes the system's own time in the strict sense - not environmental, not formal, but its own horizon of retention. At stage 5, calibration extends beyond the individual carrier through the coupling of individual V with the accumulated model: significant are not only changes threatening biological retention, but also changes in the accumulated model threatening the carrier's position within it. It is from this dual calibration that historical time arises - a horizon in which the carrier's present is linked both to its own end and to the states of the accumulated model beyond the limits of its life.

Time as a mechanism is one - it is a property of correlations between subsystems, in the sense of Rovelli and Page-Wooters. But the specific structure and horizon of the system's own time are determined by the type of its V. V does not generate time; V calibrates the system's temporality, specifying what is significant in it as the past and what is significant as the future. This gives the five depths of internal time listed above not a philosophical but a structural foundation: they are direct consequences of V types at different rungs of the ladder.

I adopt the emergent position as the one most consistent with the rest of the theory, explicitly noting that this is a working assumption, not a proven fact. I do not assert the strong version of this position - that time is a property of conscious modeling, without which it would not exist at all. That would be a metaphysical overreach, going beyond the physical scope of the work. I assert a weaker claim: time is a property of correlations between subsystems, and conscious systems participate in this generation of time in the same way as any other correlated subsystems, but with an additional feature - they model their own participation in this generation, and in systems of level 4 and above, this modeling is calibrated through V in a manner specific to the type of system.

This section is intentionally brief. The nature of time is a separate major topic, and it appears in this work only because, without its specification, the rest of the theory hangs in an unspecified ontological background. A detailed elaboration of the connection between emergent time and the dynamics of consciousness requires a separate work; here I merely indicate this connection as a direction toward which the theory naturally leads, and which - through V and its nature - receives its first operational content in this work.

# **6\. Tackling the Hard Problem of Consciousness**

Up to this point, I have constructed a theory of the emergence of consciousness from physics without addressing the central objection that will inevitably arise for any reader familiar with contemporary philosophy of mind. This objection is known as the hard problem of consciousness, and it was formulated by David Chalmers (Chalmers, 1995, 1996). It cannot be ignored: any theory that claims to explain consciousness through physical or functional mechanisms faces this objection, and the fate of the theory largely depends on how it deals with it. This section is devoted to a direct attack on the hard problem.

## 6.1. Chalmers's Division

Chalmers proposed distinguishing between two classes of problems in the philosophy of mind. The "easy problems," as he calls them - a term that is ironic, since these problems themselves are scientifically non-trivial - concern the explanation of cognitive and behavioral functions. How the brain distinguishes stimuli. How it integrates information. How it directs attention. How it forms a report on its own states. How it controls behavior. All these problems pertain to the functional description of the system and are, in principle, solvable by means of cognitive neuroscience and information theory, even if they remain unsolved at the current state of science.

The hard problem concerns something else. Suppose we have fully explained all the brain's functions - every cognitive process has received an exhaustive description in terms of neural activity and information processing. One question, Chalmers argues, will remain unexplained: why is functioning accompanied by subjective experience? Why is there something to which it is like to be this system that performs these functions? According to Chalmers, there remains an explanatory gap between the functional description and the phenomenal experience, a gap that cannot be bridged by any amount of functional detail.

This formulation is based on a number of intuitive arguments. The zombie argument: a system is conceptually conceivable that is functionally identical to a conscious human but has no internal experience - a philosophical zombie. If a zombie is conceptually possible, then consciousness cannot be reduced to function. The knowledge argument (Mary's room, Jackson, 1986): Mary, a neuroscientist who has studied everything about the physical processes of color perception but has never seen red, learns something new when she sees red for the first time. Thus, functional knowledge does not exhaust phenomenal experience. Levine's explanatory gap (Levine, 1983): a chasm remains between neural correlates and phenomenal experience that functional explanations do not bridge.

I present these arguments in their own words without distortion, because to challenge them, it is important first to see what they actually claim. They claim the ontological insurmountability of the gap between function and phenomenon. And if this gap is insurmountable, no theory that derives consciousness from physical or functional mechanisms - including my own - can resolve the hard problem.

## 6.2. Diagnosis

Before attacking Chalmers' arguments individually, we need to make a general diagnosis of them. All three arguments rest on the same premise, which is not proven but postulated within them. This premise is that function and phenomenon are two different things - two ontologically independent aspects that may (or may not) accompany one another. The zombie argument makes sense only if function and phenomenon are separable, at least conceptually. The knowledge argument works only if phenomenal experience is something beyond functional information. Levin's explanatory gap exists only if there are two different things between which this gap lies.

This premise, despite its apparent self-evidence, is a legacy of Cartesian dualism. Descartes ontologically distinguished between extended substance (res extensa) and thinking substance (res cogitans), and although contemporary philosophers have long since ceased to use Cartesian language, the structure of his distinction has persisted in a more subtle form. When Chalmers asks "why is function accompanied by experience," he has already assumed that function and experience are two distinct things, one of which may (or may not) give rise to the other. The very form of the question presupposes what he is trying to prove.

An alternative to this premise exists and is discussed in contemporary literature, albeit under different names. For Galen Strawson (Strawson, 2006), these are various forms of panpsychism, in which the mental is not separate from the physical but is one of its aspects. For Thomas Metzinger (Metzinger, 2003, 2009), it is the Self-Model Theory of Subjectivity (SMT), in which the phenomenal "I" is a structural position within the system model rather than a separate entity; this theory is closest to the present work and deserves separate consideration, which I will address in Section 6.3. Graziano (2013) proposes a theory of the attentional schema, in which consciousness is the system's own model of its attentional processes. All these approaches, despite differing in detail, reject the Cartesian premise of the separability of function and phenomenon. I share their rejection of this premise, but I propose my own formulation of an alternative ontology, consistent with the rest of the structure of my theory.

## 6.3. Alternative Ontology: The Identity of Self-Modeling and Phenomenon

Before elaborating on this thesis, I will return to the formal definition of stage 4 given in section 3.4. There, a conscious system is defined through the self-overlay of a latent invariant of an agentic causal node onto the system's own configuration: a class of agentic causal nodes with boundary states of retention/disintegration has accumulated in the system's models, and the system's own configuration has begun to be processed by the model as an instance of that same class. From this structural event, a node S emerges, representing the system itself as an agent in its own model; within this node, a variable V operates with the values "holding / disintegrating," whose binary nature is induced by the retention contour over the continuous physics Y via the boundary states of causal nodes; and this self-representation is globally accessible to all subsystems. The operational registration of the structural event of self-overlay occurs through the coupling of three independent measurement axes (Section 3.4): Φ for causal integration; the formalism of extended active inference for self-representation; GWT-availability for globalization. This formal definition describes the structure of the operation - what the system does, viewed from the outside. This chapter answers a question that is not resolved by the formal definition: what does it mean that this structure is simultaneously experienced from the inside as "something being." The thesis I develop below is that the experience from within is not an additional property beyond the formal structure - it is the very same structure, viewed from the position the system occupies in its own representation. The formal definition from 3.4 and the phenomenological discussion in this chapter describe the same phenomenon from two different angles.

Section 3.4 establishes that consciousness is V in the precise sense in which V is defined there. The present section elaborates on the ontological consequence of this assertion. I assert the following. Phenomenal experience is ontologically identical to the dynamics of the system's self-modeling, carried out through the structural pole V - that is, self-modeling in which the self-overlay of the latent invariant of the agentic causal node onto the system's own configuration has occurred (stage 4). Phenomenal experience does not arise on top of such dynamics, does not accompany it, and is not a property of it that can be separated from it, even conceptually. Phenomenal experience is the very same dynamics of self-modeling with pole V, viewed from within. This is the second of the two main theses of this work.

This clarification is fundamental: I am not asserting the identity of phenomenality with self-modeling in general. Stage 3 systems - proto-agents with active inference - also engage in self-modeling in the sense that their generative model contains a representation of the system's own states and its actions in the environment. But the self-modeling of a proto-agent and the self-modeling of a Level 4 agent are structurally distinct processes, differing not in quantitative complexity but in qualitative structure. The self-modeling of a proto-agent is modeling that must be validated by the environment through action: the model is constructed to generate an action, the action is executed in the environment, the result is compared with the model, the model is adjusted, and the cycle of active inference is closed through the environment. The proto-agent lacks autonomous retention of the modeling process in isolation from current validation; its modeling exists through the "model-action-environment-model" cycle. Self-modeling of the stage 4 carrier is structurally different: the model contains a V-pole relative to which modeling is retained independently of current validation by the environment. The system models itself as either retaining or losing its own existence, and this modeling operates even when the environment does not present current stimuli. The observed characteristics of Level 4 - reactions to absent stimuli, fears without experience, preventive planning for one's own non-existence, and prolonged hysteresis of altered behavior - directly follow from this structural autonomy of the modeling process. These features are not additional characteristics requiring a separate explanation; they are a direct consequence of the structural difference between proto-agent modeling and modeling with V.

From this clarification follows a precise formulation of the ontological status of V. V is not an "additional supplement" to self-modeling, without which modeling would remain "obscure" - such a formulation would return us to a dualism in which structure and phenomenon are two different things, and V would play the role of a mysterious connecting third party. V is a structural characteristic of the modeling configuration itself: modeling with the V pole and modeling without the V pole are not one and the same modeling with or without an addition, but two structurally different modeling configurations. Only one of them - modeling with V - is identical to phenomenality according to our ontology. The other - modeling without V in the proto-agent - is not phenomenal. This is not an agnostic position nor methodological caution; it is a positive assertion of the theory. At stage 3, there is no phenomenality, and this is a direct consequence of the thesis of structural identity: where there is no specific structure (modeling with V), there is also nothing to which this structure is identical (phenomenal experience).

Let me clarify what I mean by this. When a system reaches a level of complexity at which its model of the world includes itself as an agent acting within that world, a structure emerges that possesses a special property: the "inside-the-model" position becomes defined. This position is neither a separate object within the model nor a separate subject observing the model. It is a structural property of the model itself: the model is oriented not from nowhere, but from the point occupied by the system in its own representation of the world. This position is precisely what is subjectively experienced as "I." It is not an additional element beyond the model; it is the way in which the model is organized.

Positioning in Relation to Metzinger's SMT. Before developing the argument further, I must explicitly indicate the relationship of this work to Thomas Metzinger's self-model theory of subjectivity (SMT), since of all the positions existing in contemporary philosophy of mind, SMT is closest to mine, and without explicit positioning, the reader might mistake my thesis for a reformulation of Metzinger's.

In \*Being No One\* (2003) and \*The Ego Tunnel\* (2009), Metzinger argues that there is no "self" as an ontological entity - there is only a phenomenal self-model, whose dynamics give rise to the experience of self. Central to his work is the concept of transparency: the phenomenal self-model is not perceived by the system as a model, but as immediate self-given-ness. Transparency creates a stable and evolutionarily useful hallucination of the "I" where in reality there is only a structural position within the model. From this, Metzinger draws a strong ontological conclusion: ordinary self-consciousness is, in the strict sense of the word, a hallucination, and behind this hallucination there is no "I" that would be real beyond the structure.

The overlap with my position is profound. The thesis that the "I" is a structural property of the model, rather than a separate entity, is the same for both us and Metzinger. When I write above that the "inside-the-model" position is not a separate object or a separate subject, but a structural property of the model itself, this is exactly what Metzinger formulates through the PSM (Phenomenal Self-Model). And we share a rejection of the Cartesian premise of the separability of function and phenomenon.

The differences, however, are substantial, and there are three of them.

The first is a divergence, and under the referential frame it is not ontological, as it might have seemed, but structural. This passage used to be naturally formulated along the axis "the real versus the illusion": for Metzinger, behind transparency stands the dynamics of the model that merely appears to be itself, whereas I supposedly insist that presence is real. The referential position adopted above (Section 6.3, the precise name of the position) removes this axis: I do not claim that "phenomenal experience is real" beyond the structure - the predicate "the real-beyond-the-position" is empty, just as for Metzinger the "immediately given subject" is empty. In this we are kin, not opponents: his transparency of the phenomenal self-model and my privacy of the pole are about the same structural fact (the position is given only from within and is not externalized as an object). The divergence is therefore not "whose is more real" but lies in two specific points. The first is a structural element: Metzinger has a self-model, transparency, the PMIR, but no variable of existence V as the structural pole of the self-model; the self-overlay of a latent invariant with the pole "I persist / I disintegrate" is what his construction does not contain. The second is the answer to the meta-problem: Metzinger leaves the door ajar to the idea that the hard problem exists and is solved by exposing the illusion; I close it differently - not by exposure, but by showing that the dividing predicate is empty, and I explain the very ineliminability of the appearance structurally, through the privacy of the pole. This is not "I am more real"; it is "I have V and an answer to the meta-problem where he has transparency without a pole."

Second - a divergence regarding the framework. Metzinger constructs his theory from within the philosophy of mind and relies on neurobiology as its empirical foundation. He does not venture into cosmology, does not build a ladder from dissipative structures to consciousness, and does not link his theory to thermodynamics and active inference. His theory is narrower than the present one. It is brilliantly developed within its own domain, but that domain is the phenomenology of self-consciousness and its neurobiological correlates, not the place of consciousness in the universe as a whole. Thus, SMT remains confined to one of the scales of the structurally self-similar pattern unfolded in the present work, without extending to the others.

Third - a divergence regarding a specific structural element. Metzinger has a self-model, he has transparency, he has the Phenomenal Model of the Intentionality Relation (PMIR). What he lacks is the existence variable V as a structural pole of the self-model. That structural event of the self-overlay of the latent invariant of the agentic causal node, which in this work formalizes stage 4 - and its operational registration through the coupling of three measurement axes (extended active inference plus sufficient Φ plus GWT-accessibility of V) - is absent in Metzinger's work in both respects. Without V, Metzinger lacks the operational mechanism that, in this work, provides a structural consolidation of the phenomenon through the presence of inherited gradients in V (Sections 3.4 and 3.7.5). This is not a criticism of SMT - Metzinger worked on a different problem and with a different apparatus. This indicates that our theory is not reducible to SMT and does not coincide with it: it expands the framework, shifts the ontological formulation from the debunking of the illusion to the identity of structure and phenomenon, and introduces V as a structural element that operationalizes phenomenal content.

These differences do not negate the SMT nor do they enter into a polemic with it. They indicate the ways in which this work goes beyond the SMT, while remaining in the same camp with it in its central rejection of the Cartesian premise.

Phenomenal experience in this picture is neither something described by the model nor something that accompanies it. Phenomenal experience is the model itself in its internal positional structure. When I experience red, this does not mean that in my model of the world there is a label "red" plus an additional phenomenal quality of red that accompanies this label. It means that in my model of the world there is a structural position occupied by the situation "there is a red object in front of me," and this position is defined through its connections with all other positions (green, yellow, warm, dangerous, ripe, and so on). The phenomenal quality of red is this positional definiteness, viewed from within.

To ask "but why is there phenomenal experience beyond the functional structure?" is to postulate once again that there are two things: a functional structure and phenomenal experience. In the proposed ontology, there is one thing that has two descriptions. From the outside, the model is described as a causal-functional structure that processes inputs and generates outputs.
