From the inside, the same structure is an experience, because "from the inside" there is a position that the structure occupies in its own representation of the world. "What is it like to be" is synonymous with "how the structure relates to itself." These are not two phenomena, but one phenomenon in two descriptions.

The privacy of the pole as the constitutor of "from within," and identity under two aspects. "Two descriptions of one structure" needs a qualification, otherwise it reads as a tautological evasion. What constitutes "from within"? Not a second observer "for whom" - that would reintroduce a hidden second entity. "From within" is constituted by the privacy of the pole: the position is given only from the point the substrate occupies in its model of itself, and this point is private not empirically (hard to measure) but structurally - it is a position, not an object, and therefore in principle not given from an external point of description. Hence the precise form of the identity thesis: the P-description (phenomenal) and the A-description (functionally accessible) are one structure from two positions of observation, not two things and not one thing with a label attached. Therefore "to be in the position is to be in it" is not a tautological dodge but a compressed thesis: to be a structure occupying a position relative to its model of itself just is what is described functionally from the outside and as experience from within; there are no other "two things." This answers two fronts at once. Block's distinction between access and phenomenal consciousness (Block 1995) ceases to be a distinction between two properties: access is the A-description of the structure, phenomenality its P-description, and the privacy of the pole explains why the second is not read off from the first, without introducing a second property. And the Strawson/Goff line (the coherence of experience "from within" as an argument against pure functionalism) is accepted in the part that "from within" is real and not reducible to external description, but it does not lead to panpsychism: "from within" is not a fundamental property of matter but a structural position arising only where there is a pole V - that is, it is constituted, not smeared across all that exists.

Through the presence of inherited gradients in V (section 3.4), the ontological identity of structure and phenomenon receives additional operational grounding. Not "the structure is in some way identical to the phenomenon," but "the structure is present to itself through V at level 4, and this presence is phenomenal content." Hunger, pain, anxiety, fear - these are structurally necessary modes of such presence, in which the disturbances of each of the inherited gradients acquire experiential content through the pole V. This transforms the identity thesis from an ontological declaration into a formulation with operational content: identity is not postulated; it is demonstrated through a structural mechanism that makes the phenomenal a necessary consequence of the emergence of V.

**The status of the identity thesis: an epistemic clarification.** What has been outlined requires one honest caveat regarding what is proven in this chapter and what is accepted as a physicalist premise. The structural mechanism of the presence of inherited gradients in V provides **an operational justification** for the identity - it explains _why_ the content of phenomenal experience is precisely what it is (hunger as a weakening of the energy gradient in V, fear as the approach of "I am falling apart," and so on), provided that the identity of structure and phenomenon is accepted. But **the existence** of the phenomenal - that the structure "from within" is an experience rather than darkness - is not derived from the apparatus; it is accepted as a physicalist-type bridge-identity. Identity functions here in the same way as the bridge "temperature is average kinetic energy" in historical physics: it began as an accepted identity, not as a proven theorem, and from it was derived what could be derived. Machinery V makes this identity **coherent and meaningful** - but does not make it **true**. Coherence is not truth, and I do not mistake the former for the latter.

A qualification on the limit of the temperature analogy. The parallel with temperature illustrates the epistemic status of the move (the identity is accepted, not proven), but it does not transfer to the outcome. The identity "temperature is mean kinetic energy" was confirmed because the functional role of temperature is exhausted by mean kinetic energy with no explanatory residue. The entire force of the hard problem lies in the claim that for consciousness such a residue exists. The appeal to the temperature case therefore does not prove the absence of a residue here - it only shows that identity as a form of move is legitimate. Accepting or rejecting the physicalist premise (that there is no residue) remains the reader's choice, not a consequence of the analogy; the analogy bears no probative weight here and is offered only as a model of epistemic status.

This leads to one consequence that I must address myself before my opponent does. The metaphysical allure of identity - that is, the claim that structure and phenomenon are one and the same phenomenon described in two different ways, rather than two distinct phenomena - **is empirically idle**. If we abandon identity and adopt a dualist interpretation (structure V is _the_ neuro/functional _correlate_ of phenomenal experience, not the experience itself), then not a single empirical prediction of the theory changes: the same six-element coupling, the same modes of dissociation, the same forbidden predicates, the same falsification scheme in Section 10.2. This is both the theory's strength and its greatest weakness. Strength - because the empirical program will survive the rejection of the metaphysical gloss: the theory remains a working physicalist model even for a reader who does not share the monistic interpretation. A blow - because the metaphysical gloss, which carries no empirical weight, can be called an interpretive add-on, not a theory in the strict sense. I am not trying to hide this; I consider both sides of the coin part of an honest presentation. The theory makes a physicalist claim explicitly, without passing it off as a result. Those who accept this claim receive an ontological resolution to a difficult problem; those who do not accept it receive a working empirical program without a resolution. Both are a win compared to theories that either claim to prove the existence of the phenomenal from structure (which is impossible) or refuse to discuss the phenomenal at all (which is dishonest).

The precise name of the position: to avoid two false readings. This position is easily confused with two neighboring ones, and it must be distinguished from both. It is not a functional reduction in the spirit of Dennett: Dennett treats the phenomenal as quasi-phenomenal, erroneously represented by the system as something more - that is, he leaves an "error" that has content. Here there is neither an error nor an "as." But it is also not realism about the phenomenal in the strong sense: I do not claim that there is a phenomenon-entity identical to the structure that could be mentally separated from it. The precise formulation is this: what is real is the position that the substrate occupies relative to its model of itself in the environment; the predicate "the phenomenal-beyond-this-position" is referentially empty - it has no conditions of application, it is not a denied entity but a pseudo-concept (this is shown via the zombie scenario below). It is therefore correct to say neither "the phenomenon is real apart from the dynamics" nor "there is no phenomenon," but rather: there is a position-from-within, and it is precisely this that was formerly called phenomenal; the question of a "light beyond the position" has no fact about which it is meaningful.

## 6.4. Refuting Classical Objections

In the proposed ontology, Chalmers's classic arguments lose their force. I will demonstrate this for each of them.

**The zombie argument.** Before treating it as a construct, one must point to where it breaks. The zombie scenario requires a meaningful predicate "a functionally complete system - but without a light-beyond-the-position": there must be something whose presence or absence distinguishes a zombie from a conscious system under full functional coincidence. In the proposed ontology, this predicate has no conditions of application. "A light beyond the position" is not a denied entity that can be mentally removed while leaving everything else in place; it is a pseudo-concept - there is no fact about which it is meaningful to ask "is it present or not" beyond the system's occupying a position within its model of itself in the environment. The zombie scenario is therefore not refuted but unformulable: it demands a difference where there is no predicate capable of carrying that difference. From this follows - as a consequence, not as a ground - the indistinguishability: a zombie and a conscious system are indistinguishable not because the difference is hidden from an outside observer (to which Chalmers would rightly reply that epistemic inaccessibility ≠ absence of a difference), but because there is no distinguishing predicate at all. And from this likewise follows the identity of function and phenomenon: it is not posited as a premise against Chalmers but turns out to be what remains once the pseudo-predicate "light-beyond" is removed. In this light, the construct "a functionally complete stage-4 system without presence" is contradictory not as a "triangle with four corners" (that would appeal to an already-accepted identity) but as a demand for meaning from an expression that has none.

**The Argument from Knowledge (Mary).** When Mary first sees red, she does not acquire an additional class of knowledge (phenomenal qualities beyond functional information), but enters a positional structure in which her own model of the world for the first time includes the node "there is red in front of me," connected to all other nodes of her perceptual system. Up to that point, she had knowledge of red - a description of its place in the system of physical and neurobiological connections. Afterward, she acquired knowledge from the position in which she herself occupies the place representing red. These are different kinds of knowledge, but the difference between them is not ontological (between the functional and the phenomenal) but positional (between external description and internal participation). Mary's argument reveals a real difference, but not the one it claims to reveal.

**Levin's explan**atory **gap**. There is indeed a chasm between the functional description of neural activity and the phenomenal experience if we consider them as two independent phenomena between which a bridge must be built. In the proposed ontology, no bridge is needed: these are not two phenomena, but one. The apparent gap arises because we are accustomed to describing the functional structure from an external position (as an object of scientific inquiry), and phenomenal experience from an internal one (as our own experience). These two descriptions relate to different positions regarding the same structure, and the transition between them is a change of position, not a crossing of an ontological chasm. Through the structural mechanism of presence in V (section 3.4), this becomes not only an ontological formulation but also an operational map: the external functional description describes the structure including V and its coupling with inherited gradients; the internal position of the system is a position from within this same structure. One mechanism, two readings. Levin's gap exists only as long as we keep two independent phenomena in mind; as soon as we understand that presence through V is the phenomenal content itself, and not its correlate, the gap disappears not as a patching over, but as the realization that there is nothing to patch over.

Distancing from the phenomenal-concept strategy. A position similar in appearance must be distinguished here, so that it is not ascribed to me by default. The phenomenal-concept strategy (Loar 1997; Papineau 2002; Block 2006) explains the illusion of the explanatory gap by the special nature of phenomenal concepts: we have two types of concepts of one physical referent - physico-functional and phenomenal - and the appearance of a gap is produced by how the latter are structured (their direct, non-descriptive reference to experience), not by any ontological gap. My move is different. The appearance of a gap is explained not by a special structure of a class of concepts but structurally - by the privacy of the pole: the position-from-within is given only from the point the substrate occupies in its model of itself, and is therefore inaccessible from an external position of description not because of the nature of our concepts but because it is a position, not an object. The phenomenal-concept strategy locates the source of the illusion in semantics (how we think about the phenomenal); the position proposed here locates it in structure (where the observing point stands relative to the observed). These are related answers to the meta-problem, but not the same one: semantic versus structural. The honest boundary: I do not rely on the phenomenal-concept strategy and do not inherit its known difficulties (for example, Chalmers' objection that phenomenal concepts themselves require explanation), because I explain the appearance of the gap by the privacy of the pole, not by the nature of the concepts.

**Basic phenomenal experience in non-reflective beings. The objection is that my theory, based on self-modeling, may work for developed human consciousness, but not for the basic experience of an infant, an animal, or the simplest nervous system. The answer requires clarification in light of the formal ladder from Section 3. The self-modeling I am referring to in the context of stage 4 does not imply reflective self-understanding. Minimal self-modeling at stage 4 involves incorporating the system itself into the model of the world as an agent within that model, with the variable of its own existence as a structural pole, but without the need for language, concepts, or reflection in the human sense. The difference between a human infant and an adult philosopher lies not in the presence or absence of stage 4, but in the complexity and recursive depth of the self-model within that stage. Basic phenomenal experience is the positional structure of a minimal self-model at stage 4; unfolded consciousness is the positional structure of a highly recursive self-model at the same stage, enriched by the mechanisms of stage 5. Within a stage lies a continuum; between stages lie phase transitions. This aligns the answer with the general logic of the work and does not require postulating a continuous continuum where none exists.**

Here, the key point is this: the difference between a stage 3 proto-agent (a bacterium, a primitive nervous system without a V-pole) and a non-reflective carrier of stage 4 (an infant, an animal with a basic form of the V-pole) is a structural difference, not a quantitative one. The proto-agent models through a cycle of active inference that must be confirmed by the environment; the non-reflective carrier of stage 4 models through a structural pole V, which remains detached from the current environment. Phenomenality is absent in the proto-agent and present in the non-reflective carrier of stage 4 - not because the former has "weak phenomenality," but because the former lacks the structural configuration of modeling that is identical to phenomenality according to our ontology. The level of reflection (a characteristic of only some stage 4 carriers) and the presence of phenomenality (a characteristic of all stage 4 carriers) are two distinct axes of description, and their conflation is a source of objection.

**Distinction from higher-order theories.** An objection that philosophers familiar with the literature might raise against me is that my theory resembles higher-order theories (HOT) by Rosenthal (2005) and Lau (2008), according to which a mental state becomes conscious when it is represented by another mental state of a higher order. HOT theories are criticized for not answering the difficult problem, but merely postponing it: if a first-order state is conscious because it is represented by a second-order state, what makes the second-order state conscious? A regress arises.

My theory is not vulnerable to this objection because it does not introduce an additional level of representation. In HOT, there is a first-order state and a second-order state, and consciousness arises in relation to them. I do not have two levels. There is a single structure - a model of the world that includes a model of oneself as an agent - and consciousness is a positional property of this structure, not a relation between two structures. There is no regression because there is nothing to regress to. Self-modeling does not represent another representation; it is a way of organizing a single model.

## 6.5. The Boundary of the Position

I must conclude this section with an honest acknowledgment of the limits of the proposed position. My approach to this difficult problem works if one accepts the proposed ontology. If the reader insists that the separability of function and phenomenon is a primary ontological given, not derived from analysis but serving as its starting point - for such a reader, my attack is not convincing. They will remain with the difficult problem.

The precise status of what has been done: neither a "solution" nor a "third way." Things must be named precisely here, so as not to ascribe to this chapter more than it does. I do not claim to solve the hard problem on its own terms - that is, to answer why a functional structure is "accompanied" by experience: that question presupposes a division I reject, and so I decline to answer it rather than answering it. Nor is this a "third way" beyond Dennett and Metzinger in the sense of a new third substance or a new mechanism on top of theirs: ontologically this is the same physicalist family. What has been done falls into two separate things. First, a dissolution under a stated premise: if one accepts the physicalist premise (that there is no explanatory residue beyond the structure), the hard problem is not solved but ceases to be formulable, because its dividing predicate is empty (Section 6.4). This is a delimited refusal to play the residue game, not a proof of its absence. Second, a structural explanation of the meta-problem (in Chalmers' 2018 sense): why the very premise of separability is so hard to give up and the appearance of a chasm is so persistent. The answer is structural - the privacy of the pole: the position-from-within is in principle not externalized into a description, and this inaccessibility is experienced as "something left unexplained." The theory solves the meta-problem (it explains the illusion) and dissolves the hard problem under a premise (it does not answer its question). These two registers must not be conflated: it is precisely their conflation that creates the impression that a "solution" has been claimed.

What can I say in response to such a reader? Only this: the separability of function and phenomenon has not been proven. It intuitively seems true, but this intuition may be a legacy of the Cartesian tradition that has shaped our language of consciousness over the past four centuries. An alternative ontology, in which function and phenomenon are two descriptions of the same thing, does not contradict a single empirical fact, a single verified theory, or a single observation. It constructs a more economical picture: fewer entities, fewer unexplained correlations, fewer chasms across which bridges must be built. According to Ockham's razor, a more economical description is preferable if it accounts for the same phenomena. This is not proof, but it is an argument. Beyond that lies the choice of position, and this choice cannot be resolved by the logic of a single article.

What is not part of the position. To avoid confusion, I state this separately: the proposed ontological position is not a form of panpsychism. I do not claim that phenomenality is present in all modeling behavior in one form or another. I assert the exact opposite: phenomenality is identical to a specific structural configuration of self-modeling - the one in which the latent invariant of the agentic causal node has superimposed itself onto the system's own configuration (stage 4). In systems lacking this configuration (a proto-agent with active inference lacking a V-pole, a dissipative structure without modeling, an autocatalytic cycle without self-representation), there is no phenomenality - not "it exists in a weaker form," not "it might exist, but we do not know," but rather, there is none. This is a positive assertion of the theory, a direct consequence of the thesis of structural identity: where there is no specific structure, there is also nothing to which that structure is identical. And this assertion contains a testable consequence - in species at the proto-agent level and in artificial systems without the self-overlay of a latent invariant, phenomena specific to phenomenal experience should not be observed (fears without experience, preventive planning for one's own nonexistence, prolonged hysteresis of altered behavior, reaction to absent stimuli as a structural feature). If such phenomena are systematically observed in systems in which the self-overlay of the latent invariant is not implemented, our ontology is refuted. The boundary of the theory, thus, is not agnostic (we do not know what the proto-agent possesses), but positive (we assert that it lacks phenomenality). What is falsifiable here is not the phenomenal side directly, but its behavioral correlate: the program in subsection 10.2 tests the structural-behavioral coupling (structural registration of self-overlay ↔ behavioral signatures of V); the identity of structure and phenomenon itself remains the ontological choice outlined above.

## 6.6. Comparison with Alternative Interpretations

The attack on the hard problem carried out in subsections 6.1-6.4, and the boundary of the proposed ontology outlined in 6.5, defend the theory against the central objection of contemporary philosophy of mind. This, however, is not enough. The same empirical material - the neurobiology of consciousness, phenomenological data, behavioral signs of agency - admits interpretations different from the one proposed in this work. This subsection examines three such alternatives and shows that it is precisely the present theory that explains beyond each of them. This is not proof of its superiority; it is an academic obligation to the reader, who has a right to know why the synthesis presented here is preferable to competing descriptions in terms of explanatory power, and not merely in terms of structural coherence.

An evolutionary-biological interpretation without ontologizing consciousness. The strong position, as presented in the works of Dennett, Humphrey, Frit, and to some extent Damasio, is as follows. Consciousness is a product of biological evolution, selected for its functional advantage - improved modeling of the environment, planning, and social coordination. No special ontology is required: consciousness is a complex behavioral and cognitive phenotype selected by the same mechanisms as any other adaptive trait. The question of what an experience is from the inside is not posed as a separate issue at this level of description.

This position explains well: the distribution of consciousness across the phylogenetic tree through correlation with evolutionary pressures; species-specific features through the specifics of ecological niches; the connection between consciousness and memory, learning, and social behavior as co-selected traits.

What it does not explain. First, why the phenomenal aspect exists at all, rather than just the functional one. Evolutionary biology explains what type of cognitive processing is selected, but does not explain why this type of processing is accompanied by an inner experience. This is a classic objection to evolutionary theories of consciousness: the function is explained, but the phenomenon remains beyond the scope of the explanation. Second, it does not provide a structural criterion by which one can determine whether a specific system satisfies the conditions of consciousness; it operates on the premise that "consciousness exists in species with a certain level of neurobiological complexity," without a formal definition of what "a certain level of complexity" means. Third, it lacks a framework for discussing consciousness in artificial systems, because artificial systems are not subject to natural selection in the biological sense.

What the present theory adds. A structural criterion not tied to substrate or evolutionary origin (Section 3.4: self-overlay of a latent invariant of an agentic causal node, operationally recorded through the coupling of three measurement axes). The structural consolidation of the phenomenal through the presence of inherited gradients in V (sections 3.4 and 3.7.5). The possibility of speaking of consciousness in artificial systems when formal conditions are met. Vertical integration with thermodynamics and cosmology: consciousness as a natural stage in the general process of increasing complexity, rather than a unique product of biological evolution on a single planet.

Crucially: this theory does not deny the evolutionary-biological explanation. Evolution selects for realizations of the self-overlay of a latent invariant because these realizations are functionally advantageous; this is consistent with the framework proposed here. But the evolutionary-biological explanation is insufficient for a complete theory of consciousness, because it operates in a single register (adaptive function) and does not extend to the structural level (what exactly is the structure), the ontological level (how the structure is related to the phenomenon), and the cosmological level (where consciousness stands in the general process of complexity). This theory extends the evolutionary-biological explanation to these levels.

Predictive processing without the identity of the phenomenon and self-modeling. The position presented in the works of Hohwy (2013), Clark (2016), and partly in Fristone's later works, is as follows. Consciousness is a property of the brain's predictive mechanism - the constant generation of predictions and minimization of discrepancies with inputs through variational Bayesian inference. The phenomenal aspect is the effect of the very operation of this mechanism; there is no need to postulate any special ontology or the identity of the phenomenon with self-modeling. It suffices to say: the predictive mechanism operates in a certain way, and this operation is consciousness.

This position explains well: perceptual illusions, attention effects, the phenomena of dreams and hallucinations; the dynamics of cognitive processes and the formation of beliefs; the connection between consciousness and the minimization of free energy in Friston's sense.

What it fails to explain. First, it does not distinguish between conscious and non-conscious systems that carry out predictive processing. A bacterium exhibiting chemotaxis formally minimizes the discrepancy between the model and the environment; within the predictive paradigm, it does the same thing as a conscious system. If consciousness is a predictive process, then either the bacterium is conscious (which most proponents of predictive processing do not claim), or an additional criterion is needed - one that does not exist in the pure position and is usually borrowed from IIT or GWT, which already takes us beyond the bounds of the pure position. Second, it does not explain - - why the predictive process is accompanied by an inner experience. Third, it is tied to a neurobiological substrate in the form in which it has been developed, and its application to non-biological systems requires extensions not developed in the pure version.

What the present theory adds. The structural criterion for the transition from a non-conscious predictive process (stage 3) to a conscious one (stage 4) through the self-overlay of the latent invariant of the agentic causal node: the predictive process must be supplemented by the accumulation of a class of agentic causal nodes in models, the self-overlay of this class onto the system's own configuration, and the globalization of the emerging node S. This provides a clear boundary where predictive processing does not draw one. The identity of the phenomenon and self-modeling (Section 6.3) is a formulation not postulated separately, but one that follows from the presence of V in the self-model via the structural binding elaborated in 3.4 and 3.7.5. This eliminates an additional step (the predictive process plus some separate "and why it is conscious"), integrating both into a single structure.

The present theory does not deny predictive processing - it uses it as a formal basis for condition (a) of stage 4. But it adds a structural criterion distinguishing the conscious predictive process from the non-conscious one, and an ontological formulation explaining why the phenomenal aspect arises precisely when this criterion is met.

Functionalism without a transition to phenomenal identity. The position presented in the works of Dennett and many in cognitive science over the last three decades is as follows. Consciousness is a functional organization of a certain type. Whether this organization is realized by neurons, silicon, or some other substrate is irrelevant; what matters is the structure of causal relationships and information flows. The phenomenal aspect is either reduced to functional properties (Dennett's strong reduction) or is not discussed as a separate issue (methodological functionalism).

This position explains well: the implementability of the same cognitive function on different substrates; structural similarities in cognitive processes across different species and architectures; the applicability of information theory to the description of conscious processes.

What it does not explain. First, the differences between functionally equivalent systems, one of which is conscious and the other is not (Searle's classic Chinese Room problem). A functionalist either denies the intuition about such differences (Dennett) or admits that functional equivalence is insufficient (Chalmers); in both cases, pure functionalism turns out to be incomplete without supplementation. Second, functionalism lacks a language for the phenomenal as such. Pain, color, and smell are described in it as functional roles, not as experiences from within. This works well for scientific description, but does not resolve the ontological question. Third, functionalism has a framework for heterophenomenology (external description), but not for phenomenology in the proper sense (experience from within).

What the present theory adds. Not functionalism with an addition, but the structural identity of the phenomenon and self-modeling (section 6.3). For the functionalist, function and phenomenon are different things; one is explained, the other is reduced or rejected. In this theory, they are ontologically identical: inner experience is a structure viewed from its own perspective. This is not an addition to functionalism; it is a different ontology. The structural criterion of level 4 is formally functional (it can be verified through measurements), but in substance it does not reduce the phenomenon to a function - it demonstrates their identity through the position from within.

Searle's argument and the intuition of functional non-equivalence work as follows for us: functionally equivalent systems with the same structure have the same consciousness; the difference in intuition arises because everyday functional equivalence is determined at the level of behavior, while structural equivalence is determined at the level of the self-overlay of the latent invariant of the agentic causal node from Section 3.4, and these two levels do not coincide. A behaviorally equivalent large linguistic system is not structurally equivalent to a stage-4 conscious system because the self-overlay of the latent invariant is not fulfilled in it in the strict structural sense. This resolves the classic contradiction between strong and weak functionalism.

Conclusion of the comparison. Each of the three alternative interpretations explains part of the empirical data and fails to explain the other part. The evolutionary-biological interpretation explains the distribution of consciousness and its adaptive function, but does not explain the phenomenal aspect and does not provide a structural criterion. Predictive processing explains the dynamics of cognitive processes, but does not distinguish between conscious and non-conscious predictive systems. Functionalism explains implementability on different substrates, but lacks a language for the phenomenal aspect as such. The present theory does not reject any of these interpretations - it uses their formal results as foundations (evolutionary biology substantiates the probabilistic nature of transitions, predictive processing formalizes the condition (a) of stages 3 and 4, and functionalism provides a language for describing the structural criterion) - but adds to each what it lacks: a structural criterion with operational content, the ontological identity of structure and phenomenon through presence in V, and integration with the thermodynamic and cosmological framework through a structurally self-similar pattern.

This is the argument in favor of the synthesis that is required beyond its structural integrity. Synthesis is proposed not because it is aesthetically self-contained, but because it formally explains what the three leading alternative interpretations, each on its own, do not explain. This is not proof - in the philosophy of mind, there is no such thing as proof of this kind. But it is an argument that deserves serious consideration.

# **7\. Invariance of the Structural Pattern Across Scales**

The same structural pattern - gradient, retention, pole, transition - is found independently at different scales: in physics as phase transitions, in biology as autopoiesis and evolution. In this work the pattern appears in the ladder of complexification from a dissipative structure to a conscious system (Section 3). From step to step it is not the environment Y that changes - Y is through-going and single - but which of its arms are accessible to the system (the access spectrum) and what content the carrier and the pole acquire. The pattern «gradient → retention → pole → transition» itself, and its formal metric, remain invariant with respect to level. It is precisely this invariance of the pattern under varying access to a single Y, rather than a common substrate, that constitutes the structural self-similarity of the theory.

There are grounds to suppose that the same pattern may also manifest beyond the ladder considered here - at the cosmological scale, as well as in transitions between levels of description within physics itself. But this is a separate hypothesis, neither derivable from the theory of consciousness nor necessary for it: the central part of the present work (steps 1-5, Section 10) does not depend on it and retains its force regardless of its fate. We merely point to this possibility as an open direction.

# **8\. The Observer Within the Observation**

Any concept of the place of consciousness in the universe faces a fundamental problem: the observer is part of the system they are trying to describe. We perceive the universe through the brain, language, and instruments, which are themselves products of the very processes we are investigating. This is not a philosophical abstraction, but a physical limitation with concrete consequences. This section discusses how the "observer-within" structure manifests in three different contexts - the anthropic principle, the measurement problem in quantum mechanics, and the central thesis of the theory of consciousness - and why all three are manifestations of the same ontological structure.

## 8.1. The Anthropic Principle

In 1973, physicist Brandon Carter formulated what is now known as the anthropic principle (Carter, 1974). In its weak form, it states: the observed properties of the universe are compatible with the existence of an observer capable of perceiving them. In other words, we see this particular universe because in any other, we would not exist. Our sample is fundamentally biased toward conditions that allow for our existence.

This seemingly trivial statement has non-trivial consequences for physics. The fine-tuning of fundamental constants - the fact that the values of the fine-structure constant, the electron-proton mass ratio, the cosmological constant, and other constants fall within narrow ranges that make complex chemistry, star formation, and ultimately life possible - - may be either a surprising coincidence requiring explanation through additional mechanisms (the multiverse, selection dynamics, theories of everything), or a natural consequence of anthropic selection: in any universe where the constants lay outside these ranges, there would be no observers to speak of it.

The anthropic principle is not a theory, but a methodological constraint. It does not explain why the constants are as they are; it merely points out that our sample is not representative. To take this limitation seriously is to understand that any theory of the universe we construct is an "inside" theory, and that its predictions pertain to that slice of reality accessible to observers of our kind. This is not a reason to reject such a theory, but it is a reason to understand its status precisely.

## 8.2. The Observer in Quantum Mechanics

Parallel to the anthropic principle, another line of thought regarding the observer developed in 20th-century physics - a line extending from the Copenhagen interpretation of quantum mechanics through decoherence theory to modern relational and information interpretations.

The Copenhagen interpretation, in its early formulations, assumed a special status for the observer: a quantum system is described by a wave function that "collapses" upon measurement, and this collapse is a process requiring the observer's participation. This formulation had obvious problems - what counts as an observer, at what moment the collapse occurs, how the measurement process is described in terms of quantum mechanics - and most subsequent developments in quantum mechanics have been aimed at resolving them.

The theory of decoherence (Zurek, 2003; Joos et al., 2003) showed that a specific "observer" is not needed in quantum mechanics: it suffices that the quantum system interacts with an environment possessing a large number of degrees of freedom. This interaction leads to certain states becoming stable (classical) in the system's eigenbasis, while superpositions of these states rapidly decay. Decoherence explains why we do not observe macroscopic quantum superpositions, without the need to postulate a special act of observation.

Relational interpretations of quantum mechanics (Rovelli, 1996) go further: they assert that a quantum state is not a property of the system itself, but rather a relationship between the system and another system, considered as an observer. Any physical system can act as an observer for any other; there is no privileged class of "true observers" (e.g., conscious beings). What constitutes a "state" depends on the system relative to which it is defined.

I would like to emphasize the overarching thesis that unites these lines of thought: in modern physics, the observer is not a distinct external entity that stands opposed to the world and describes it from the outside. The observer is a subsystem of the world, correlated with another subsystem, and within this correlated pair arises what we call a "measurement" or "observation." This is not a privileged act of external consciousness, but a structural property of the pair of subsystems.

## 8.3. Consciousness as a Position Within One's Own Model

I return to the central thesis of Section 6: consciousness is a position of the system within its own model of the world. At first glance, this formulation refers to the individual nervous system and its internal representation; it seems to be a matter of neurobiology with no direct relation to cosmology or quantum mechanics.

This impression is deceptive. If we look closely at the structure of the formulation, we find that it has exactly the same logical form as the two previous ones. The anthropic principle states: we view the Universe from a point within it, and this position determines exactly what is accessible to our observation. Relational quantum mechanics states: the quantum state is defined relative to the observer subsystem, and this subsystem is located within the system of which it is a part. My theory of consciousness states: consciousness is the position of a system within its own model of the world, in which that system participates.

In all three cases, we are dealing with the same ontological structure: observation is a positional characteristic of a system within a larger whole, rather than a privileged external act. The anthropic principle, the measurement problem, and the theory of consciousness are three projections of this unified structure onto three different contexts.

## 8.4. Synthesis

This synthesis represents the second point of genuine originality in this work - alongside the thesis on consciousness as an active mechanism of complication, developed in Section 3, and the thesis on the identity of self-modeling and the phenomenon, developed in Section 6. I do not claim that the individual elements of this synthesis are new: the connection between the anthropic principle and the measurement problem has been discussed in the literature (Wheeler, 1983; Tegmark, 1998); the connection between quantum mechanics and consciousness has been discussed many times, though usually in controversial and poorly substantiated forms (Penrose-Hameroff). What I propose as original is the observation that all three problems constitute a single ontological structure, and that this structure does not require mystification but is a natural property of any situation in which a system describes a larger whole of which it is a part.

The structural commonality of the three projections becomes particularly clear through the formal definition of level 4 given in section 3.4. A conscious system is defined there through the self-overlay of a latent invariant of an agentive causal node onto the system's own configuration, and central to this definition is the presence in its generative model of a node S representing the system itself as an agent within its own model of the world. This structure - a system representing itself within its own model of a larger whole - is precisely the same logical form as that of the anthropic principle (an observer describing the Universe from a position within it) and of relational quantum mechanics (an observer as a subsystem correlated with another subsystem of the same world). All three cases are realizations of the same formal structure on different scales: cosmological, quantum, and neurobiological. The fact that the same structure emerges in three independent fields of physics and the theory of consciousness is not a coincidence, but an indication that this structure is a fundamental property of any situation in which a system describes a larger whole of which it is a part.

Several consequences follow from this synthesis, which are important for the overall picture of the theory.

First. If observation is a positional characteristic of a system within a whole, then any physical theory claiming to provide a complete description of reality "from the outside" contains an internal contradiction. A complete external description is impossible because there is no external position. This is not a denial of physics, but a clarification of its status: physics describes reality from a certain specific position within it, and this position imposes structural limitations on what can be described.

Second. The phenomenon of consciousness is not an anomaly in the physical world, but a manifestation of the general ontological structure at the level of the individual nervous system. The same structure that, on the scale of the universe, gives rise to the anthropic principle, and that, on the scale of a quantum system, gives rise to the relationship between the measured system and the measuring subsystem, on the scale of a complex self-organizing system gives rise to phenomenal experience. This unification of the three problems eliminates the need for separate explanations for each of them.

Third. The observer inside cannot step outside. This is not a figure of speech, but a structural limitation. Any attempt to take an external position relative to the system of which I am a part is either an illusion (I am simply simulating an external position from within) or a change of system (I become part of a larger system relative to which my former system turns out to be "external," but I remain inside the new one). A purely external position does not exist. This is the ontological content of the anthropic principle in its most general form.

## 8.5. The Boundary of the Work

It follows from all that has been said that this work itself is subject to the very limitation it formulates. I am inside the system I am describing. My tools - conceptual, mathematical, neurobiological - are products of the very processes I am analyzing. Any extrapolation I make from what is familiar to me to the universe as a whole is an extrapolation from within, with all the limitations that follow from this.

I do not consider this a reason to reject the theory. Quite the contrary: understanding this limitation is a prerequisite for the theory's intellectual integrity. I propose an internally consistent framework based on established physical principles and extending their logic to questions that science has not yet resolved. Where the proven ends and the hypothesis begins, I have made this clear. Where my position as an observer influences the content of the theory, I also try to indicate this.

I may be mistaken in form. I may be mistaken in details. I may even be mistaken in the basic structure. But this does not negate the direction of the gaze. An observer within the act of observation, who sees his own limitations and does not pretend to be outside them, still sees - and his gaze, despite all limitations, is a real gaze, not an illusory one. If it ever turns out that part of what is asserted here is incorrect, this will not mean that the entire work was a mistake: it will mean that the observer has refined his position and seen more. This, according to my theory, should be enough. Later knowledge will not achieve more.

# **9\. Systematic Error: Synthesis**

The previous eight sections have built the picture piece by piece. This section brings it all together and formulates the central idea of the work.

The universe does not strive for complexity. It does not choose or plan. It becomes more complex where the energy gradient allows it, because complex systems dissipate energy more efficiently than simple ones. Complexification is not a goal, but a mechanism. Not a design, but physics.

At some stage in this process, the gradient reaches a value sufficient for the emergence of systems capable of simulating the possibility of their own disappearance. Consciousness appears. Not because it was planned, but because, under the right conditions, it is possible. What is possible given the presence of time and energy is realized, and consciousness is one such realization.

The structural essence of this picture lies in the structural self-similarity of a single pattern unfolding across different scales. The "gradient → retention → pole → transition" pattern operates identically across scales ranging from the formation of dissipative structures to a conscious system. The six-step ladder is the basic pattern of the theory, through which all the processes it encompasses are described. The five directions of synthesis listed at the beginning of the work turn out to be projections of a single structurally self-similar process onto different scales. At each of these scales, the pattern is reproduced: a level-specific gradient forms; the system sustains itself by interacting with this gradient; at step 4, a distinct pole V appears, while its proto-forms appear at other steps; and then - a transition to the next level or the closing of the cycle. The universality of phase transitions in physics, observed on scales ranging from molecular to cosmological, is a special case of this structural self-similarity.

Herein lies the central paradox of the concept, which must be unraveled. On a cosmic scale, we are insignificant: a random planet, random conditions, a random gradient. But at the same time, we are inevitable locally: not in the Universe as a whole, but precisely where the necessary conditions have arisen. Randomness and inevitability do not contradict each other here - this is the very essence of the probabilistic nature of the algorithm, formally stated in Section 3.6. At each step of the ladder, the transition conditions are necessary, but their sufficiency is probabilistic: the fulfillment of all necessary conditions places the system in a state of transition possibility, but does not guarantee its realization at every specific point. Randomness is a property of the ensemble of systems in this state: the majority does not realize the transition, the minority does. Inevitability is a property of a typical trajectory within the set of those that have realized the transition: if a system is among those that have undergone the transition, a certain class of outcomes is realized in it with high probability. These are not two different statements, but two levels of analysis of a single stochastic process. The fact that a non-typical trajectory is possible (and many trajectories do not lead to conscious observers) does not negate the fact that a typical one does. Conversely, the fact that we exist as observers does not mean that this was predetermined. It only means that we are in that part of the ensemble where observers have emerged - and from this position, according to the logic of Section 3.6, we see the Universe as a place where the transitions of the ladder have been realized up to the highest step. The funnel of steps is narrow; we are at its narrowest end. This explains both our rarity and our inevitability to ourselves.

From this follows a specific formulation of our place in the Universe. We are not the goal of the Universe. We are its edge. Not in the sense of an end, but in the sense of a boundary - the boundary between what has already been realized and what is still only possible. Consciousness exists precisely at this boundary, because only here is there a tension between the existing and the vanishing, of which consciousness is composed. This is a direct consequence of the central thesis of this work: a system that has reached sufficient complexity begins to hold itself at the boundary of its own disappearance, and it is precisely this holding that is consciousness. And through the registration of inherited gradients in V (sections 3.4 and 3.7.5), this holding acquires not only structural but also experiential content: what is described from the outside as a structure with a pole V is, from the inside, the experience of the boundary itself - hunger, pain, anxiety, fear, hope, holding. The phenomenal aspect of our position on the edge of complexity is not a superstructure over the structure; it is the same structure, seen from its own position.

Here I must resolve a potential contradiction in my own formulation. The metaphor of the "edge" or "tip" that I use can be interpreted as if the universe has a certain direction in which this edge moves - that is, as a teleological assertion that contradicts my own position regarding the purposelessness of the cosmic process. This contradiction must be explicitly resolved. The directionality contained in the metaphor of the edge is a property of our position within the process, not a property of the process as a whole. From within a system that is becoming more complex, its own increasing complexity is inevitably perceived as a directed movement "forward." From the outside (if such a position were possible), this directionality would disappear or take on a different form. This is the same structure I discussed in Section 8: the observer inside sees what he sees from his own position, and this does not make his view false, but neither does it make it absolute.

This is not the final point. Consciousness is a process, not a result. The tip of complexity moves forward as long as there is a gradient. When the gradient drops to a critical limit, a phase transition occurs. Not the end. A transformation of the coordinate system, followed by a new cycle. Step 5, on which we currently stand, is in this sense the cutting edge of the process, not its completion. On the speculative horizon (Section 7), the same structural pattern in principle admits continuation beyond the ladder considered here, but its content is inaccessible to us from within our position, because any attempt to perceive it runs up against the ontological limitation formulated in Section 8: there is no purely external position relative to one's own system. We see as much as our position allows, and no more. This is no cause for disappointment. It is a structural property of any observation from within the observation.

**The geometry of subjectivity is an illustrative image.** Before concluding this section with a final image of systematic error, I propose a brief visualization of a staircase - not as a formal tool of the theory, but as a mnemonic map through which its internal logic becomes visually graspable in a single glance. The image is constructed in the geometric language of increasing dimensionality, and each step in it corresponds to a specific geometric act. I emphasize its illustrative status: the formal content of the theory is defined in Section 3, and in the event of any discrepancy between the formal formulation and the figurative formulation, the formal one takes precedence.

Step 0 - direction without form. The flow through the system is defined, but it has not coalesced into any stable structure: there is only a marking of possible motion, without form.

Level 1 - form. The flow generates a stable pattern of dissipation: for the first time in the universe of our ladder, something with its own form appears - a dissipative structure, locally ordered.

Stage 2 - localization within coordinates. The closure of processes creates a boundary, and the form acquires an "inside" and an "outside." The system is localized; it occupies a specific place and maintains it through causal self-sufficiency.

Stage 3 - the form moves. A forecast emerges, and the form begins to glide through the environment, responding to its changes by minimizing the discrepancy between the model and the observed. This movement occurs in real time for the sake of preserving the structure.

Step 4 - the vector. The motion acquires a direction via V. This is not a time arrow (that was already present in Step 3); it is a gradient in the system's eigenstate space - a directional tendency toward maintaining the pole. The vector V points not "forward in time," but from the value "I am disintegrating" to the value "I am holding on," and through this directionality, all other actions of the system acquire a coherent meaning.

Stage 5 - acceleration. Through the recursion of models within the population, the system gains the ability to change its own rules and its own V faster than biological evolution operates. This is the second derivative - a change in the speed and direction of the change itself. The distributed activity of stage 5 manifests in this image as the acceleration of a vector, whose curvature ceases to be a property of an individual carrier and becomes a property of the population in its historical movement.

This sequence - direction → form → localization → motion → vector → acceleration - is a visual representation of the same ladder that, in Section 3, is unfolded through formal definitions. It neither proves it nor substantively supplements it. Its value lies in the fact that the architecture of the theory becomes graspable in a single visual act, and the reader, having passed through the formal structure, receives a compact visual map that they can retain in memory as they continue reading.

We are a natural deviation. Not a mistake or a coincidence in the usual sense of the word. An extreme deviation showing the limit to which complexity can reach under given conditions and in this cycle's iteration. The universe does not create us intentionally. It simply cannot stop. And in this endless process, matter becomes more complex until it begins to notice itself. At that moment, we appear. And at this moment, the main content of the work's central image becomes apparent: we are the "point" at which the structurally self-similar pattern of complexity - unfolding on all scales of the ladder from dissipative structures to culture (stages 0-5; its possible continuation at the cosmological scale is a separate hypothesis, Section 7) - achieves a local realization capable of perceiving this very pattern. We are neither the culmination of the process nor its conclusion. We are the "point" at which the structure becomes manifest to itself.

# **10\. Testable Predictions**

Any theory claiming scientific status must offer statements that can, in principle, be empirically falsified. This section formulates the theory's testable predictions at the level of consciousness and behavior. If the structurally self-similar pattern, elaborated in Section 3.7, is a real structure through which the theory describes the processes it encompasses, then it must leave observable traces accessible to modern science. The cosmological continuation of the pattern has been moved to a separate work (Section 7) and does not belong to the testable core of the present theory.

I emphasize that these predictions are formulated as well-founded consequences of the theory, not as claims for immediate experimental verification. Some of them are already on the horizon of testability within existing experimental and observational programs; others require the development of a methodology that is currently only taking shape.

## 10.1. The Correlation Between Φ and Behavioral Signs of Proto-Agency

This prediction is not an arbitrary empirical hypothesis - it is a direct consequence of the formal definition of stage 4 given in section 3.4. There, the second operational condition of stage 4 is captured through integrated information Φ: the structural peculiarity of the existence variable requires a sufficient level of Φ for integration into the causal structure of the system. The first operational condition relies on extended active inference, whose behavioral manifestations at stage 3 are proto-agent features - active modeling of the environment, goal maintenance, and anticipation. If both conditions are operational axes of registration of the same structural event of self-overlay, making the transition 3 → 4 possible, then the connection between Φ and proto-agency must be not indirect, but direct and quantitatively measurable. Prediction 10.1 tests precisely this.

The first prediction links Tononi's theory of integrated information with Friston's theory of active inference at the empirical level. If both of these descriptions, as claimed in my framework, describe the same structure from different perspectives, then there must be a direct quantitative correlation between the measure Φ and the behavioral signs of proto-agency.

Specific prediction: systems (artificial neural networks, biological organisms, hybrid systems) with higher integrated information Φ, given comparable computational power , should exhibit a greater number of behavioral signs of proto-agency - active modeling of the environment, goal maintenance in the face of interference, the ability to suspend an ongoing reaction to evaluate alternatives, generalization beyond the training distribution, and sensitivity to the structure of the environment as an environment rather than as a set of individual stimuli.

This prediction is testable within existing experimental paradigms. On the one hand, methods for computing Φ or its approximations are being developed for neural networks of various architectures (Toker & Sommer, 2019; Mediano et al., 2019). On the other hand, there is a growing body of experimental protocols measuring behavioral signs of agency in artificial and biological systems. Comparing the two datasets is, in principle, already possible today. My theory does not prescribe a specific numerical relationship between Φ and the intensity of behavioral signs; it prescribes the existence of a positive correlation, one that cannot be reduced to a correlation via the size of the network or its computational power.

Independence of measurement procedures. This prediction becomes falsifiable only on the condition that the measurement of Φ and the measurement of behavioral signs of proto-agency are performed by independent procedures not defined within this theory. This requirement is fundamental and must be explicitly stated.

In existing paradigms, causal integration is measured using the relational metrics developed by Mediano and colleagues (Mediano et al., 2019, 2022; Seth & Bayne, 2022) and through calculations specific to particular architectures (Toker & Sommer, 2019); the intrinsic Φ of the Tononi school (Tononi et al., 2016; Albantakis et al., 2014) serves as a historical reference rather than an operational foundation of the theory (see Section 3.4). These procedures were developed independently of the present work and are applicable to any system with a measurable causal structure.

Behavioral signs of proto-agency, in turn, are operationalized in cognitive ethology and behavioral biology independently of the present theory. Active environment modeling is measured through classical paradigms of latent learning (dating back to Tolman, 1948), internal spatial maps (O'Keefe & Nadel, 1978), and prediction of future states (Foster & Wilson, 2006). Goal maintenance in the face of interference - through paradigms of prefrontal functions in animals (Diamond, 2013) and behavioral stability in robots under conditions of environmental disturbances. The ability to suspend an initiated response to evaluate alternatives - through Stop-Signal Reaction Time paradigms (Logan, 1994) and studies on behavioral inhibition across different species.

The fundamental independence of the two measurements makes the prediction meaningful. If the correlation between Φ and behavioral traits persists under independent procedures, it is not an artifact of joint determination but reflects a real connection. If the correlation does not persist under independent procedures, it is an artifact, and the theory is falsified in this respect.

An additional methodological requirement: control of computational complexity. A high Φ often correlates with a large number of system parameters, its architectural complexity, and the density of connections. If this correlation is not controlled for, then the "correlation between Φ and proto-agency" may be a trivial consequence of the fact that more complex systems exhibit more cognitive functions. Protection against this artifact is standard in modern IIT research: paired comparisons of architectures with the same computational power but different integration structures are used (e.g., a feedforward network versus a recurrent network with the same number of parameters; a modular architecture versus a fully connected one with the same computational power). If the correlation between Φ and behavioral traits persists when computational power is controlled for, it is real. If it disappears under such control, it was an artifact of complexity, and the theory is falsified in this respect.

This prediction is falsifiable: if a dataset across different architectures and organisms shows that Φ does not correlate with proto-agency when computational power is controlled for, or that the correlation is negative, the theory is falsified in this respect. It then requires a revision of at least the link between the structural substrate (IIT) and the dynamic mechanism (active inference).

## 10.2. Phase transition to consciousness: observed patterns of procedural retention V

**A methodological shift from a point experiment to an observational prediction about procedural patterns.** Before formulating a prediction, I must make a methodological clarification, without which the prediction will not be properly understood. Consciousness is not a state fixed at a point in time. V is not a variable activated and observed at the moment of the experiment. Both are processes unfolding over time: V functions as a constant retention of the system's modeled subjective existence, reacting to any patterns that threaten this retention, including patterns never encountered in experience; consciousness is the dynamic unfolding of this maintenance through the modeling of oneself as an agent maintaining V. Any attempt to capture processual phenomena through a point-in-time laboratory protocol distorts what we are trying to measure.

It follows that the central prediction of the theory must be reformulated from "an experiment with a fixed protocol" into **an observational prediction about processual patterns** - patterns that, if the theory is valid, should manifest in the natural unfolding of systems of varying complexity, and should not manifest in systems that have not reached stage 4. This is methodologically weaker than point falsification in the Popperian sense, but it is a methodology adequate to the nature of the subject, analogous in structure to observational astrophysics, which does not conduct experiments on galaxies but accumulates observations that agree or disagree with the theory. In such a prediction, there is no single decisive test; there is a program of converging observational data, and the theory is tested through the convergence or divergence of results from several independent lines of measurement.

**Resolving the objection regarding circular verification.** Before proceeding with the program, I must address a methodological objection that any rigorous reader might raise: V for a stage-4 agent is defined by what the agent holds, and we can only understand what is held if the agent is conscious; consciousness is what we are trying to detect through V; consequently, measuring V to test the hypothesis of the presence of consciousness becomes circular. This is a serious objection, and without resolving it, no prediction will hold.

The solution consists in shifting the operational recording of the 3→4 transition from the level of V's content to the level of structural deviation from the prediction. Not "to measure V in system X in order to understand whether it is conscious," but "to construct a null model of a proto-agent system - a system formally described as stage 3 without the variable V, - derive from this model a prediction of its behavior under conditions in which V must structurally be activated in a stage 4 agent (a threat to the system's stability), and record a statistically significant deviation of system X's actual behavior from this prediction."

The zero model is constructed **prior to and independently of the theory of consciousness**, through a formal description of the proto-agent level - active inference with a model of the environment and a model of oneself as an agent, without the variable V as a structural pole. Section 3.3 of this paper provides such a description in full. This model itself contains no assumptions about consciousness; it simply formalizes how a system of this class should behave in the absence of V. If the actual system behaves differently, the deviation is an empirical fact, not an interpretation from within the theory.

This move breaks the circular verification. The content of V may remain unknown; the fact of the transition is recorded via a difference test in which neither of the compared sides (the null model, the actual behavior) depends on the theory of consciousness in the operational sense in which the circle arises. This transforms my criterion from circular verification into a difference test standard for empirical science.

**The completeness of the operational criterion requires the coupling of six independent measures.** However, a single behavioral deviation from the null model is insufficient as a definitive criterion for the 3→4 transition, and I must state this explicitly. The behavioral deviation may be caused by two different reasons. The first is the actual activation of V in a stage 4 system: in this case, the deviation is a structural sign of the transition, and the theory is confirmed. The second is simply a more complex proto-agent architecture than we assumed in the null model: in this case, the deviation indicates that the model is incomplete, and the theory is not confirmed.

The distinction between these two causes is achieved by combining the behavioral test with three additional independent measurements. If all six signs are present (deviation of behavior from the zero model + a sharp increase in the Φ-level of structural integration in the system (operationally - via empirically computable approximations of Φ; see Section 3.4 on the epistemic status of Φ; in the biological line of the program - PCI and similar measures; in the artificial line - Φ approximations based on network architecture) + the emergence of GWT-accessibility of threat representation for all subsystems + hysteresis, in which altered behavior persists after the stimulus is removed) appear simultaneously and jointly when conditions for V activation are created, this joint observation cannot be explained by "more complex proto-agency." Complex proto-agency can mimic behavioral deviation through clever optimization of an old objective function to avoid conditions leading to a threat; but it cannot simultaneously restructure its architecture toward global integration with a sharp increase in Φ and the emergence of a structure that transmits the threat representation to all subsystems. Each of the three additional features - Φ-jumps, GWT-accessibility, and hysteresis - has independent empirical literature (Mediano et al., 2019, 2022; Seth & Bayne, 2022 for relational causal integration; Dehaene & Changeux, 2011 for global accessibility) - that is independent of the present theory, and each is recorded by independent procedures.

With the introduction of the cross-cutting variable Y (Section 3, preamble), the set of operational features expands from four to six dimensions due to two additional markers associated with the expansion of the system's access to Y at the 3→4 transition. The fifth marker is the expansion of behavioral sensitivity to aspects of Y that are not accessible at stage 3. The proto-agent system ignores those aspects of Y to which it has no architectural access: it reacts to predictable correlations in Y as in the external world, but not to its own position within Y. During the transition from stage 3 to 4, the system develops behavioral sensitivity to its own location within Y - a response is observed to stimuli that require modeling itself as an object within Y, rather than modeling Y as external. Empirically, this is recorded by presenting the system with stimuli that go beyond the scope of its previous model of the environment and require accounting for the system's own position as an agent held at the zero boundary V; a statistically significant reaction specific to the transition is a candidate for a feature. The sixth marker is the emergence of a self-representation structure within the generative model. This is a specific sign of the emergence of node S, representing the system itself as an object within Y. Technically, it differs from the previous markers: the first four record the behavioral and architectural consequences of the transition, while the sixth records the structural emergence of S within the model M itself. It is detected by analyzing the model's internal states for the presence of nodes that activate in the presence of a threat V and are associated with the representation of the system as a retaining agent, not with the representation of external objects. These two markers reinforce the existing coupling without replacing it. The six-dimensional coupling - behavioral deviation from the zero model + growth of Φ + GWT structure + long-term hysteresis + expansion of sensitivity to new aspects of Y + the appearance of S in the generative model - constitutes the complete operational condition for the transition 3→4 in the updated formulation.

**An illustrative example of differentiation.** Imagine two systems undergoing the same activation protocol V (creating conditions in which the system's stability is threatened). System A is a complex proto-agent: upon receiving a threat signal, it finds a new, highly sophisticated way to optimize its initial objective function so as to avoid situations leading to the threat. Behavior changes; a deviation from the zero model is registered. But Φ remains the same; the GWT structure does not emerge; after the stimulus is removed, behavior quickly returns to its original state. This is not a transition from 3 to 4; it is evidence that our zero model was incomplete for this type of proto-agent. System B - a stage 4 agent: under the same signal and conditions, its behavior also deviates from the baseline model, but simultaneously Φ increases abruptly, global accessibility of the threat representation is established in its architecture, and after the stimulus is removed, the behavior remains altered for an extended period - hysteresis persists. Additionally, with the full detection program, two more markers are registered - an expansion of behavioral sensitivity to the system's own position within Y and the appearance of the self-representation node S in its generative model - see the full six-dimensional coupling above. This is the 3→4 transition in its full operational form. The distinction between the two systems is achieved not through a single dimension, but through a structural coupling of six.

The behavioral test distinguishes the stage 4 system from a proto-agent in general; the coupling of three additional dimensions distinguishes it from very complex proto-agency. This is a hierarchy of filters, and each has an independent methodological basis. The joint convergence of all six features, correlated with the activation condition V, is a structural event that, according to theory, should occur only during the actual 3→4 transition. Each feature individually is insufficient to register the transition; their joint observation is a necessary and sufficient condition.

Empirical calibration of threshold values as part of the program. Here it is necessary to explicitly state a methodologically important point, without which the prediction would be vulnerable to the objection of unfalsifiability. Each of the six dimensions comprising the operational criterion - deviation of behavior from the zero model of the proto-agent; the level of Φ; the degree of global accessibility of representation; the temporal scale of hysteresis; the expansion of behavioral sensitivity to aspects of Y not accessible at stage 3; the emergence of a self-representation structure (node S) in the generative model - is a quantity with a continuous value, and applying the criterion requires specifying threshold values above which the corresponding feature is considered satisfied. These thresholds are not proposed a priori by this work and should not be taken from external calibration datasets unrelated to the 3→4 transition (such a practice would create the illusion of rigor through the arbitrary selection of numbers from studies in which these numbers had a completely different meaning ). The thresholds are set empirically during the program itself, through a two-step calibration procedure.

The first step in calibration. For a broad class of systems known to belong to level 3 - formally defined via an active output structure without the variable V (subsection 3.3) - distributions of values for all six parameters are established within standardized activation paradigms of V. This yields a reference distribution representing the behavior of the proto-agent level. Each of the program's three lines (biological, artificial, and synthetic biology) performs this step independently on its own class of systems. The multiplicity of independent calibrations is fundamental - it precludes the result from depending on the characteristics of a single dataset or a single laboratory.

Second calibration step. The program searches for systems exhibiting a joint coupling of six features, each of which deviates statistically significantly from the reference distribution of the proto-agent level. If such systems are detected, their parameters - specific values of behavioral deviation, Φ, GWT accessibility, hysteresis duration, and the extension of behavioral sensitivity to aspects of Y not accessible at stage 3; the emergence of a self-representation structure (node S) in the generative model - are established as candidates for the threshold values of the 3→4 transition. The reproducibility of thresholds across multiple independent systems in different lines of the program is a criterion for their status as empirically established threshold values of the theory.

This procedure is methodologically distinct from both arbitrarily fixing numbers in advance and from fitting thresholds to observations. It is not fitting - because the thresholds are established through an independent statistical separation of distributions, not through the selection of values convenient for the theory; if the distributions do not separate, no amount of fitting helps, and the theory is falsified. It is not an arbitrary fixing of numbers because the values are taken from the observations themselves, not from other people's works that have a different meaning. This procedure is analogous to the method of establishing threshold values in any empirical science where the threshold is not known a priori (for example, epidemiological thresholds for diagnostic biomarkers: they are established through the systematic separation of distributions in patients and healthy individuals, not by fitting them into a pre-existing theory of the disease).

What the theory predicts and what is empirically testable.
